1401 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


County of Ventura v. Tillett


Civ. No. 63044


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


133 Cal. App. 3d 105; 183 Cal. Rptr. 741; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1700



June 28, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 22, 1982. Richardson, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. D 98169, Roland N. Purnell, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The order denying defendant's motion to vacate the judgment is reversed. The case is remanded to the trial court with directions: (1) to enter an order granting that motion; (2) to allow defendant, properly represented by counsel, to file an answer to the complaint and to have a trial on the issues thus presented. Since defendant's present economic status cannot be determined on the record before us, the trial court shall determine whether she is now entitled to appointed counsel as an indigent. Defendant shall recover her costs in this court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant mother appealed the order from the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which denied defendant's motion to set aside and vacate the judgment of contempt for nonpayment of child support to plaintiff County of Ventura.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff County of Ventura filed a complaint against defendant mother for reimbursement of welfare funds which the county had provided in support of defendant's two minor children. Defendant signed a "Judgment for Child Support and Reimbursement of Welfare Funds by Stipulation." After failing to pay the ordered child support, defendant pled guilty to four counts of contempt. Defendant filed a motion to set aside and vacate the original stipulated judgment on the ground that the judgment was unconstitutional in that defendant did not have representation of counsel in entering into the stipulation. The trial court denied the motion and defendant appealed. The reviewing court reversed, holding that the judgment was void because defendant did not voluntarily, knowingly, and intelligently waive her right to counsel and to trial. The court held that a stipulated judgment in a civil action under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11350 required a judicial determination of the validity of any constitutional waiver. The court held that the appeal was permissible pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 904.1(b). The court held that defendant was not guilty of laches and plaintiff claimed no prejudice.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order denying defendant County of Ventura's motion to vacate the judgment and remanded the case with instructions. The court held that the stipulated judgment was void because defendant mother was denied due process.

 

1402 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Bamboo Brothers v. Carpenter


Civ. No. 63380


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


133 Cal. App. 3d 116; 183 Cal. Rptr. 748; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1701



June 28, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 134868, Bruce W. Dodds, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sheriff and others sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California) after it issued a permanent injunction that enjoined enforcement of Ordinance No. 3194 of the County of Santa Barbara. Respondent retail store argued that the ordinance was in conflict with and preempted by state laws; was vague and overbroad; and violated respondent's First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I, rights.


OVERVIEW: Ordinance No. 3194 of the County of Santa Barbara (ordinance) regulated the use, possession, delivery, and manufacture of drug paraphernalia, as well as advertisements promoting the sale of drug paraphernalia. Respondent retail store argued that the ordinance was in conflict with and preempted by state laws; was vague and overbroad; and violated respondent's First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I, rights. The lower court ruled in favor of respondent and enjoined enforcement of the ordinance. Appellant sheriff and others sought review. The lower court judgment was reversed. The court held that there was no direct conflict between state law and the ordinance. The ordinance was not vague and overbroad. Commercial speech that promoted an illegal activity may be restricted without subjecting the restriction to scrutiny under a balancing test. Illegal conduct was not protected merely because it was in part initiated, evidenced, or carried out by language.


OUTCOME: The lower court judgment that enjoined enforcement of a county ordinance was reversed. The court held that there was no direct conflict between state law and the county ordinance at issue. The ordinance was not vague and overbroad. Commercial speech that promoted an illegal activity may be restricted without subjecting the restriction to scrutiny under a balancing test.

 

1403 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Manning


Crim. No. 4884


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


133 Cal. App. 3d 159; 183 Cal. Rptr. 727; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1705



June 28, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied July 19, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 249894-7, Joseph L. Joy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed. The matter is remanded for resentencing on the second rape count.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), which, following a jury trial, found defendant guilty of false imprisonment, battery with serious bodily injury, and two counts of rape. Prior to trial, defendant made a Cal. Penal Code § 995 motion to set aside the information, which was denied.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the trial court erred in denying his Cal. Penal Code § 995 motion to set aside the hybrid battery charge. Defendant argued that by waiting until after the preliminary hearing to charge the battery, a wobbler, the prosecution deprived appellant of a substantial right. Defendant also argued that the concurrent sentence for false imprisonment violated Cal. Penal Code § 654 because the imprisonment was part and parcel of the rapes and thus the court should have stayed the sentence. On appeal, the court rejected defendant's arguments. The court held that the amendment of the information after the preliminary hearing to add the hybrid battery charge as a felony was proper and that defendant was not denied a substantial right at the preliminary examination. The court also found that the false imprisonment was not incidental to the rapes. However, the court held that the trial court should have stated reasons for consecutively sentencing appellant for the second rape. Accordingly, the court affirmed the judgment of conviction but remanded the matter to the trial court for resentencing on the second rape count.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of conviction and remanded the matter for resentencing on the second rape count. The court held that the amendment of the information after the preliminary hearing to add the hybrid battery charge as a felony was proper and that defendant was not denied a substantial right at the preliminary examination.

 

1404 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Olson v. Cory


Civ. No. 63644


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


134 Cal. App. 3d 85; 184 Cal. Rptr. 325; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1838



June 25, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified July 22, 1982. Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 8, 1982. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County No. CA 000662, Dickran M. Tevrizian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court is corrected as set forth in paragraph 2, supra, by striking out the words "and California" in the two places where such words occur, and as modified herein the judgment is affirmed. Respondents shall be entitled to their costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state comptroller sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted plaintiff judges' motion for summary judgment and declared that Cal. Const. art. III, § 4, as amended in 1980, could not be applied to adjust the salary of active judges during their term of office, or adjust the retirement benefits of retired judges calculated on the basis of the salary of active judges.


OVERVIEW: Defendant state comptroller sought review of a judgment which granted plaintiff judges' motion for summary judgment and declared that Cal. Const. art. III, § 4, as amended in 1980, could not constitutionally be applied to adjust the salary of active judges during their term of office, or adjust the retirement benefits of retired judges calculated on the basis of the salary of active judges. The court first explained that although each member of the court was financially interested in the outcome, because there was no other judge or court available to hear and resolve the cause necessity required that the judges not be disqualified on the ground of conflict of interest. The court held that Cal. Const. art. III, § 4, as amended, was ineffective as applied to the "protected" class of judges because it was in derogation of the contract clause of the federal constitutional and in derogation of the due process clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV, because the protected class of judges possessed vested rights in their salaries and retirement benefits. Accordingly, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court because a state constitutional provision, as amended, which adjusted the salaries of judges, was ineffective as applied to plaintiff judges because it violated the federal contracts clause and the due process clause of the Fourteenth Amendment.

 

1405 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Manning v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 53203


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


132 Cal. App. 3d 825; 183 Cal. Rptr. 458; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1667



June 16, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 542319-8, Winton McKibben, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The purported appeal from the order denying plaintiff's motion to strike the district attorney's return is dismissed. The order denying the petition for writ of mandate/prohibition is reversed, and the trial court is directed to issue a writ of mandate compelling defendant municipal court to sustain the demurrer to the complaint which charges plaintiff with violating section 316.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of an order by the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), denying her peremptory writ of mandamus to compel respondent municipal court to sustain her demurrer, contending that Cal. Penal Code § 316, under which she was convicted of unlawfully keeping a house for the purpose of assignation or prostitution was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was charged for violating Cal. Penal Code § 316, willfully and unlawfully keeping a house used for the purpose of assignation or prostitution, letting rooms, apartments, or tenements, with knowledge of their unlawful use. Appellee municipal court overruled appellant's demurrer in which she contended that § 316 was unconstitutional. The superior court denied appellant's writ of mandamus to compel the municipal court to sustain the demurrer. The court reversed the order denying appellants' petition for writ of mandate and directed the superior court to issue a writ of mandate compelling appellee municipal court to sustain the demurrer. The court found that the word "assignation," as used in § 316, was too vague to give notice to appellee of the conduct to be avoided and allowed the police of unfettered discretion to determine what conduct would violate the statute. The court concluded that § 316 violated U.S. Const. amend. I because it was overbroad, void for vagueness, and violated due process rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order denying appellant's petition for writ of mandate in the state's action against her for unlawfully keeping a house for the purpose of assignation or prostitution. The superior court was directed to issue a writ of mandate compelling appellee municipal court to sustain appellant's demurrer because the statute under which appellant was convicted was unconstitutionally overbroad and void for vagueness.

 

1406 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Committee to Defend Reproductive Rights v. Cory


Civ. No. 53393


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


132 Cal. App. 3d 852; 183 Cal. Rptr. 475; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1670



June 16, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] A peremptory writ shall issue, directing respondents to refrain from enforcing the unconstitutional restrictions in the Budget Act of 1981 challenged herein. Petitioners are entitled to recover their costs, including reasonable attorneys' fees pursuant to Code of Civil Procedure section 1021.5. The trial court judge before whom the cost award in Committee to Defend Reproductive Rights v. Myers, supra, 29 Cal.3d 252 [172 Cal.Rptr. 866, 625 P.2d 779] is pending is appointed as referee to fix an appropriate cost award, including attorneys' fees.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner abortion groups' original petition for a writ of mandate filed with the California Supreme Court was remanded, and petitioners sought to compel respondent governmental officials to refrain from enforcing 1981 Cal. Stat. ch. 99, § 2, items 426-101-001 through 426-101-890, provision 27, of the 1981 Budget Act, which limited funding for abortions under the Medi-Cal Act, Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 14000 et seq.


OVERVIEW: The legislature attempted to restrict abortion funding by specifying that none of the funds appropriated for the Medi-Cal Act (Act), Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 14000 et seq., could be used to pay for abortions except in enumerated limited circumstances under 1981 Cal. Stat. ch. 99, § 2, items 426-101-001 through 426-101-890, provision 27. The appellate court issued a peremptory writ that directed respondent governmental officials to refrain from enforcing the restriction on funding abortions. The appellate court relied upon the two prior supreme court decisions, which held the identical restrictions unconstitutional in observing that once California decided to provide medical benefits to the poor, it could not selectively withdraw part of that care because a women exercised her constitutional right to choose to have an abortion. The separation of powers doctrine did not preclude the judiciary from ordering funds already appropriated for general health services under the Act, within which abortions fell, to be paid without regard to the invalid legislative restriction. Petitioner abortion groups were entitled to attorney fees under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5.


OUTCOME: The court issued a peremptory writ of mandate, as sought by petitioner abortion groups, which directed respondent governmental officials to refrain from enforcing the restrictions on funding of abortions under the Medi-Cal Act within the 1981 Budget Act because those restrictions were unconstitutional. The writ, which directed that already appropriated funds be used to pay for abortions, did not violate the separation of powers.

 

1407 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Anton v. San Antonio Community Hospital


Civ. No. 25726


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


132 Cal. App. 3d 638; 183 Cal. Rptr. 423; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1649



June 7, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified June 24, 1982. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 15, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. 161711, Duke D. Rouse, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff sought review of a judgment rendered by the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California) that denied his petition for a writ of mandate to compel defendant to reinstate him to membership in defendant's medical staff.


OVERVIEW: The California Supreme Court held that plaintiff's application for reappointment to defendant's medical staff involved a vested and fundamental right and that pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5(c), the trial court erred in not exercising its independent judgment in reviewing the factual basis for the decision of defendant's board of directors. The judgment was reversed and the cause remanded. After remand and before trial, the legislature amended § 1094.5 to make the standard of review one of substantial evidence. The trial court denied plaintiff's peremptory writ seeking reinstatement finding that substantial evidence supported defendant's decision not to reinstate. Plaintiff sought review. Plaintiff contended that because a fundamental vested right was involved, due process required a judicial determination of the facts. Plaintiff argued that the Supreme Court's decision was the law of the case on the proper standard of review. The court disagreed and affirmed the judgment. The doctrine of law of the case could not be applied because a change in law intervened between the earlier decision and trial.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that denied plaintiff's petition for a writ of mandate to compel defendant to reinstate him to membership in defendant's medical staff because the substantial evidence standard of review for administrative hearings was not a violation of the due process clause.

Stockton Metropolitan Transit Dist. v. Amalgamated Transit Union


Civ. No. 19766


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


132 Cal. App. 3d 203; 183 Cal. Rptr. 24; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1609



May 25, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 24, 1982, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 4, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. 146157, William Biddick, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff district sought review of an order of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California) that entered judgment, compelling plaintiff to arbitrate a controversy arising between it and defendant union over proposed changes in plaintiff's retirement plan for its workers.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff district acquired a private bus company through eminent domain. The employees of the private company were absorbed into plaintiff. Thereafter, defendant union was certified as the employees' collective bargaining representative. From time to time, plaintiff sought and received financial assistance from the federal government under the Urban Mass Transportation Act of 1964. In order to obtain such assistance plaintiff entered into an arbitration agreement with defendant. After plaintiff suggested changes in a retirement plan, defendant demanded arbitration of the dispute. In response, plaintiff filed a complaint for declaratory relief, an injunction, a temporary restraining order, and a preliminary injunction, asserting it could not be compelled to submit to arbitration. Defendant filed a cross-complaint to compel arbitration. The trial court denied a request for a preliminary injunction to prevent arbitration, granted the motion to compel arbitration, and stayed the issues of the complaint pending arbitration. Plaintiff sought review, and the court affirmed, because plaintiff had the authority to enter into the arbitration agreement as a condition of federal assistance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of judgment that required plaintiff district to submit to arbitration, because there was clear statutory authority for plaintiff to enter into the arbitration agreement required by the federal government as a condition to the receipt of federal assistance.

 

1410 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Norman v. Unemployment Ins. Appeals Bd.


Civ. No. 52713.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION THREE


131 Cal. App. 3d 946; 182 Cal. Rptr. 708; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1469



May 20, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted July 22, 1982 (See 34 C.3d 1).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 459961, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board challenged an order of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which granted a peremptory writ of mandate to set aside a decision of appellant that denied unemployment compensation benefits to respondent claimant because she voluntarily quit her job to join her fiance out of state.


OVERVIEW: Appellant Unemployment Insurance Appeals Board challenged a trial court order granting a peremptory writ of mandate to set aside a decision that denied unemployment compensation benefits to respondent claimant. Appellant contended that good cause for voluntarily leaving employment pursuant to Cal. Unemp. Ins. Code § 1256 did not exist where respondent quit her job to join her fiance out of state. Appellant contended that, absent an existing or imminent marital relationship, there was no authority for holding good cause existed for leaving employment to join a boyfriend, fiance, or cohabitee. Respondent argued that to deny her benefits because she quit her job to move to another state with her fiance discriminated against her solely because of the relationship. The appellate court agreed and affirmed the peremptory writ of mandate because allowing respondent to receive benefits was not in derogation of the public policy to foster and promote the institution of marriage. The court held that the morals of society had changed so radically in regard to cohabitation that courts could not impose a standard based on moral considerations that had been so widely abandoned.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order granting a peremptory writ of mandate because it concluded that respondent had left her job with good cause. The court held that allowing a fiance to receive unemployment compensation benefits was not in derogation of the well-established public policy to foster and promote the institution of marriage.

 

1411 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Hamilton v. County of L.A.


Civ. No. 60585


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


131 Cal. App. 3d 982; 182 Cal. Rptr. 868; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1472



May 20, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 22, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SOC 44692, D. Sterry Fagan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Since the trial court declined to make any findings as to liability or damages, it is necessary to remand for a determination of those issues. The judgment is reversed, and the matter is remanded for proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) in favor of respondent county in appellant's action under the Jones Act, 46 U.S.C.S. § 688, and the general maritime law, after he was injured while employed by respondent as a lifeguard and rescue boat lieutenant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, who was employed by respondent county as a rescue boat lieutenant, was permanently disabled after suffering from caisson disease, or the bends, after a dive during which he relied on an inaccurate depth gauge supplied by respondent. He filed suit under the Jones Act, 46 U.S.C.S. § 688, and the general maritime law. The trial court found that appellant was a seaman, but that his only remedy was under the workers' compensation laws, and that the exclusive remedy of Cal. Lab. Code § 3601 created no material prejudice to the general maritime law or the Jones Act and did not interfere with the uniformity and harmony of that body of law. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment, holding that because appellant's employment was of a distinctly maritime nature, application of the exclusive remedy provision would materially prejudice the Jones Act and the general maritime law. The court further held that because respondent's activities were not of primarily local concern, as they affected interstate commerce and navigation, both the commerce power and the admiralty power supported application of the Jones Act and maritime law to respondent as appellant's employer.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment, holding that because appellant's employment was of a distinctly maritime nature, application of the exclusive remedy provisions of the workers' compensation law would materially prejudice the Jones Act and the general maritime law, and that the impact of respondent county's rescue and other activities on interstate navigation supported the application of maritime law under the commerce and admiralty powers.

 

1412 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Laguna Publ'g Co. v. Golden Rain Found.


Civ. No. 20650


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


131 Cal. App. 3d 816; 182 Cal. Rptr. 813; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1461; 1982 Trade Cas. (CCH) P65,125



May 18, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 16, 1982, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 18, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 207112, Walter W. Charamza, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Item No. 5 deemed to be without substantial controversy is stricken, there being absolutely no evidence in the record to support such a determination. Insofar as the judgment denied plaintiff's application for an injunction to terminate its exclusion from Leisure World, the judgment is reversed with directions. The trial court is directed to enter a new and different judgment granting such application on terms and conditions substantially as follows: For so long as Golden Rain or any other entity, exercising a power of control over the right of entry into Leisure World, authorizes or suffers the unsolicited, live carrier delivery of any give-away type newspaper, including the Leisure World News, to any residence in Leisure World where any occupant thereof has not personally requested or subscribed to such delivery, the plaintiff shall be entitled to enter Leisure World for the purpose of delivering its newspaper, unsolicited, to any such residence in Leisure [***2] World, provided nevertheless that such delivery shall be under the same rules and regulations as to time, place, and manner as apply to the delivery of e.g., the Los Angeles Times and other newspapers offered for sale to subscribers, and provided further that if any resident of Leisure World shall expressly state in writing to Golden Rain or to the management of Leisure World that he or she does not wish to receive unsolicited delivery of the Laguna News-Post to his or her residence, then plaintiff shall refrain thereafter from any delivery to that resident. In this latter instance, plaintiff shall be entitled to verify independently by telephone call or personal visit that any given resident does not wish to receive unsolicited delivery of the Laguna News-Post.

Because we have decided that plaintiff's exclusion from Leisure World was unconstitutional discrimination and therefore wrongful as a matter of law, the trial court is further directed, upon due application of plaintiff, to try, with a jury if requested, those issues of damages arising from the illegality of the exclusion of the Laguna News-Post from Leisure World, namely: (1) whether plaintiff suffered any damages caused [***3] by its illegal exclusion from Leisure World as measured by sections 1708 and 3333 of the Civil Code; (2) whether there was any concerted action or agreement between Golden Rain and Golden West, per section 16720, subdivision (a) of the Business and Professions Code, which caused the unconstitutional exclusion of the Laguna News-Post from Leisure World such as to constitute an unreasonable restraint of trade; and (3) whether there were any actual damages proximately resulting from any such unreasonable restraint of trade over the four years next preceding the filing of the action for assessment per section 16750.1 of the Business and Professions Code.

Except as reversed with directions above, the judgment is affirmed, and each party shall bear its own costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff publisher and defendant foundation sought a second rehearing of an appeal in the Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two. In its first rehearing, the appellate court upheld its decision in the original appeal that plaintiff was entitled to an injunction and a limited new trial on issues under the Cartwright Act. Plaintiff's action challenged defendant's exclusion of a newspaper from a walled community.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff sought to make unsolicited, live-carrier delivery of its giveaway newspaper to residents of a walled community. Defendant excluded plaintiff's newspaper from the community but allowed delivery of a similar unsolicited newspaper. Plaintiff sued defendant on constitutional and unfair trade grounds. The trial jury resolved the issues raised in the complaint against plaintiff. In the original appeal, the court reversed as to the constitutional claim and permitted a trial on damages. In the second rehearing of that appeal, the court decided that plaintiff's free speech, free-press rights secured under Cal. Const. art. I § 2 were violated when defendant discriminatorily excluded plaintiff's unsolicited live-carrier delivery of its newspaper from the walled community. The court held that plaintiff was allowed to enter and make deliveries except to residents who expressed that they did not wish to receive the newspaper. The jury trial of issues arising under the Cartwright Act and the Unfair Trade Practices Act proceeded without recognition of the deprivation of plaintiff's constitutional rights. Thus, the trial court was directed to determine those issues and issues of damages.


OUTCOME: Except as it reversed on constitutional grounds, the court affirmed the judgment of the superior court. Defendant foundation violated plaintiff publisher's California free-speech, free-press rights when it excluded live-carrier delivery of plaintiff's giveaway newspaper from the walled community. The trial court was directed to try, upon due application of plaintiff, issues of damages arising from the illegal exclusion.

 

1413 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


County of Ventura v. Tillett


Civ. No. 63044.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


132 Cal. App. 3d 767; 183 Cal. Rptr. 741; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1661



May 17, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted June 14, 1982 (See 133 Cal. App. 3d 105)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. D 98169, Roland N. Purnell, Temporary Judge. * 


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order from the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which denied appellant's motion to set aside and vacate a stipulated judgment in an action by respondent county government seeking reimbursement of welfare funds that respondent provided for the support of two of appellant's minor children.


OVERVIEW: Appellant challenged a trial court order that denied her motion to set aside a stipulated judgment in favor of respondent county government in an action for reimbursement of welfare funds respondent provided for appellant's minor children. . Appellant contended that she did not have the assistance of counsel in negotiating the stipulated judgment, and there was no indication that she waived any offer of free counsel. Appellant, now represented by counsel, contended that she signed the document even though she lacked the ability to make support payments because she was afraid that if she did not sign she would go to jail and lose her insurance license that she had just obtained. The appellate court reversed the order denying appellant's motion to set aside the judgment because as an indigent defendant in a prosecution for child support appellant was constitutionally entitled to appointment of free counsel pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11350. The court held that the constitutional requirement of free counsel would become a nullity if respondent was allowed to circumvent the requirement by executing stipulated judgments.


OUTCOME: The order denying appellant's motion to set aside the stipulated judgment was reversed, and the case was remanded with directions to the lower court to allow appellant, properly represented by counsel, to file an answer to the complaint and have a trial on the issues. The court held that such a stipulated judgment, like a confession of judgment, was constitutional only if it constituted a valid waiver of appellant's due process rights.

 

1414 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


County of Los Angeles v. State of California


Civ. No. 62911


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


132 Cal. App. 3d 761; 183 Cal. Rptr. 5; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1660



May 17, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified June 14, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C342764, Thomas T. Johnson, Judge.



DISPOSITION:  The judgment is reversed with directions: (a) to dismiss the State of California as a party; and (2) to issue a writ of mandate to the board, requiring it to vacate its decision in this matter and to approve the claim, subject to the board's determination under section 2254 as to the dollar amount of the claim.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county challenged a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) which denied its petition for a writ of mandate to compel reimbursement to it for funds expended under Cal. Penal Code § 1026.5.


OVERVIEW: The legislature enacted Cal. Penal Code § 1026.5, limiting the maximum confinement of persons committed as criminally insane to the maximum term for the underlying offense and providing, for persons whose underlying offense was of a violent nature, a procedure for extension of confinement patterned after the mentally disordered sex offender act (MDSO) provision. Appellant county filed a petition for writ of mandate to compel reimbursement to it from respondent state for funds expended under § 1026.5. The writ was denied, and appellant challenged the decision. Appellant argued that it was entitled to reimbursement based on Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 2207, which provided for reimbursement for costs incurred as a result of any law enacted after January 1, 1973, that mandated a new program or an increased level of service of an existing program. Respondent contended that § 1026.5 did not increase any cost to the county. The reviewing court held that § 1026.5 set forth an entirely new program for dealing with violent persons found guilty by reason of insanity. Hence, the judgment was reversed, and appellant's writ of mandate was issued.


OUTCOME: The court held that the statute in question set forth an entirely new program for dealing with violent persons found guilty by reason of insanity. Hence, appellant county was entitled to bring a claim for reimbursement for costs incurred because the statute increased the level of service of an existing program. The judgment denying appellant's writ of mandate was reversed.

 

1415 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Georgia-Pacific Corp. v. California Coastal Com.


Civ. No. 51055


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


132 Cal. App. 3d 678; 183 Cal. Rptr. 395; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1653



May 12, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified June 11, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 744590, Eugene F. Lynch, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to enter a new and different judgment, and to conduct further proceedings, consistent with this opinion. Respondent and appellant Georgia-Pacific Corporation shall recover its costs on both appeals taken herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state coastal commission appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which issued a peremptory writ of mandamus and ordered defendant to issue new development permits to plaintiff corporation without public access conditions.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff corporation operated a lumber processing facility along the coastline. As required by the Coastal Act, Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 30000 et seq., appellant applied to defendant state coastal commission for permits to construct facilities on its property. Defendant granted the permits on the condition that plaintiff dedicate easements for public access to the shoreline. On appeal, defendant contended that the trial court judgment was based on an erroneous interpretation of the term "new development" as used in Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 30212. Lacking a statutory definition of the words "new" or "project" for purposes of the Coastal Act, the court resorted to the usual and ordinary meaning. Thus, the court held that plaintiff did not have a right to construct new development in the coastal zone without a permit. Defendant could constitutionally require a dedication of property in the interests of the general welfare as a condition of permitting land development. The public access easements were reasonably related to a principal objective of the Coastal Act in Cal. Pub. Res. Code § 30001.5(c) to provide for maximum access to the coast by the public.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court that ordered defendant state coastal commission to issue permits to plaintiff corporation without a conditional public access easement. The court held that plaintiff did not have a right to construct new development without a permit and that the public access easements were reasonably related to the objectives of the Coastal Act.

 

1416 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bennett


Crim. No. 39139


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


131 Cal. App. 3d 488; 182 Cal. Rptr. 473; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1579



May 6, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 22, 1982. Newman, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 071694, Bonnie Lee Martin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which recommitted appellant to a mental hospital after being found not guilty by reason of insanity of assault with a deadly weapon.


OVERVIEW: Appellant shot a friend while under the delusion that the friend was stealing appellant's mind and body and causing him to have pains in his heart. Appellant was found to have committed assault with a deadly weapon and to be not guilty thereof by reason of insanity; he was thereafter committed to a mental institution. After many years, the prosecution filed a petition for extended commitment pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1026.5(b) and the trial court ordered appellant recommitted. Appellant sought review, alleging that the trial court erred in ruling that a finding of amenability to treatment was not required under § 1026.5(b) and that such an extension of commitment violated Cal. Const. art. I, § 7, and U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court affirmed, holding that § 1026.5(b) did not require a finding of amenability to treatment, and that the omission of this requirement was a valid exercise of the legislature's police power and did not impinge upon appellant's constitutional rights. In addition, the court held that the testimony of the mental health professionals was properly admitted to show appellant's dangerousness as required by § 1026.5(b).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order for appellant's recommitment to the mental institution because a finding of amenability was not required for an extension of a commitment for insanity, appellant's constitutional rights were not infringed upon, and the mental health professional's expert opinion testimony on appellant's dangerousness was properly admitted.

 

1417 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


County of Alameda v. State Bd. of Equalization


Civ. No. 20642


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


131 Cal. App. 3d 374; 182 Cal. Rptr. 450; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1565



April 30, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 26, 1982, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 28, 1982. Broussard, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 290504, Fred W. Marler, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff county appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), granting defendant State Board of Equalization's motion for summary judgment and denying plaintiff's cross motion for summary judgment on plaintiff's action for declaratory relief under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 538 to test the validity of the representative period for assessing nonscheduled aircraft.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff county filed an action for declaratory relief to test the validity of the representative period designated by defendant State Board of Equalization for assessing nonscheduled aircraft for the 1980-1981 assessment year. The trial court granted defendant's motion for summary judgment. On appeal, plaintiff argued defendant did not have statutory authority to modify the designated representative period; defendant's choice of a representative period constituted an abuse of discretion; and the modification unconstitutionally exempted property from ad valorem taxation. The court concluded that the legislature's intent to tax aircraft only to the extent they were normally present within the state reasonably implied a power to substitute periods of atypical and abnormal aircraft activity with equivalent periods of normal aircraft activity. The court held defendant did not abuse its discretion in using the time periods because the method was a reasonable attempt to pick a time period that is likely to reflect the average activity for the ensuing tax year. The court found defendant committed no unconstitutional exemption of property from taxation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the granting of defendant State Board of Equalization's motion for summary judgment because defendant had statutory authority to modify the designated representative period, defendant's choice of a representative period was not an abuse of discretion, and there was no unconstitutional exemption of property from taxation.

 

1418 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Karsai


Crim. No. 10917


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


131 Cal. App. 3d 224; 182 Cal. Rptr. 406; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1551



April 28, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 26, 1982, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 14, 1982. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Placer County, No. 56481, Keith F. Sparks, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment from the Superior Court of Placer County (California), which convicted defendant of false imprisonment with force, Cal. Penal Code § 236, oral copulation by force, Cal. Penal Code § 288a(c), and rape by force or violence, Cal. Penal Code § 261(2)-(3).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of false imprisonment with force, Cal. Penal Code § 236, oral copulation by force, Cal. Penal Code § 288a(c), and rape by force or violence, Cal. Penal Code § 261(2)-(3). The court affirmed the judgment. The court held that vaginal penetration was not necessary in order to constitute the crime of rape. Any penetration of the female genitalia by the penis, however slight, was sufficient and the evidence supported the jury's finding that defendant made actual sexual penetration and was guilty of rape. The court rejected defendant's contention that the California sentencing scheme for violent sexual offenses constituted cruel and unusual punishment in view of the outrageous nature of violent sexual offenses and the manifest danger to society from recidivism and multiplicity of offenses. The court concluded that the California sentencing scheme for violent sexual offenses did not violate equal protection of the law because violent sex offenders were not similarly situated with other offenders. The court determined that a flight after crime instruction was properly given because flight was evidence that defendant had a consciousness of guilt.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment against defendant. The evidence supported the jury's finding that defendant made actual sexual penetration and was guilty of rape. The California sentencing scheme for violent sexual offenses did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment or violate equal protection of the law.

 

1419 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Fridley v. Eu


Civ. No. 52537


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


131 Cal. App. 3d 100; 182 Cal. Rptr. 232; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1540



April 26, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 4, 1982. Mosk, J., Newman, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 457889, John R. Kennedy, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant election candidate challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which denied his petition for a peremptory writ of mandate to compel respondent election officials to place his name on the ballot.


OVERVIEW: Appellant election candidate missed the deadline for filing nomination papers and sought to have his name placed on the ballot as a write-in candidate of the Libertarian Party. He was unable to obtain one percent of the votes cast for the office in the last preceding election as required by statute to have his name placed on the ballot as a write-in candidate. He claimed it was impossible for him to meet the requirement because there were less registered Libertarians residing within the district than the one percent of votes he was required to obtain. The lower court denied him a writ of mandate to order respondent election officials to place his name on the ballot and the court affirmed. It held that the subject requirement did not have an appreciable impact on the right to vote because appellant had access to the ballot by timely filing nomination papers or encouraging more voters to register with his party during his campaign. It held that the requirement was rationally related to the legitimate state goal of avoiding confusion and deception of the democratic process at a general election as well as the fostering of the efficient operation of the primary system.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of writ of mandate compelling respondent election officials to place appellant election candidate's name on the ballot as a write-in candidate because the write-in requirements did not have an appreciable impact on the right to vote since appellant had other means of access to the ballot.

 

1420 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Funk


Crim. No. 22392.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FIRST APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


130 Cal. App. 3d 1043; 182 Cal. Rptr. 103; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1453



April 23, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. June 16, 1982, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 24097, Richard P. Calhoun, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution appealed a decision from the Superior Court, Contra Costa County (California) dismissing a charge against defendant that he had sold a narcotic in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11352, following the trial court's finding of his guilt. Defendant contended that the order from which prosecution appealed was a nonappealable order. The prosecution contended there was no discriminatory prosecution in the matter.


OVERVIEW: The prosecution appealed an order dismissing a charge that defendant had sold a narcotic in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11352, following the trial court's affirmative finding of his guilt. Defendant argued that the order from which the prosecution appealed was a nonappealable order. The trial court held that there was a discriminatory prosecution. On appeal, the court held that whether an order was appealable depended on the effect of the ruling sought to be reviewed; it was to be determined not by form but by substance. The court held that the order was in legal effect an order arresting judgment, from which an appeal was expressly permitted by Cal. Penal Code § 1238(a)(4). The appellate court held that there were no authorities to indicate that the court could arbitrarily dismiss the action. The judgment was reversed.


OUTCOME: The order in arrest of judgment and the ensuing judgment of dismissal were reversed in an appeal by the prosecution following dismissal of a charge that defendant had sold a narcotic. The court concluded that the dismissal was in effect an order in arrest of judgment and therefore appeal by the prosecution was proper. Further, there was no equal protection violation.

 

1421 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Salzman


Crim. No. 11429


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


131 Cal. App. 3d 676; 182 Cal. Rptr. 748; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1600



April 21, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Siskiyou County, No. 33181, James E. Kleaver, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying the People's motion to reinstate the complaint under section 871.5 is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Siskiyou County (California), which denied its motion to reinstate a complaint under Cal. Penal Code § 871.5 after defendant's motion to suppress evidence was granted.


OVERVIEW: The people challenged the denial of its motion, by the superior court, to reinstate a complaint under Cal. Penal Code § 871.5, which was dismissed by a magistrate after having granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence. Even though Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5 made no provisions for the people to have appealed the grant of a motion to suppress evidence by a magistrate in a felony case, the appeal of a dismissal under § 871.5 was proper. The court held that appellant was entitled to a superior court review of the magistrate's dismissal order and entitled to appeal the superior court's denial of appellant's motion to reinstate the complaint. The court held that the motion to suppress was proper because there was a warrantless helicopter overflight of defendant's property, and the intrusion could not be justified by the doctrine of exigent circumstances. The observations were made from a position to which the police officers were not expressly or implicitly invited. The court affirmed the order, which denied appellant's motion to reinstate the complaint.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order, which denied the people's motion to reinstate a felony complaint, because defendant's motion to suppress evidence was supported by the evidence when police officers made observations from a position from which they were not expressly or impliedly invited.

 

1422 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Moran v. Harris


Civ. No. 22798.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


130 Cal. App. 3d 872; 182 Cal. Rptr. 519; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1440



April 20, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Reprinted as mod. on den. rehg. 131 Cal. App. 3d 913.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 369502, Donald R. Work, Judge. * 


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff attorney appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) in favor of defendant attorney in plaintiff's action on a referral fee contract. Plaintiff argued that public policy, as reflected in former Rule of Professional Conduct 22, did not bar fee sharing arrangements.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff attorney referred a case to a medical malpractice specialist. The two agreed to divide any legal fees received. After this agreement was made, the Rules of Professional Conduct, rule 2-108 was enacted, which prohibited such agreements. The specialist was unable to handle the case, and defendant attorney agreed to take the case and honor the fee arrangement. Defendant subsequently had the agreement voided on public policy grounds. The court reversed the judgment, however, finding that when made, the bargain was not void. In determining whether the subject of the given contract violated public policy, the court looked to the state of the law as it existed at the time the contract was made. The relevant time was not when defendant succeeded to the rights and obligations of the contract, but related back to its making. The court also noted that the prohibition against such agreements was lifted within just a few years after it was declared. As fee splitting arrangements continued to abound with the apparent approval of the legislature and the state bar, justice was better served by having defendant bound by his word than by depriving plaintiff of his part of the gross fee.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment with instructions to enter judgment in favor of plaintiff. The fee sharing contract was not void at the time it was made, which was the relevant time in determining whether the contract was void as against public policy.

 

1423 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Moran v. Harris


Civ. No. 22798


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


131 Cal. App. 3d 913; 182 Cal. Rptr. 519; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1467; 28 A.L.R.4th 655



April 20, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 13, 1982, and the petition of appellant Harris for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 16, 1982. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 369502, Donald R. Work, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed with instructions to enter judgment for plaintiff against defendants Wesley H. Harris and Wesley H. Harris, a professional corporation, 6 in the sum of $ 88,952.59. 7



6 In his petition for rehearing, Harris asserts that a reversal with directions to enter judgment against him is inappropriate since he pleaded an additional affirmative defense which the trial court did not rule upon. His third affirmative defense alleged: "[Any] performance due and owing to the plaintiff by this defendant, predicated upon the contract alleged in plaintiff's complaint, was and is excused by operation of law in that subsequent to the alleged date of the alleged agreement, the conduct which forms the basis of the alleged agreement was declared unlawful."

In essence, Harris explains, his claim is that rule 2-108 as currently drafted operates as a supervening prohibition to excuse nonperformance. (See Rest.2d Contracts, § 264; Calamari & Perillo, Contracts (2d ed. 1977) § 13-4, p. 486.)

The problem with Harris' argument is that application of the doctrine of supervening prohibition presumes a legal conclusion that performance of the contract would be contrary to the newly enacted statute or regulation. As we have already noted, the general rule is that a subsequent change in the law will not render unenforceable a previously created contractual right. (See ante, p. 918.) A well-established corollary to the rule states that, "a statute is presumed to be prospective only in operation and will not be retroactively applied unless such intention clearly appears from the language of the statute itself. [Citations.]" ( Whitmire v. H. K. Ferguson Co., supra, 261 Cal.App.2d at p. 602.) Both this court and the Altschul court (see 83 Cal.App.3d 153, 163) accepted the proposition that rule 22 did not operate retroactively to invalidate contracts entered into before November 1972. A similar conclusion must also hold true with respect to the current draft of rule 2-108. (See ante, fn. 2.) Thus, the legal premise for application of Harris' third affirmative defense is simply not present: Performance of the contract has not been made impossible by the enactment of rule 2-108. Consequently, there is no need to remand to the trial court for any additional factual inquiry.


7 The sum due Moran was computed as follows:

$  9,800.00

 

(3) Costs

$ 14,291.19

 

 

 

 

Total:

$ 49,091.19

 

 

 

 

Net attorney's fees

 

$ 177,905.18

 

 

 

$ 177,905.18/2=

 

$  88,952.59

We believe the trial court erred in not allowing the Von Kalinowski and medi-legal services as legitimate charges against the gross fees. Because of Harris' successful efforts under all of the circumstances of this case, he should not bear the total burden for these expenses.

 [***2]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant attorney challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which ruled that the referral fee agreement entered into between appellant and respondent attorney was unenforceable.


OVERVIEW: Appellant attorney filed action for damages, alleging that respondent attorney failed to honor the parties referral fee contract. The trial court held the agreement unenforceable, but awarded appellant the reasonable value for his services actually rendered in the underlying medical malpractice case. While appeal was pending Cal. Bar R., Prof. Conduct R. 2-108 was amended to remove the prohibition against referral fee contracts. The court reversed and held that rule 2-108 indicated not only that referral fee agreements were no longer contrary to public policy prospectively from its enactment, but also that they were not contrary to public policy at the time the original contract at issue was entered into. The court noted the fact that the Legislature, the State Bar, and the California Supreme Court before 1972 did not see fit to prohibit referral fee agreements was significant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and held that public policy did not prohibit the enforcement of the parties' referral fee agreement.

American River Flood Control Dist. v. Sayre


Civ. Nos. 20959, 20966.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,THIRD Dist.


130 Cal. App. 3d 707; 182 Cal. Rptr. 38; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1425



April 15, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehearing granted May 12, 1982 (See 136 C.A.3d 347)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner district sought writs of mandate that were consolidated for disposition from a trial court (California) and arose out of respondent county's refusal to collect ad valorem special assessments to operate and maintain water projects in a river zone behalf of petitioners in accordance with California law. Respondent had ceased to levy the special assessments for petitioner after previously doing so.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner district had sought for writs of mandate against respondent county when it did not levy and collect a special assessment used for water projects as required by Cal. Water Code §§ 12878.25 to 12878.39 and the American River Flood Control District Act, 1927 Cal. Stat. 808. Respondent contended it did not collect the assessment because the assessment violated Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 1(a) requiring voter approval of collections of special assessment, thus was void. Petitioner needed the special assessment monies as its reserves for maintenance were nearly exhausted. The court noted a special assessment was imposed for the public benefit, but in reality it was a compulsory charge to recoup the cost of a public improvement. The court held that respondent had to collect the requested assessments because the plain language of the statutes required them to do so, and that the California Constitution did not change the county's obligation to collect the special assessment. The court granted the petitions for writ of mandate requested by petitioner district and ordered the respondent to collect the special assessment as required by statute on an ad valorem roll.


OUTCOME: The court granted the petitions for writ of mandate requested by petitioner district and ordered respondent county to collect the special assessment as required by statute on an ad valorem roll. A writ of mandate was to issue directing the board of supervisors of respondent county to levy and collect the special assessments sought by the petitioners and to collect these special assessments based on an ad valorem roll pursuant to statutes.

Pating v. Board of Medical Quality Assurance


Civ. No. 61947


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


130 Cal. App. 3d 608; 182 Cal. Rptr. 20; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1414



April 13, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 11, 1982, and the petition of plaintiff and appellant for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 9, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 312625, Vernon G. Foster, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed insofar as it holds the hospital records admissible. It is reversed insofar as it holds the office medical records of the named patients inadmissible in evidence with instructions to the trial court to enter judgment in accord with the opinions expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant doctor sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), which granted his petition for writ of mandate insofar as the trial court found admissible hospital records pertaining to certain patients. Respondents, division of medical quality and board of medical quality assurance, cross-appealed the trial court's ruling that the office medical charts were not admissible at respondents' hearing.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, division of medical quality and board of medical quality assurance, charged appellant doctor with acts involving dishonesty and falsification of medical records. The administrative law judge denied appellant's motion to exclude medical records, and appellant filed a petition for writ of mandate. The trial court issued a writ of mandate finding hospital charts admissible and office charts inadmissible. Appellant sought review of the judgment regarding the admissibility of hospital records, and respondents cross-appealed the judgment regarding the inadmissibility of office medical charts. The court affirmed the judgment as to the admissibility of the hospital records and reversed as to the inadmissibility of the office medical records. The court held that the trial court gave careful consideration to the evidence and correctly concluded that a case which held that administrative agencies were not exempt from supporting their subpoenas with adequate declarations was not to be given retroactive application. The court further held that it found insufficient grounds for extending the exclusionary rule from criminal cases to the administrative investigation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment insofar as it held the hospital records admissible and reversed the judgment insofar as it held the office medical records inadmissible in evidence, with instructions to the trial court to enter judgment in accord with its opinions. In balancing the interests and various concerns involved, the court concluded that the scales were weighted heavily against the application of the exclusionary rule in the case.

People v. Nevarez


Crim. No. 11335


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


130 Cal. App. 3d 388; 181 Cal. Rptr. 691; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1524



March 31, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied April 29, 1982, and the petition of respondent Acosta for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 27, 1982. Reynoso, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. 31674, John F. Cruikshank, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The orders of dismissal are reversed as to each defendant.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants were charged with the crime of murder under Cal. Penal Code § 187 by information filed on December 1, 1980. The People appealed an order from the Superior Court of San Joaquin County, California, that dismissed the actions against each defendant, on the basis that the amendment to Cal. Penal Code § 1387 could not apply to dismissal prior to its effective date.


OVERVIEW: Defendants were charged with the crime of murder ( Cal. Penal Code, § 187), by information filed on December 1, 1980. After the effective date of the amendment to Cal. Penal Code § 1387 one month later, defendants moved to dismiss pursuant to that section, asserting that a prior dismissal of the charges under Cal. Penal Code § 995 operated as a bar to further prosecution. The trial court agreed and dismissed the actions against each defendant. The People appealed, contending that the amendment to §1387 could not apply to dismissal prior to its effective date. The court reversed the orders of dismissal, holding that a dismissal under § 995 operated as a bar to further prosecution only where the dismissal occurred after the effective date of the amendment of § 1387, which gave it that effect. The court stated that the construction of the amendment of § 1387 urged by defendants defeated the fundamental notions of fairness and disregard entirely the reliance of the superior court on the former procedural rules at the time it granted defendants' motion for dismissal, as the superior court made it abundantly clear it was relying upon the People's right to refile the charges.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders of dismissal as to each defendant holding that the dismissal which defendants asserted as a bar to further prosecution occurred at a time when such a dismissal was not a bar to further prosecution and therefore did not bar further prosecution and holding that to hold otherwise would have defeated the fundamental principles of justice and the valid expectations of the bench and bar.

Welsch v. Goswick


Civ. Nos. 24797, 26331


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


130 Cal. App. 3d 398; 181 Cal. Rptr. 703; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1525



March 31, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 30, 1982, and the petition of respondent and real party in interest Welsch for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 16, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. N 7347, Charles W. Froehlich, Jr. and Fiorenzo V. Lopardo, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The order awarding attorney fees and directing the cease and desist order is reversed. The trial court is instructed to modify the underlying injunction so as to prohibit only those uses of the property not consistent with a single-family residential purpose.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, husband and wife, sought a writ of prohibition or certiorari following an order from respondent Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which found them in violation of a stipulated injunctive judgment, ordered them to cease and desist operating a residential care facility, and awarded attorney's fees to the real party in interest.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, husband and wife, operated a nursing home. Real party filed a lawsuit to enjoin petitioners from using their property, arguing that the proposed use violated the single-family residential purpose restriction. The parties entered into a stipulated judgment. Petitioners filed a declaratory relief action, seeking to have the stipulated injunctive judgment declared invalid. Respondent trial court determined that petitioners' activities violated the injunction previously issued pursuant to the stipulated judgment and concluded that recently passed legislation had no effect on the injunction. On appeal, the court held that respondent abused its discretion in failing to modify the injunction to allow petitioners to use their property as a nursing home. The court stated that respondent had the inherent power to modify the injunction where changes rendered such action just and equitable. The court found that, in light of broad social changes, the covenant which the injunction purported to enforce could not prohibit operation of petitioners' nursing home. The judgment was reversed and respondent was instructed to modify the underlying injunction.


OUTCOME: Respondent trial court's order that found petitioners, husband and wife, in violation of a stipulated injunctive judgment was reversed because respondent could not enforce the injunction for activities that could no longer be deemed inconsistent with single-family residential use restrictions.
