Hiatt v. City of Berkeley


Civ. No. 39033


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


130 Cal. App. 3d 298; 181 Cal. Rptr. 661; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1516; 29 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P32,872



March 29, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 453459-2, Lyle E. Cook, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment, insofar as it fails to make an award of attorney fees to respondents, is reversed, with directions to the trial court to reconsider appellants' motion for attorney fees in light of Code of Civil Procedure section 1021.5 and to enter judgment for any attorney fees to which it finds appellants entitled. Those portions of the judgment enjoining that part of paragraph III of AAP pertaining to the use of written tests, and the whole of paragraph V of AAP regarding employment list qualifying categories are reversed. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed. Respondents to recover costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city officials sought review of judgment from the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which awarded damages to respondent employees and enjoined appellants from enforcement of certain provisions of appellant's affirmative action program. Respondents also appealed the trial court's refusal to order the promotion of respondents and denial of attorney fees.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, city and officials, sought review of judgment from the trial court which awarded damages to respondent employees and which enjoined appellants from enforcement of certain provisions of appellant city's affirmative action program. Respondents also appealed the trial court's refusal to order the promotion of respondents and denial of attorney fees. The court affirmed the judgment to the extent that it enjoined appellants from denying promotions on the basis of race, color, sex, national origin, or ancestry. The court found that any deliberate attempt to maintain a racial balance, whatever such a balance might be, would involve a violation of equal protection because maintaining such a balance would require an employer to hire or to refuse to hire on the basis of race. The court affirmed the denial of promotion, remanded to the trial court for a determination on the attorney fee issue, and reversed the lower court's decision requiring use of quantitative factors of test scores or grades and certain qualifying categories.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment enjoining appellants from denying promotions on the basis of race, color, sex, national origin, or ancestry but reversed the requirement for use of quantitative factors of test scores or grades and certain qualifying categories. The court affirmed the denial of promotion and remanded to the trial court for a determination on the attorney fee issue.

 

1429 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Brice


Crim. No. 20476


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


130 Cal. App. 3d 201; 181 Cal. Rptr. 518; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1505



March 25, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 20, 1982, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 23, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 22728, Coleman F. Fannin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a decision from the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which convicted him of conspiracy to commit kidnapping. The prosecution charged appellant with crimes not named in the commitment order under Cal. Penal Code § 739, which appellant claimed violated Cal. Const. art. I, § 14.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted of conspiracy to commit kidnapping, but had originally been charged by information under Cal. Penal Code § 739 with five offenses, including murder, which was the subject of the original complaint. The magistrate refused to rule on the sufficiency of the evidence of six offenses requested by appellant at the preliminary hearing. After trial, appellant sought review, asserting that § 739 violated Cal. Const. art. I, § 14. The court on appeal disagreed and affirmed the conviction, finding that the magistrate had erred in refusing to rule on probable cause for the offenses requested by appellant, but that appellant suffered no prejudice at trial because his own motion showed that he was aware of the possibilities. The court held that it was not the complaint but the totality of the evidence produced at the preliminary hearing which notified appellant of the potential charges. Finally, the court found that the information was not based on knowingly elicited perjured testimony because the prosecution informed appellant of it when the perjury was discovered, and the perjured testimony was favorable to appellant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for conspiracy to commit kidnapping, finding that the information statute was constitutional, and that although the magistrate erred in refusing to rule on the sufficiency of the evidence of other offenses at the preliminary hearing, appellant suffered no prejudice at trial because he was aware of the potential charges.

 

1430 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Aguilar v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 52709


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


130 Cal. App. 3d 34; 181 Cal. Rptr. 516; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1493



March 24, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 465139, Bruce F. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the matter is remanded to the Santa Clara County Superior Court with directions to issue a peremptory writ of prohibition restraining the respondent municipal court from proceeding upon criminal citation Nos. A0022269-V and A0021990-L.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a decision from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California), which summarily denied his petition for a writ of prohibition restraining respondent municipal court from proceeding upon criminal citations for violations of a town ordinance prohibiting "cruising" in the city's central business district.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was cited on two occasions for violating a town ordinance which prohibited "cruising" in the city's central business district. Appellant demurred to the citations on the ground that respondent municipal court had no jurisdiction to proceed on the charged offenses because the area of legislation was preempted by the state vehicle code. The demurrer was overruled. Appellant then filed a petition for a writ of prohibition in which he sought to restrain respondent from proceeding upon the criminal citations. Appellant's petition was summarily denied and appellant sought review of the decision. Cal. Veh. Code § 21 provided that no local authority could enact or enforce any ordinance on the matters covered by the code unless expressly authorized. The reviewing court found that the town ordinance was a regulation of individual motor vehicle drivers. As such, it was an enactment in violation of § 21, and hence was void. Therefore, the judgment was reversed and a peremptory writ of prohibition restraining respondent from proceeding upon the criminal citations was issued.


OUTCOME: The reviewing court held that the town ordinance prohibiting "cruising" was a regulation of individual motor vehicle drivers. As such, it was an enactment in violation of the state vehicle code and hence was void. Accordingly, a peremptory writ of prohibition restraining respondent municipal court from proceeding upon the criminal citations against appellant was issued.

 

1431 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Domino v. Superior Court


Civ. Nos. 53772, 53787


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


129 Cal. App. 3d 1000; 181 Cal. Rptr. 486; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1393



March 23, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 13, 1982. Barry-Deal, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. The petition of the real party in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 26, 1982. Broussard, J., did not participate therein. Richardson, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of prohibition issue restraining all further proceedings on the special circumstance allegations that Louis Reyes was killed while defendants were lying in wait, other than the dismissal of the allegations. In all other respects the two petitions are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants petitioned the court for a peremptory writ challenging a decision from the circuit court (California) that denied their motion to dismiss the state's special circumstances allegations brought under Cal. Penal Code § 995.


OVERVIEW: Defendants believed that the victim was planning on ripping them off. Defendants planned on beating the victim, so they picked him and his friend up. The victim and his friend were eventually killed. Defendants were charged with first-degree murder. Pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 187, the state alleged special circumstances, contending that the murders were committed while lying in wait. Defendants moved to dismiss the special circumstances. The trial court denied defendants' motions, so they filed a petition for peremptory writ. On appeal, the court issued the writ. The victim and his friend were killed one to five hours after defendants picked them up. In order for the lying in wait circumstances to apply, the killing had to take place during the period of concealment and flow continuously from the concealment. In defendants' case, there was a separation in time between the lying in wait period and the killing.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision denying defendants' motion to dismiss the state's special circumstances allegations because the special circumstances did not exist. The victims were not killed while defendants were lying in wait. The victims were captured during the period of lying in wait, but were not killed until some one to five hours after such capture.

 

1432 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hawes


Crim. No. 11245


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


129 Cal. App. 3d 930; 181 Cal. Rptr. 456; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1384



March 19, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 13, 1982, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 9, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Shasta County, No. 67148, Harold Underwood, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The motion to dismiss the appeal is denied; the judgment of conviction is reversed. Because the accusation was filed more than one year after the act of misconduct for which defendant was convicted, the trial court on remand is directed to dismiss count III.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant public official sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Shasta County (California), which removed him from office as district attorney after the jury found him guilty of one count of wilful misconduct, intoxication during working hours, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 3060.


OVERVIEW: Appellant public official was removed from office following a conviction on one count of wilful misconduct pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 3060. The court found that appellant was convicted based upon intoxication during working hours. Appellant argued that § 3060 was a general statute, and he should not have been charged under that statute. The court found that Cal. Penal Code § 3001 dealt specifically with the intoxication of a public official during working hours. The court found that a specific statute superseded a general statute, especially in this case where the specific statute dealt with the actual conduct of appellant. The court reversed and remanded the case, holding that under § 3001, appellant should have been charged within one year after the commission of the offense; prosecution was barred.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the removal from office of appellant public official, holding that he was convicted under a general statute when a specific statute would have applied to the charges of intoxication during working hours, and the specific statute superseded the general statute.

 

1433 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Benard


Crim. No. 10159.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


129 Cal. App. 3d 841; 181 Cal. Rptr. 436; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1376



March 18, 1982., As modified April 1, 1982


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. July 7, 1982, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. CR-14999, Roland Wilson, Judge. * 


* Retired judge of the municipal court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which convicted him of first-degree murder with special circumstances, robbery, firearm offenses, attempted murder, burglary, unlawful taking of a car, and receiving stolen property and sentenced him to life in prison without parole. Defendant argued that the urgency clause of 1977 Cal. Stat. 316, § 26, under which he was convicted, was invalid.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder with special circumstances, robbery, firearm offenses, attempted murder, burglary, unlawful taking of a vehicle, and receiving stolen property and was sentenced to life without parole. The court affirmed the judgment except that it stayed execution of the sentences for the unlawful taking and firearm counts because they violated the multiple punishment proscription of Cal. Penal Code § 654. The investigatory stop was not rendered improper merely because it was later determined that no actual parking violation occurred. The car search was justified by the detection of a marijuana odor. Severance of the attempted murder charge was properly denied because the modus operandi was so similar to that of the murder charge that the probative value of admitting evidence of one in a trial for the other outweighed the prejudicial effect. A special circumstance finding was proper because the robbery was not merely incidental to the murder. The urgency clause of 1977 Cal. Stat. 316, § 26, under which defendant was convicted and sentenced, was valid because Cal. Const. art. I, § 27 did not require that it be subject to a referendum.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment convicting defendant of first-degree murder with special circumstances, robbery, firearm offenses, attempted murder, burglary, unlawful taking of a vehicle, and receiving stolen property and the sentence of life without possibility of parole, except that execution of sentences for the unlawful taking and firearm counts was stayed because they violated the multiple punishment proscription of an applicable law.

 

1434 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Nakasone v. Randall


Civ. No. 63332


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


129 Cal. App. 3d 757; 181 Cal. Rptr. 324; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1365



March 15, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-3170, Sherman Juster, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The order is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant nonresident seller appealed from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied a motion to quash a prejudgment writ of attachment on real property and which continued the attachment in favor of plaintiff buyer based upon the determination that the sale arose out of the defendant's conduct of a trade, business, or profession pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 483.010(c).


OVERVIEW: Defendant nonresident seller, as a part of a move to Mexico, sold real property that she and her husband, before his death, had held as joint tenants for most of their adult lives. Plaintiff buyer filed a complaint that alleged breach of contract for sale of property and also obtained an order for issuance of writ of attachment for a nonresident under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 492.010, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 485.210. Defendant filed an answer and a motion to set aside the attachment order so as to release the attached property, but the motion was denied. On appeal the court reversed. The attachment order had to be set aside once defendant filed a general appearance unless plaintiff showed that his right to attachment was authorized under a provision other than § 492.010. Under the ordinary attachment procedure contained in Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 483.010(c), plaintiff failed to prove defendant sold the real property as part of a trade, business, or profession, because the sale of a personal real estate parcel that defendant and her husband had held for 30 years could not have arisen out of the business of being a broker but rather was strictly for her own account.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that granted plaintiff buyer a continuance of a prejudgment writ of attachment on defendant nonresident seller's real property. Plaintiff failed to prove that the sale of the real property arose out of defendant's conduct of a trade, business or profession and therefore rendered improper the continuance of the writ of attachment.

Hayworth v. City of Oakland


Civ. No. 45395


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


129 Cal. App. 3d 723; 181 Cal. Rptr. 214; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1362; 29 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P32,836



March 12, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 496642-3, Robert L. Bostick, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant firefighters sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County, (California), which denied their request, for a writ of mandate in an action against respondent city for not filling vacancies from the eligibility list.


OVERVIEW: Appellant firefighters sought review of the denial of their request for a writ of mandate in an action alleging that respondent city failed to fill vacancies from the eligibility lists which constituted a violation of the Meyer-Milias-Brown Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 3500 et seq. The trial court determined that an earlier writ of mandate was not stayed by a pending appeal. Respondents argued that the judgment of dismissal should be affirmed on the grounds of estoppel, failure to pursue grievance procedures, and lack of discriminatory motive. Respondents contended that appellants were estopped from repudiating a commitment to which they had agreed. The court held that the alleged acquiescence of appellants had a bearing on the question of a remedy but it was a matter for the trial court to determine on remand. Respondents contended appellants were barred by their failure to pursue the grievance procedure. The court concluded appellants were not bound to pursue grievance procedures as a prerequisite to judicial litigation of their constitutional and statutory claims. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded the case.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court, because appellant firefighters did not have to exhaust the grievance procedures before they sought judicial litigation of their constitutional and statutory claim.

In re Robert J.


Civ. No. 49166


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


129 Cal. App. 3d 894; 181 Cal. Rptr. 188; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1380



March 8, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified March 17, 1982. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 20, 1982. Broussard, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 15396, Allen E. Broussard, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which declared that appellant's minor son was free from appellant's custody and control pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 232(a)(7).


OVERVIEW: After a minor child was in foster care for approximately three years, respondent social services agency filed a petition pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 232(a)(7) seeking to have appellant mother's parental rights terminated. The trial court granted the petition and appellant sought review, contending a denial of due process stemming from failure to notify appellant of the legal consequences flowing from the minor's placement in a foster home; that respondent failed to meet its burden of proof under § 232(a)(7); and that the trial court erred in failing to consider alternatives to termination, or at least in continuing appellant's visitation rights. In this latter contention, appellant challenged the constitutionality of the statutory scheme insofar as it precluded continuation of visitation rights after termination. The court affirmed because notification was not required, respondent's evidence was sufficient, and the trial court did not err because complete termination was the essence of § 232. The court also held that the statute was not unconstitutional because alternatives to termination were available.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the termination of appellant mother's parental rights because the statute was not unconstitutionally overbroad, respondent social services agency's evidence was sufficient to support the termination of the rights, and respondent was not required to give appellant notice of the legal consequences of long-term foster care.

Medlock Dusters, Inc. v. Dooley


Civ. No. 19771


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


129 Cal. App. 3d 496; 181 Cal. Rptr. 80; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1342



March 4, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 31, 1982, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 12, 1982. Reynoso, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 287175, Cecily Nyomarkay, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the superior court denying Medlock Dusters, Inc.'s petition for a peremptory writ of mandate is reversed and the cause is remanded to the trial court with directions to issue a writ of mandate remanding the matter to the Department of Food and Agriculture for a specification of the reasons for the discipline imposed against Medlock by the adoption of appropriate findings of fact. The stay previously issued herein is discharged effective the date of the finality of this decision without further order. The parties will bear their respective costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant licensed pesticide operator sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate.


OVERVIEW: Appellant licensed pest control operator's employees made several aerial applications of pesticide that came into contact with humans. Respondent, the department of food and agriculture, commenced administrative proceedings against appellant. Appellant was disciplined pursuant to Cal. Food & Agric. Code § 11791(b) & (c) and Cal. Admin. Code tit. 3, § 3093. The administrative law judge (ALJ) suspended appellant's license and placed appellant on probation. Appellant petitioned the trial court for a writ of administrative mandate, which was denied. Appellant sought review, contending that the statutes and regulation under which it was disciplined were unconstitutionally vague and ambiguous, and that the findings of fact of respondent were inadequate. The court reversed the denial of the writ because the ALJ's proposed decision was inadequate. The trial court was to issue the writ remanding the matter to respondent for reasons that the discipline was imposed against appellant by adoption of appropriate findings of fact. In addition, the court upheld the validity of the statute because the use of nonmathematical terms did not render the statute impermissively vague.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of the administrative writ of mandate because respondent, the department of food and agriculture, did not specifically state the reasons that respondent adopted the findings of fact and imposed the discipline against appellant licensed pesticide operator.

Sacramento Data Processing Etc. Sales v. Department of Consumer Affairs


Civ. No. 20805


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


129 Cal. App. 3d 348; 181 Cal. Rptr. 51; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1325



February 26, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 20, 1982. Reynoso, J., did not participate therein. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 296418, Joseph A. DeCristoforo, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order) is reversed and the cause remanded to the trial court with directions to modify its injunction in a manner consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant partnership challenged the order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which granted preliminary injunction in favor of respondent Department of Consumer Affairs. Appellant filed action after being enjoined from engaging in false, fraudulent, or misleading advertising, acting as "employment agency" under the Employment Agency Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 9900 et seq., or collecting employment fees until licensed.


OVERVIEW: Appellant partnership sold a publication containing available jobs listings, and also advertised specific jobs in local newspapers. Respondent Department of Consumer Affairs, as well as the Bureau of Employment Agencies, informed appellant that it was conducting business in violation of the Employment Agency Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code §9900 et seq. Appellant filed a complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief, seeking a declaration that its' business did fall under § 9900, and to enjoin respondent and the bureau from enforcing the statutory provisions against appellant. OUTCOME: The court reversed the order and remanded to the trial court with directions to modify its injunction, holding that the trial court erred in awarding injunctive relief to respondent Department of Consumer Affairs. Under the Employment Agency Act, partnership which provided information about available job opportunities by selling daily booklet containing list of current openings was not "employment agency."

American Booksellers Assn., Inc. v. Superior Court

Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


129 Cal. App. 3d 197; 181 Cal. Rptr. 33; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1315; 8 Media L. Rep. 2014



February 25, 1982 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing respondent court to vacate its orders of January 7, 1981, denying petitioners' requests for preliminary injunctions in those matters entitled American Booksellers Association v. City of Paramount, Los Angeles Superior Court case No. C-345815, and American Booksellers Association v. Redondo Beach, Los Angeles Superior Court case No. C-345818, and to enter new and different orders granting preliminary injunctions against enforcement of Paramount Ordinance No. 478 and Redondo Beach Ordinance No. 2301 c.s.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, a booksellers association and its members, sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), to vacate its order denying petitioners injunctive relief from enforcement of city ordinances that prohibited the sale of sexually oriented materials to minors. Petitioners alleged that the city ordinances violated their U.S. Const. amend. I rights to free speech.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, a booksellers association and its members, sought injunctive relief from city ordinances that prohibited the sale of sexually explicit material to minors. Respondent trial court denied petitioners' request and petitioners filed a petition for writ of mandate to compel injunctive relief. Upon review, the court granted the writ of mandate and ordered respondent court to issue the injunction. The court held that the ordinances, which required sexually explicit materials to either be sealed or placed beyond the reach of minors, were overbroad because such requirements would also place the materials out of the view of adults who had the right to view them. The ordinances also gave merchants the option to exclude minors who were unaccompanied by parents. The court held that the attempt to exclude minors from retail establishments that sell a variety of other literature and other regular goods, was unconstitutional. The court also held that the ordinances had a chilling effect on petitioners' U.S. Const. amend. I, exercise of free speech. It directed that respondent court vacate its prior order and issue a preliminary injunction against enforcement of the ordinances.


OUTCOME: The court issued the writ requested by petitioners, a booksellers association and its members, and directed respondent trial court to vacate its previous order and enter an order enjoining enforcement of the ordinances because the ordinances were unconstitutionally overbroad and had a chilling effect on petitioners' exercise of free speech.

* Retired Judge of the superior court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.

Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 268254, Vernon G. Foster, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, law firm, corporation, and property owner, sought review of order of Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which granted summary judgment for respondent California Coastal Commission, in appellants' action for relief from public access regulations. The appeal was consolidated with appellant property owner's motion for attorneys' fees under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1021.5.

People v. Amremi


Crim. No. 38767.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


128 Cal. App. 3d 750; 180 Cal. Rptr. 471; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1263



February 11, 1982, As modified March 1, 1982


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. May 12, 1982, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A328907, Henry P. Nelson, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which revoked defendant's probation for involuntary manslaughter and sentenced him to state prison.


OVERVIEW: Defendant pleaded guilty to involuntary manslaughter and was placed on probation. Defendant was turned over to immigration for deportation. If he was not deported, he was to present himself for sentencing. Defendant's probation was revoked because he made threatening telephone calls and failed to either leave the United States or present himself for sentencing. The court affirmed the judgment that revoked defendant's probation. The court held that defendant failed to present substantial evidence that would have compelled the trial court to hold a hearing on his competence to stand trial. The court also held that defendant's probation could be revoked for subsequently committed offenses regardless of whether defendant had been prosecuted for them, because the standard of proof in the two proceedings was different. Further, there was no reasonable basis for concluding that the trial court punished defendant for contesting the hearing since there was evidence presented that defendant was dangerous. Finally, as a term of his plea bargain, defendant waived his right under Cal. Penal Code § 2900.5 to credit for the time served in the county jail.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that revoked defendant's probation for involuntary manslaughter because defendant failed to present substantial evidence that would have compelled the trial court to hold a hearing on his competence to stand trial, he subsequently committed other offenses, he was not punished for contesting the hearing, and he waived his right to receive credit for time served in the county jail.

 

1443 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Castner v. City of Oakland


Civ. No. 26396


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


129 Cal. App. 3d 94; 180 Cal. Rptr. 682; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1307



February 2, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 510124-5, Alan A. Lindsay, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant businessman sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which denied his request for a peremptory writ of mandate to compel respondent city to grant him a conditional use permit for the operation of an adult bookstore.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city passed an ordinance banning adult entertainment activity within 1,000 feet of a residential zone. As a nonconforming activity, appellant businessman's bookstore needed to obtain a conditional use permit to continue operation beyond the ordinance's one-year grace period. At the end of the grace period the respondent's planning commission denied appellant's application for a conditional use permit, which was affirmed by the respondent's city council. Appellant filed a writ of mandate challenging the validity of the ordinance. The trial court denied his request for a peremptory writ and appellant sought review on the grounds that the ordinance was unconstitutional because it caused him to lose his vested right to operate an adult entertainment business in existence before the ordinance was enacted. The court affirmed, holding that zoning legislation may validly provide for the eventual termination of nonconforming property uses without compensation if it provides a reasonable amortization period commensurate with the investment involved. Appellant had the burden of establishing the unreasonableness of the period as applied to his vested right, which he did not meet.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of a writ of mandate in favor of appellant businessman, holding that zoning legislation may validly provide for the eventual termination of nonconforming property uses without compensation if it provides a reasonable amortization period commensurate with the investment involved.

 

1444 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Money v. Krall


Civ. No. 6096, Crim. No. 5742


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


128 Cal. App. 3d 378; 180 Cal. Rptr. 376; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1237



February 1, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 3, 1982, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 20, 1982. Bird, C. J., Newman, J., and Reynoso, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County, No. 15867, Wickson R. Woolpert, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of commitment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant individual challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California), which found him mentally retarded and dangerous to others under the Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6500 et seq. and committed him to the Department of Developmental Services pursuant thereto.


OVERVIEW: At appellant individual's commitment hearing under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6500, appellant stipulated that he was dangerous to others, but denied the allegation of mental retardation. After receipt of testimony and documentary evidence, the trial court found petitioner to be mentally retarded and under § 6500 ordered him committed to a state institution. In affirming the judgment of the lower court, the court found that though § 6500 did not include a definition for the term "mental retardation" there existed commonly accepted definitions of the term, which the trial court applied in making its findings. Thus, the statute was not unconstitutionally vague. The court also found that the trial court applied the appropriate criteria as the trier of fact to the case, and that its judgment that appellant was mentally retarded was supported by the record.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court because it found that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6500 et seq. was not unconstitutionally vague in its definition of the term "mentally retarded" and that substantial evidence supported the lower court's decision that appellant individual was both mentally retarded and dangerous to others.

 

1445 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


White v. Board of Medical Quality Assurance


Civ. No. 24123


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


128 Cal. App. 3d 699; 180 Cal. Rptr. 516; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1258



January 29, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 17, 1982, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 14, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 450890, Jack R. Levitt, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant physician challenged the decision of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which denied her petition for writ of mandate to annul respondent Board of Medical Quality Assurance's decision revoking appellant's medical license.


OVERVIEW: Appellant physician was charged with unprofessional conduct in the acts of prescribing medicine to persons who were addicts and to persons representing themselves to be addicts. Respondent Board of Medical Quality Assurance revoked appellant's medical license but stayed the revocation for five years and placed her on probation with certain conditions. The trial court denied appellant's petition for writ of mandate to annul respondent's decision, ruling that the decision was supported by the record. The court affirmed concluding that there was no mandatory requirement of a finding that appellant failed to conduct a good faith prior examination before disciplinary could be taken under Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 2399.5. The court ruled that appellant was not denied due process of law since the purported exculpatory evidence was not admissible for want of foundation. The court rejected appellant's denial of effectiveness of counsel claim, noting that there was no right to counsel in civil cases. The court also found that the challenged provisions of § 2399.5 and Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11156 were not so vague as to raise constitutional doubts as to their enforceability.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment, holding that appellant physician received a fair hearing before respondent Board of Medical Quality Assurance and substantial evidence supported the trial court's findings approving respondent's procedures and decision.

 

1446 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Peer v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 62206


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


128 Cal. App. 3d 733; 180 Cal. Rptr. 137; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1261



January 26, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified February 10, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 347871, Richard A. Ibanez, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed with directions to issue a peremptory writ of prohibition against any further proceedings on count IX and to prohibit any further proceedings on counts I, II and III unless and until they are amended to conform to this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant misdemeanant sought review of a judgment of respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his petition for a writ of prohibition to prohibit his trial on a series of misdemeanor offenses.


OVERVIEW: Appellant misdemeanant was charged with nine misdemeanor counts involving unlawful practice of psychology and related misrepresentations and misbehaviors arising therefrom. Appellant petitioned respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) for writ of prohibition, contending that real party in interest state's charges against him failed to attach certain detailed declarations indorsed on the complaint, so that appellant could not determine the persons he was alleged to have unlawfully treated nor the offenses he was alleged to have unlawfully performed. Appellant's petition was denied and he sought review. The court reversed and granted appellant's petition for a writ of prohibition, holding that in misdemeanor cases not involving obscenity, the state must advise a defendant of what unlawful acts were intended to be covered by the complaint.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) and granted appellant misdemeanant's petition for a writ of prohibition on the grounds that real party in interest state's complaint failed to include endorsements referenced in the complaint which specified the charges against appellant.

 

1447 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Long Beach Fireman's Credit Union v. Franchise Tax Bd.


Civ. No. 61685


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


128 Cal. App. 3d 50; 179 Cal. Rptr. 869; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1209



January 20, 1982 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SOC 53084, Campbell M. Lucas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant credit union sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that was in favor of respondent franchise tax board in appellant's suit to recover corporate franchise taxes.


OVERVIEW: Appellant credit union was a member of a credit union national association. Appellant invested surplus funds in a securities program operated by a wholly owned subsidiary of the association. Appellant included in the gross income on which its state franchise tax was based, interest income received with respect to its investment in the program. Appellant then sued respondent franchise tax board for a refund of taxes, arguing that appellant's investments in the program were business activities with a member, and the income generated thereby was deductible from gross income pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax Code § 24405. The court affirmed a trial court judgment in favor of respondent. Covered business activities with members were the only income yielding "business activities" that were deductible under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 24405. The investment of surplus funds in government securities, whether or not through the conduit of the credit union membership, was not a covered transaction within the meaning of Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 24405.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Interest income, received by appellant credit union from the investment of its surplus funds in a government securities program operated by a wholly owned subsidiary of the national association of which appellant was a member, was not deductible from the gross income on which appellant's franchise tax was based.

 

1448 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


National Independent Business Alliance v. City of Beverly Hills


Civ. No. 61855


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


128 Cal. App. 3d 13; 180 Cal. Rptr. 59; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1205



January 19, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 7, 1982. Kaus, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 290321, Leon Savitch, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff business owners sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that granted summary judgment in favor of defendant city and found Beverly Hills, Cal., Ordinance 1697 and 1717 were constitutional and properly enacted as urgent.


OVERVIEW: California voters adopted Cal. Const. art. XIII A, which allowed cities to impose special taxes. Defendant city adopted Beverly Hills, Cal., Ordinance 1697, which increased business license taxes. The ordinance was enacted as urgent because defendant was confronted with a reduction of its budget and would not have been able to provide public services. Beverly Hills, Cal., Ordinance 1717 did not change the amount of taxes. Plaintiff business owners filed suit to challenge the constitutionality of the ordinances. The trial court found the ordinances were constitutional. Plaintiffs appealed. Cal. Gov't Code § 36934 provided that ordinances could not be passed within five days of their introduction unless they were urgent. The court found there was an urgency to allow continued provision of public services. Cal. Gov't Code § 36937 provided that an ordinance could take effect immediately if it was for immediate preservation of public peace, health, safety, or related to taxes for the expenses of the city. The court found there was a waiver of Cal. Gov't Code § 36937, and the ordinance could have immediate effect because the money related to the current expenses of defendant city.


OUTCOME: Judgment of the lower court, finding two city ordinances constitutional, was affirmed because the ordinances that increased a business license tax were properly enacted as urgent because their enactment was necessary to provide public services and the ordinances related to taxes for the expenses of defendant city.

 

1449 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Prysock


Crim. No. 4051


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


127 Cal. App. 3d 972; 180 Cal. Rptr. 15; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1200



January 18, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 17, 1982. Andreen, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 15, 1982. Bird, C. J., Mosk, J., and Reynoso, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 18655, David L. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is modified to provide that appellant is sentenced to state prison as follows: On count one (murder), to a term of life, the special circumstances findings are stricken; on count two (robbery), for the three-year middle base term plus an additional one-year term for use of a deadly weapon; execution of the sentence as to count two is stayed until finality of the conviction and sentence on count one, at which time the stay shall become permanent; on count three (burglary), for the two-year middle base term plus an additional one-year term for use of a deadly weapon; sentence on count three shall run concurrently with sentence as to count two; execution of the sentence as to count three is stayed until finality of the conviction and sentence on count one, at which time the stay shall become [***2] permanent; further, in the event that the stay of execution of sentence is vacated as to counts two and three, execution of the additional term for use of a deadly weapon as to count three is stayed until finality of the conviction and sentence as to count two, at which time the stay shall become permanent; on count four (vehicle theft), for the two-year middle base term; upon the finality of the conviction and sentence on count one, sentence as to count four is merged into the life term imposed on count one; on count five (escape), appellant is sentenced to county jail for six months; upon the finality of the conviction and sentence on count one, sentence as to count five is merged into the life term imposed on count one. Judgment as to count one is reversed and remanded for the limited purpose of resentencing as to the use of a deadly weapon. In the event the trial court does not exercise its discretion to strike the additional term of punishment for the deadly weapon enhancement, the one-year term for use of a deadly weapon is stayed pending finality of conviction and sentence on count one. As to count six, the judgment is reversed. The trial court is directed to prepare an [***3] amended abstract of judgment which reflects such modification and to forward a certified copy of the amended abstract to the Department of Corrections, which shall file same.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant minor challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 18655 (California), which found him guilty of first-degree murder, robbery, burglary, auto theft, escape from a youth facility and destroying evidence, arguing that he was improperly given his Miranda warning in violation of state law, his confession was not freely given, evidence did not support the conviction, and the prosecutor committed misconduct.


OVERVIEW: Defendant minor and another escaped from a youth facility, stole a vehicle, and broke into the victim's house. After they entered the house, they stabbed the victim and stole her belongings. Defendant was charged and found guilty of first degree murder, robbery, burglary, auto theft, escape from a youth facility and destroying evidence. Defendant challenged his conviction, arguing that he was not given proper Miranda warnings. The court reversed the conviction, stating that the Miranda warnings violated federal law. Plaintiff State of California appealed to the U.S. Supreme Court, which held the Miranda warnings was adequate under federal law, and remanded. On remand, the court determined that the Miranda warnings did not violate state law, and that his confession was freely given. The court also determined that the evidence was sufficient to support the conviction, and that the prosecutor did not commit misconduct during closing arguments. However, the court modified defendant's sentence to life imprisonment, rather than death, due to his age, and reversed the count for destroying evidence, as defendant did not destroy the evidence while there was an investigation of his crime.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part, and reversed in part, the judgment of the lower court, and held that defendant's Miranda warning did not violate state law, his confession was freely given, the evidence supported the guilty verdict and that the prosecutor did not commit misconduct. However, the court reversed defendant's conviction for destruction of evidence, as there was no investigation at the time of the destruction, and modified the sentence.

 

1450 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Mercedes-Benz v. State Bd. of Equalization


Civ. No. 60903


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


127 Cal. App. 3d 871; 179 Cal. Rptr. 758; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1177



January 14, 1982 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 17, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-61937, Thomas Wilfred LeSage, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff merchant sought review of an adverse judgment from the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California) in its action to recover taxes. The trial court held that plaintiff was subject to the use tax, and that the tax base was the price paid by plaintiff pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 6244.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff automobile merchant sought review of an adverse judgment from the trial court in its action to recover taxes. The court affirmed, holding that plaintiff was subject to the use tax, and that the tax base was the price paid by plaintiff for the vehicles pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 6244 as opposed to the cost to the car manufacturer of the raw materials used to manufacture the vehicles. The court held that § 6244 was not unconstitutional. The statute did not discriminate between merchants. In addition, in assessing income taxes, wholly owned subsidiary corporations and corporate divisions were treated as a unit for the calculation of the overall income to be used in arriving at the portion of that income attributable to California. In addition, the two taxes are entirely different and imposed on entirely different bases.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed, holding that plaintiff merchant was subject to a use tax on the vehicles it purchased and that the tax base was properly based on the price paid by plaintiff for the vehicles. The court held that there was no merit to plaintiff's contentions that the statute imposing the tax was unconstitutional because there was no evidence that the statute discriminated between merchants in establishing the tax base.

 

1451 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Roa v. Lodi Medical Group, Inc.


Civ. No. 19336.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


129 Cal. App. 3d 318; 181 Cal. Rptr. 44; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1323



Feb. 26, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted June 15, 1982 (S.F. 24435)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Joaquin County, No. 137828, James P. Darrah, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, minor and parents, sought review of an order of the Superior Court of San Joaquin County (California), which restricted the amount of appellants' attorney fees awarded pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6146, in appellants' medical negligence action against respondents, medical group, doctor, and hospital.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, minor and parents, entered into a contingency fee agreement with their attorneys for 25 percent of their recovery in a medical malpractice action seeking damages for alleged substandard care given by respondents, medical group, doctor, and hospital, during the birth of appellant minor. The trial court approved the settlement of appellants' claims but restricted appellants' attorney fees to an amount less than 25 percent of the recovery pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 6146. Appellants filed a notice of appeal and asserted that § 6146 was unconstitutional. The court dismissed the appeal. The court held that § 6146 was rationally related to the legitimate state interest of skyrocketing medical malpractice insurance premiums and did not deny equal protection under state or federal constitutions. The court found that § 6146 did not deprive appellants of the due process right to counsel under the state or federal constitutions. The court held that § 6146 did not violate the doctrine of separation of powers because the legislature's limitation on the amount of recoverable attorney fees did not interfere with the Supreme Court's authority to regulate the practice of law.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed appeal of order which restricted the amount of attorney fees of appellants, minor and parents, in their medical malpractice case against respondents, medical group, doctor, and hospital because the restriction did not deny state or federal constitutional standards of equal protection, due process right of counsel, or separation of powers.

 

1452 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sangiacomo


Civ. No. 50620.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


128 Cal. App. 3d 942; 180 Cal. Rptr. 594; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1286



Feb. 19, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted Mar. 19, 1982.
Appeals ordered dism. May 25, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 755428, Ira A. Brown, Jr., Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a suit brought by the state, defendant landlords appealed an order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), enjoining them from assessing their tenants a "nonrefundable rental fee" in violation of Cal. Civ. Code § 1950.5(i) and from failing to return the fee to their tenants within the statutory period. Defendants contended that they were denied due process and that the statute was void for vagueness.


OVERVIEW: In a suit brought by the state, the trial court enjoined defendant landlords from assessing their tenants a "nonrefundable rental fee" in violation of Cal. Civ. Code § 1950.5(i) and from failing to refund the fee within the statutory period in violation of Cal. Civ. Code § 1950.5(e). Defendants contended they were denied due process because the relief granted exceeded the state's request in its notice of motion for preliminary injunction for relief with respect to the assessment and not the refund, and that the trial court erred in applying the statute to a fee for entering a lease, instead of limiting it to payments used to secure the performance of conditions of the lease. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court held that defendants failed to produce a record on appeal that would allow the court to determine whether the defect in the notice was waived at the hearing in the trial court and thus it presumed the validity of the trial court's order. The court held that the "rental fee" was a "security" for purposes of the statute whose broad language clearly proscribed the assessment of a nonrefundable "payment" for a "any purpose."


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's order in a consumer protection suit brought by the state enjoining defendant landlords from assessing their tenants a "nonrefundable rental fee" and from failing to return the fee to their tenants within the statutory period. The court held that defendants failed to produce a record showing they were denied due process and that the applicable statute clearly defined "security" so as to include a rental fee.

 

1453 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Huston v. Pulley


Civ. No. 51170.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


128 Cal. App. 3d 120; 180 Cal. Rptr. 113; 1982 Cal. App. LEXIS 1216



Jan. 22, 1982 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Mar. 17, 1982 (S.F. 24411)
Mar. 16, 1983, cause retrans to Ct. of App., 1st Dist., Div. 1, for reconsideration in light of Bailey v. Loggins, 32 Cal.3d 907. (See 148 C.A.3d 590)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. HC 739, Richard M. Silver, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant prison officials sought review of the writ of habeas corpus issued in favor of respondent editor by the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), compelling appellants to publish a cartoon and photograph in a prison newspaper.


OVERVIEW: Appellant prison officials refused to allow respondent editor to publish a cartoon and photograph in the prison newspaper. Respondent appealed unsuccessfully to the board of corrections, and then filed a habeas corpus petition with the lower court, which was granted. Appellants sought review. The court affirmed the judgement of the lower court and concluded that First Amendment rights attached to the publication of a prison newspaper, limited only by prison security and discipline. The court held that § 723(a) of the Department of Corrections Administrative Manual, as applied by appellants to deny publication of the cartoon and photograph, was unconstitutional and contrary to statutory mandate, because it unjustifiably imposed limitations upon legitimate rights of prisoners. The materials did not so threaten prison discipline or other legitimate prison interests as to justify the deprivation of the First Amendment rights of inmates. The court concluded that censorship of the materials violated respondent's free speech rights, was prohibited by Cal. Penal Code § 2600, and was properly enjoined by the trial court's order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court granting respondent editor's petition for habeas corpus. The court found that the censorship of the prison newspaper was unconstitutional because it unjustifiably imposed limitations on legitimate rights of prisoners. The material at issue did not threaten prison discipline or other legitimate prison interests as to justify the deprivation of the First Amendment rights of inmates.

 

1454 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Rivera


Crim. No. 13082


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


127 Cal. App. 3d 136; 179 Cal. Rptr. 384; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2516



December 24, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 3, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR-51630, Earl H. Maas, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted him of first degree burglary in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 459, and assault with intent to commit murder in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 217.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was originally charged with assault with a deadly weapon and burglary. Prior to a readiness conference, appellant was told that state intended to file an amended information charging more serious crimes and enhancing allegations. At the conference, appellant was offered a plea agreement. After appellant rejected the plea, state amended the information and charged appellant with burglary committed within an inhabited building in the nighttime, Cal. Penal Code § 12022(b), and assault with intent to commit murder, Cal. Penal Code § 217, while using a knife. Appellant was convicted of the offenses charged in the amended indictment. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that the plea bargaining process was a valid part of the criminal justice system that did not offend appellant's due process rights. Plea bargains were a necessity to the functioning of the system. The court held that there was no error when the trial court denied a lineup requested by appellant because nothing was presented to suggest that there was any identification problem. The court also held that there was no improper interrogation that led to the post-arrest statement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction of first degree burglary and assault with intent to commit murder because state was permitted to charge appellant with more serious crimes and enhancing allegations after appellant rejected a plea offer.

 

1455 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Sandoval


Crim. No. 38592.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


127 Cal. App. 3d 58; 179 Cal. Rptr. 372; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2510



December 23, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Mar. 17, 1982 (Crim. 22523).
Mar. 16, 1983, cause retrans. to Ct. of App., 2nd Dist., Div. 2, for reconsideration in light of People v. Spears (1983) 33 Cal.3d 279.
Opn. filed Apr. 15, 1983 not for publication.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. CR 15142, Marvin H. Lewis, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which tried him as an adult and convicted him of murder with special circumstances, robbery, attempted robbery, and being armed and using a firearm during the commission of the those offenses.


OVERVIEW: Appellant juvenile was tried as an adult and convicted of murder with special circumstances, robbery, attempted robbery, and armed and using a firearm in the commission of the crimes. Appellant sought review of the convictions, asserting that his motion to set aside the information was improperly denied, the trial court abused discretion in denying his motion to sever his charges, and that as a minor, he could not be properly sentenced to life imprisonment. On review, the court affirmed the judgment. The court held that there was sufficient evidence at the preliminary hearing in the form of eyewitness testimony and positive identification for the trial judge to form reasonable suspicion that appellant committed the crimes charged. The court also held that the trial court did not err in refusing to sever his charges, because all of the crimes contained a common element of substantial importance and were properly joined. Further, the court maintained that the trial court did not err in sentencing appellant to life imprisonment, because the court properly imposed the only sentence that was available to him as a minor for the offense of murder under Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(i).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant juvenile's conviction as an adult of murder with special circumstances, robbery, and attempted robbery, because the trial court did not abuse its discretion when it refused to sever appellant's charges or when it denied appellant's motion to set aside the indictment. Further, the trial court properly imposed appellant's life imprisonment sentence.

 

1456 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Parker v. City of Fountain Valley


Civ. No. 25139


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


127 Cal. App. 3d 99; 179 Cal. Rptr. 351; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2514



December 23, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. 319783, Edward J. Wallin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and the case is remanded to the trial court with instructions to issue a peremptory writ of mandate compelling the city to set aside its decision sustaining the termination of appellant from employment with the city; to reconsider its decision, following a hearing conducted pursuant to a fair procedure which accords with the requirements of due process; and pending the hearing, to pay appellant back pay for the period between the date of his termination and the date of the hearing before the city manager.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former police officer sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which denied appellant's petition for a writ of mandate to compel respondent city to reinstate him to his permanent position in the police department. Appellant had been dismissed for using excessive force during an arrest.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, former police officer's, tenured employment was terminated by respondent city after appellant was found to have used excessive force during an arrest. Appellant was given a pre-disciplinary hearing and a post-termination administrative hearing, but did not have an opportunity to cross-examine witnesses. Appellant filed a petition for a writ of mandate, by which he sought to compel respondent to reinstate him to his permanent position. The trial court denied the writ, and appellant sought review. The court reversed and held that appellant's pretermination hearing did not fulfill minimum constitutional due process requirements, and that respondent never assumed the burden of proving the charges against appellant in the post-termination hearing. Appellant was not provided with a copy of materials upon which his termination was based and was not accorded a right to respond thereto. The record made it quite clear that respondent improperly shifted the burden of proof to appellant to disprove the charges against him.


OUTCOME: The court reversed, and remanded with direction to the trial court to issue a peremptory writ to compel respondent city to set aside its decision and hold a new hearing, because appellant police officer had been denied his fundamental due process rights in the prior proceedings and the burden of proof had been impermissibly shifted onto appellant. The court also ordered that appellant receive back pay pending the new hearing.

 

1457 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Bruno v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 25964


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


127 Cal. App. 3d 120; 179 Cal. Rptr. 342; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2515; 1981-2 Trade Cas. (CCH) P64,431



December 23, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 19, 1982.



DISPOSITION: Writ of mandate issued.

 [***2]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner milk purchasers filed a request for writ of mandate against respondent trial court, the Superior Court of Orange County (California), seeking to vacate respondent's order granting a motion filed by real parties in interest to strike petitioners' request for fluid class or "cy pres" procedures to distribute damages in an antitrust class action filed by petitioners under the Cartwright Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 16700 et seq.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner milk purchasers filed a class action suit against defendant supermarkets, under the Cartwright Act, Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 16799 et seq., alleging antitrust violations through artificially inflated milk prices. Petitioners sought fluid class or "cy pres" procedures for distribution of damages. Respondent trial court granted defendants' motion to strike the request, and petitioners filed for a writ of mandate. The court granted the writ, holding that fluid class recovery was not prohibited by the Cartwright Act, but that its use must be determined by the facts of a particular case. The court held that fluid class recovery was not a denial of due process, and stated that it would not follow federal interpretation of federal law on this issue. The court ordered the trial court to vacate its order striking petitioners' request for fluid class recovery and to issue a new order denying defendants' motion to strike.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner milk purchasers' writ of mandate, and ordered the trial court to vacate its order striking petitioners' request for fluid class damages and to issue a new order denying the motion to strike of defendant in the underlying action, because fluid class damages were not prohibited by state antitrust law and the trial court had not determined whether they were appropriate under the facts of the case.

People v. Noble


Crim. No. 39402


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


126 Cal. App. 3d 1011; 179 Cal. Rptr. 302; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2491



December 21, 1981, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 17, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 615935, Morio L. Fukuto, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which convicted him of first degree murder of a sheriff's deputy, felonious assault, and attempted murder, with personal use of a firearm as to each count.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first degree murder of a sheriff's deputy, felonious assault, and attempted murder, with personal use of a firearm as to each count. Defendant appealed. The court held that the penalty of life imprisonment without possibility of parole for the intentional murder with malice aforethought of a police officer in the performance of his duties and in retaliation therefor was not so disproportionate to the crime for which it was inflicted that it shocked the conscience and offended fundamental notions of human dignity as to constitute cruel and unusual punishment. The court affirmed the judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, holding that the penalty of life imprisonment was not disproportionate to defendant's offense of first degree murder of a sheriff's deputy intentionally killed while in the performance of his official duties and in retaliation for the performance of those duties, and that the penalty did not constitute cruel and unusual punishment.
