PMI Mortgage Ins. Co. v. City of Pacific Grove


Civ. No. 48451


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


128 Cal. App. 3d 724; 179 Cal. Rptr. 185; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2547



December 18, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of plaintiffs and appellants for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 17, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 75113, D. Richard Barelli, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed as to the cause of action for administrative mandamus and affirmed as to the denial of relief on the cause of action for declaratory relief; PMI and Morrow's motion for judicial notice of matters that occurred after the entry of judgment is denied. The City is awarded its costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, city, city council, and planning commission, appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), that granted a writ of mandamus pursuant to Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 1094.5, in favor of plaintiffs, property owners and mortgage insurance company, commanding defendants to grant a zoning variance to plaintiffs which would permit a lot split of contiguous lots into two separate building sites.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff property owners and mortgage insurance company were the owners of three contiguous lots located in a subdivision recognized by the Pacific Grove, Cal., Municipal Code § 23.57.010. The three lots had previously been under one ownership as a single building site and defendant planning commission denied plaintiffs' request for variance. Defendant city council sustained the defendant planning commission's denial of the request on appeal. The trial court granted plaintiffs' writ of mandate pursuant to Cal. Code Civ. Proc. § 1094.5 commanding defendant city council to grant plaintiffs' request. The trial court erroneously concluded that defendant city was estopped as a matter of law from denying the variance because the city was aware of the merger, restrictions and variances which had been granted to the previous single ownership. Defendants appealed and plaintiffs' cross-appealed the denial of declaratory relief. The court reversed the writ of mandamus and affirmed the denial of declaratory relief. The court found the trial court erred in finding that a fundamental vested right had been involved and for considering evidence that had not been before defendant city council.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's writ of mandamus compelling defendant city council to grant plaintiffs, property owners and mortgage insurance company, the requested variance. The court held sufficient evidence existed to deny the variance and plaintiffs had failed to meet plaintiffs' burden of showing hardship.

 

1460 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Funk


Crim. No. 22392.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


126 Cal. App. 3d 798; 179 Cal. Rptr. 155; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2467



December 16, 1981, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted Jan. 14, 1982 (See 130 C.A.3d 1043)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 24097, Richard P. Calhoun, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The people appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), dismissing a charge against defendant offender for selling narcotics in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11352. The people asserted the trial court erred in granting defendant's motion to arrest judgment and dismiss and in not pronouncing judgment following the trial court's guilty finding.


OVERVIEW: Defendant offender was found guilty following a bench trial of selling a narcotic in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11352. The trial court subsequently dismissed defendant's case finding defendant had suffered a discriminatory prosecution as a result of conduct by a police officer which in effect suppressed evidence from the prosecutor. The court reversed the judgment of dismissal holding that the trial court had erred in not denying the motion to arrest judgment and to dismiss and had erred in not appointing a time for pronouncement of judgment or determining a motion for probation as required by Cal. Penal Code § 1191. The court noted that following a conviction, the trial court had no discretion but to pronounce sentence or to grant probation and because the trial court's error only occurred following the unquestioned judicial finding of guilt, there would be no second trial but the case was remanded to the trial court for pronouncement of sentence.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court orders arresting the judgment and the judgment of dismissal finding that the trial court abused its discretion because upon conviction, the trial court only had the discretion to either pronounce sentence or grant lawful probation. The case was remanded back to the trial court for pronouncement of judgment.

 

1461 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Roth


Crim. No. 11797.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


126 Cal. App. 3d 876; 179 Cal. Rptr. 469; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2479



December 16, 1981, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. Feb. 10, 1982, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 44503, Judith McConnell, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), that resentenced defendant after determining an error had been made in computing the original sentence. Defendant asserted that the trial court lacked jurisdiction upon resentencing under Cal. Penal Code § 1170(d) and the court had no power to impose a greater sentence than initially pronounced.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was originally charged with 19 counts of robbery or attempted robbery with enhanced allegations of use of a firearm or deadly weapon. Defendant entered a guilty plea agreement stipulating that defendant's maximum prison term would be nine years, eight months. The trial court originally sentenced defendant to nine years, eight months but upon hearing resentenced defendant changing the disposition of count 9 from concurrent to consecutive. Defendant claimed this had the effect of imposing a greater sentence. The court affirmed the judgment holding that a prohibition against imposing a greater sentence did apply to a sentence enhanced by running multiple terms consecutively where the original sentence provided for concurrent terms. However, the court noted the prohibition did not apply to resentencing occasioned by an error of law in the first sentencing procedure where the enhancement of subordinate terms resulted in an "unauthorized" sentence or a sentence which utilized an unavailable ground for enhancement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court holding that the trial court was not bound by the limits of the term originally imposed upon defendant because the prohibition against imposing a greater sentence on resentencing did not apply when resentencing was required due to an error of law during the first sentencing procedure which resulted in an unauthorized sentence.

 

1462 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Wells v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 20662


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


126 Cal. App. 3d 808; 179 Cal. Rptr. 128; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2468



December 16, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 7, 1982, and petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 10, 1982. Reynoso, J., did not participate therein.



DISPOSITION: The stay is dissolved, the alternative writ of prohibition is discharged, and the peremptory writ is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner public defender challenged the validity of sentencing guidelines established by respondent Municipal Court of Sacramento (California), for use in cases of prostitution and drunk driving. Petitioner contended the guidelines were unconstitutional in application, violative of due process, contrary to statutory law, and legislation by a judicial body. Respondent argued that petitioner lacked legal authority to file the action.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner public defender by petition for writ of prohibition questioned the validity of sentencing guidelines established by respondent municipal court. Petitioner asserted that the guidelines were unconstitutional and contrary to statutory law. Petitioner also contended that he had authority from the legislative intent in the progressive expansion of the duties of the public defender. The court held that petitioner's duty arose from statute upon court appointment or request by the defendant; the private attorney's duty arose from the contractual undertaking of representation. The court reasoned that the authority of the public defender was limited by law to act in a representative capacity upon request or order of the court, or request of a particular defendant under specified circumstances, and therefore petitioner could not proceed in his official capacity, on his own initiative, to raise issues detached from the representation of a particular defendant. The court found that it would have been acting in excess of its jurisdiction were it to grant the petition. The stay was dissolved, the alternative writ of prohibition was discharged, and the peremptory writ was denied.


OUTCOME: The court held that the stay was dissolved, the alternative writ of prohibition was discharged, and the peremptory writ was denied. The court held that petitioner public defender could not proceed in his official capacity, on his own initiative, to raise issues detached from the representation of a particular defendant. The court also reasoned that it would be acting in excess of its jurisdiction were it to grant the petition.

 

1463 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Vincent B. v. Joan R.


Civ. No. 61975


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


126 Cal. App. 3d 619; 179 Cal. Rptr. 9; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2449



December 8, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 17, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. CF13517, Raymond Choate, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, alleged biological father, sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), dismissing under Cal. Evid. Code § 621 his action to establish that he was the biological father of the child of respondent parents.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, alleged biological father, brought an action to establish a father and child relationship with the son of respondent father and mother. The trial court dismissed the action, and appellant sought review. The court affirmed, finding that, under Cal. Evid. Code § 621, the issue of a wife cohabiting with her husband, who was not impotent or sterile, was conclusively presumed to be a child of the marriage, so that there were no triable issues of fact as to conditions for application of the presumption. The court found that there was no merit to appellant's contention that the court should have deferred ruling until blood tests could be taken because blood test results were not admissible to contradict the conclusive presumption of § 621. The court found that a husband and acknowledged legal father was in a vastly different situation from a man outside the family unit claiming to be the father, so that dissimilar treatment was fully warranted and not prohibited discrimination.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the dismissal of action of appellant, alleged biological father, to establish a father and child relationship with respondent father and mother's son, because issue of wife cohabiting with her husband was conclusively presumed to be a child of the marriage.

 

1464 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Gilliland v. City of Palmdale


Civ. No. 59028.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


127 Cal. App. 3d 386; 179 Cal. Rptr. 627; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2535



December 7, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. Feb. 10, 1982, and the Reporter of Decisions is directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C107092, Thomas Wilfred LeSage, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowner appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered judgment on the pleadings in favor of defendant city of Palmdale, California, after defendant refused plaintiff's request to rezone his property for commercial development.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff landowner requested and was denied by defendant city of Palmdale, California, a change in zoning for his land to commercial planned development. The property was situated in a zone designated for agricultural and open land uses, but also permitted uses such as one-family dwellings, animal hospitals, schools, kennels, and veterinary offices. Plaintiff brought an action alleging that the denial of zone change amounted to a taking without just compensation in violation of U.S. Const. amends. V, XIV. The trial court entered judgment on the pleadings without leave to amend in favor of defendant. The court affirmed, holding that plaintiff had failed to show that the refusal to rezone his property was a taking of property. The health and welfare of defendant's citizens were well served by maintaining open land in the vicinity of the airport, and therefore the refusal to rezone was a reasonable exercise of police power. Moreover, the mere diminution in value of property did not amount to a deprivation of all reasonable or economically viable uses. The court noted that the zoning ordinance permitted any number of uses valuable and essential to a diversified community economy.


OUTCOME: Judgment on the pleadings without leave to amend in favor of defendant city of Palmdale, California, was affirmed. Plaintiff landowner had failed to show that defendant's refusal to rezone his land for commercial development was an unconstitutional taking. The refusal was a reasonable exercise of police power, and did not deprive plaintiff of all economically viable uses, as the zone in which it was situated allowed a variety of valuable uses.

Santa Catalina Island Conservancy v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 59428


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


126 Cal. App. 3d 221; 178 Cal. Rptr. 708; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2415



November 30, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 27, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 159602, Campbell M. Lucas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant Los Angeles County and the California Board of Equalization appealed a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), in favor of plaintiff conservancy, finding that Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 214 and 214.02 were constitutional and that the property owned by plaintiff on Santa Catalina Island was, with limited exceptions, entitled to the welfare exemption provided therein.


OVERVIEW: The trial court found that Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 214 and 214.02 were constitutional and that the property owned by plaintiff conservancy on Santa Catalina Island was, with limited exceptions, entitled to the welfare exemption provided therein. Defendants Los Angeles County and the California Board of Equalization appealed, contending that the trial court erred in holding § 214.02 constitutional in that the legislature exceeded the authority to exempt property from taxation granted by Cal. Const. art. XIII, § 4(b). The court disagreed, finding that nonprofit organizations such as plaintiff, formed and conducted for the purpose of preserving natural environments and recreational opportunities for the benefit of the public, came within the term "charitable" by lessening the burdens of government. Moreover, the legislature had construed the term "charitable" as authorizing exemption of such organizations. The court concluded that § 4(b) authorized the exemption in question. The court also found that plaintiff complied with the requirements of Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 214.02. Each use was itself exempt and all were part of an integrated operation. The court affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment below against defendant Los Angeles County and the California Board of Equalization, finding that the statute in question was constitutional and that the property owned by plaintiff conservancy was with limited exceptions entitled to a welfare exemption. Plaintiff's use of the property complied with the requirements of the statute because each use was itself exempt and all were part of an integrated operation.

 

1466 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Truett


Crim. No. 13117


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


126 Cal. App. 3d 156; 178 Cal. Rptr. 535; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2409



November 25, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 11, 1981, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 20, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CRV 6098, Zalman J. Scherer, Temporary Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged an order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which sentenced him to prison for a mid-term of two years after appellant entered a plea of guilty for indecent exposure, Cal. Penal Code § 314, and admitted to prior convictions.


OVERVIEW: Appellant entered a plea of guilty for indecent exposure and admitted to prior convictions, which elevated a second conviction to a felony under Cal. Penal Code § 18. Appellant was sentenced to a mid-term of two years in prison. Appellant claimed that the punishment violated the prohibition against cruel and unusual punishment found in Cal. Const. art. I, § 6 and U.S. Const. amend. 8. The court affirmed the sentence and held that no ground existed for concluding that the repeated offense of indecent exposure could not be classified by the legislature as a felony. The court found that fixing the penalty was a matter of legislative discretion, and the discretion was not abused by selecting the least severe set of alternative felony penalties prescribed by law. The court ruled that there was no constitutional infirmity in the legal framework utilized as the bases for appellant's sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a decision to sentence appellant to a mid-term of two years in prison for indecent exposure because the repeated offense of indecent exposure could be classified by the legislature as a felony, and it was not an abuse of discretion to select the least severe felony penalty.

 

1467 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


City and County of San Francisco v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 51855


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


125 Cal. App. 3d 879; 178 Cal. Rptr. 435; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2374



November 23, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 22, 1981, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. The petition of real parties in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 27, 1982.



DISPOSITION: A peremptory writ of mandate will issue. The alternative writ is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner brought a petition for a writ of mandate from an order of the trial court (California) permitting the disclosure of citizens' complaints in the personnel file of the police officer who arrested real parties in interest.


OVERVIEW: Real parties in interest were arrested, and they sought the disclosure of the arresting officer's personnel file relating to the use of excessive force, racial/ethnic prejudice, or false arrest, for the period of his employment with petitioner, a city and county. The trial court ordered disclosure of the information, and petitioner brought a petition for a writ of mandate, arguing that the order violated Cal. Evid. Code § 1045, which excluded from disclosure to any litigant information consisting of complaints concerning conduct of a peace officer occurring more than five years before the event or transaction which was the subject of the litigation in aid of which discovery or disclosure was sought. The court ruled that when the right to disclosure of information conflicted with the constitutional right of privacy, there must be a careful balancing of the compelling public need for discovery against the fundamental right of privacy. The court granted the petition, concluding that § 1045 was reasonably necessary to balance the competing public interests in preserving an individual's privacy and in facilitating the ascertainment of truth in judicial proceedings.


OUTCOME: Petition for a writ of mandate was granted, and the order of the trial court was vacated, where the legislature did not violate due process so long as an enactment was procedurally fair and reasonably related to a proper legislative goal, and the cutoff period of the statute excluding the disclosure of information related to a peace officer's conduct carefully and properly balanced the competing state interests of privacy and disclosure.

People v. Roder


Crim. No. 21419.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


126 Cal. App. 3d 985; 179 Cal. Rptr. 244; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2489



November 19, 1981, Decided


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Jan. 20, 1982 (See 33 C.3d 491)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 72347, Roland K. Hall, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), which after a jury verdict, found defendant guilty of receiving stolen property in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 496 and possessing marijuana for sale in violation Cal. Health & Safety Code §11357.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was coproprieter of a second-hand store. Items were seized from the store and were identified as stolen. A jury found defendant guilty of receiving stolen property and possessing marijuana for sale. On appeal, defendant contended that he was deprived of due process because a jury instruction based on the statutory presumption of Cal. Penal Code § 496(2) regarding the element of knowledge, unconstitutionally shifted the burden of proof. The court affirmed the judgment and held that a reasonable jury could have concluded, even without the statutory presumption, that defendant knew that property had been stolen. The court found that an examination of § 496(2) supported the court's finding that the presumption affected only the burden of production, and did not shift the burden of persuasion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision finding defendant guilty of receiving stolen property because a reasonable jury could have found that defendant knew property was stolen even without a statutory presumption, and the presumption did not shift the burden of persuasion.

 

1469 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Music Plus v. Baker


Civ. No. 59841


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


125 Cal. App. 3d 819; 178 Cal. Rptr. 415; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2366



November 18, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 13, 1982. Kaus, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 281370, Robert I. Weil, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city and others sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that declaring a local ordinance unconstitutional and permanently enjoining its enforcement. The ordinance regulated the display to minors of drug paraphernalia for sale. The injunction had been granted primarily on the ground that the ordinance was impermissibly vague.


OVERVIEW: Appellant city and others sought review of a judgment that declared a local ordinance, which regulated the display to minors of drug paraphernalia for sale, was unconstitutional. Under the judgment, appellants were permanently enjoined from enforcing the ordinance. The injunction had been granted primarily on the ground that the ordinance was impermissibly vague. The court reversed the judgment that granted the injunction, as the trial court had erred in its finding that the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague because the ordinance, consistent with a similar ordinance that had been sustained by a prior decision, defined the paraphernalia by the manner in which it was displayed and the proscribed devices were limited by the context to devices being sold or displayed for sale for the purpose of using the specified drugs. The court further construed the ordinance to include a knowledge requirement.


OUTCOME: The judgment granting a permanent injunction enjoining appellant city and others from enforcing a city ordinance regulating the display to minors of drug paraphernalia for sale was reversed. The ordinance was not unconstitutionally vague and the alternative constitutional grounds urged for sustaining the injunction had been rejected in a prior decision that involved a similar ordinance.

 

1470 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Licorice Pizza Records & Tapes, Inc. v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 61215


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


125 Cal. App. 3d 825; 178 Cal. Rptr. 309; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2367



November 18, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 13, 1982. Kaus, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 327731, Jerry Pacht, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting a preliminary injunction is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant county and others sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that granted a preliminary injunction, restraining the enforcement of County of Los Angeles, Cal., Ordinance 12146, which regulated the display to minors of drug paraphernalia for sale. The trial court granted the injunction primarily on the ground that the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Appellant county and others sought review of an order, which granted a preliminary injunction and restrained the enforcement of County of Los Angeles, Cal., Ordinance 12146. The ordinance essentially regulated the display to minors of drug paraphernalia for sale. The injunction had been sought by respondent corporation and others and had been granted primarily on the ground that the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague. The court reversed the order granting the injunction, as the order enjoining enforcement of the ordinance was error. The ordinance, consistent with a similar ordinance that had been sustained in another case, defined the paraphernalia by the manner in which it was displayed and the proscribed devices were limited by the context to devices being sold or displayed for sale for the purpose of smoking or otherwise using the specified drugs. The court opined that the ordinance made explicit that the evil in certain devices was not necessarily inherent in the device itself but in the manner in which it was promoted for drug purposes and concluded that the ordinance was not unconstitutionally vague or overbroad.


OUTCOME: The order granting a preliminary injunction, restraining appellant county from enforcing a county ordinance regulating the display to minors of drug paraphernalia for sale, was reversed, as the ordinance was not unconstitutionally vague and the alternative constitutional grounds urged for upholding the injunction were rejected in another case involving a similar ordinance.

 

1471 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


International Ass'n of Firefighters v. City of San Diego


Civ. No. 22873.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


125 Cal. App. 3d 605; 178 Cal. Rptr. 250; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2343



November 12, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Jan. 27, 1982 (See 34 C.3d 292)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 421183, Louis M. Welsh, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, city, retirement fund, and board of administration, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which found in favor of respondent union and set aside the decision by appellant board of administration to increase the rate of contribution paid by respondent's members to appellant retirement fund.


OVERVIEW: Respondent union sought to set aside a decision by appellant board of administration that increased the rate of contribution by respondent's members who were appellant city's employees to appellant retirement fund. The trial court found in respondent's favor, and appellants, city, retirement fund, and board of administration, sought review. The court affirmed, finding that the language of appellant city's charter and ordinance did not authorize the increased rates of contribution. The court found that the percentage contributed by respondent's members depended on their age when they became members and was to remain unchanged, except on change in benefits or upon increased fund earnings. The court found that appellant city agreed to contribute an amount "sufficient" to make up the retirement allowance that might be somewhat greater than the employee contribution and that appellant retirement fund's integrity was not endangered by the court's construction of the charter and ordinances. The court determined that vested contractual rights applied only to respondent's members employed before July 1, 1978, and that appellant city could change the rate for those employed thereafter.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court that set aside the decision by appellant board of administration, which increased the rate of contribution paid by respondent union's members to appellant retirement fund, was affirmed because appellant city's charter and ordinances prohibited the rate increase.

 

1472 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Service Employees Internat. Union v. City of Santa Barbara


Civ. No. 60943


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


125 Cal. App. 3d 459; 178 Cal. Rptr. 89; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2333



November 10, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 20, 1982.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Barbara County, No. 133387, L. Donald Boden, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (denying the petition for peremptory writs of prohibition and mandamus) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant union challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Barbara County (California), which denied appellant's petition for writs of mandamus and prohibition to restrain respondents, city, administrator and employee relations officer, from holding an election of city employees to determine whether appellant union or real party in interest, respondent association, would represent them.


OVERVIEW: Appellant union was the exclusive bargaining representative of respondent city's employees. Respondent association sought a decertification election to create two new bargaining units, and respondent employee relations officer granted its request for an election. The trial court denied appellant's petition for writs of mandamus and prohibition, and the court of appeal denied appellant's petition for a writ of supersedeas. The election was held; appellant was decertified as to the two new units and sought review. The court affirmed, holding that imposition of a three-year contract bar doctrine would constitute an invasion of a legislative prerogative. By consulting with appellant's predecessors on the intent of Santa Barbara, Cal., Mun. Code § 3.12, respondent city fully complied with the requirements of Cal. Gov't Code § 3507. Once the 12-month period following recognition of appellant as exclusive bargaining agent expired, appellant had no cognizable property interest supported by "rules and understanding" that would be entitled to due process protection, and appellant was not deprived of equal protection under the law.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court that denied appellant union's petition for writs of prohibition and mandamus to restrain respondents, city, administrator, and officer, from holding a decertification election to determine whether respondent association would represent respondent city's employees was affirmed because the three-year contract bar doctrine did not apply and no deprivation of due process protection or equal protection existed.

 

1473 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Cramer v. Gillermina R.


Civ. Nos. 25613, 25604, 25605


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


125 Cal. App. 3d 380; 178 Cal. Rptr. 69; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2325



November 6, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 20, 1982. Bird, C. J., and Newman, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, Nos. 196441, 199836, and 200316, Bob Neil Krug, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order of commitment) in each case is affirmed. The views expressed herein regarding due process do not require modification of the judgment, since the matter is moot.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mentally retarded patients sought review of orders of commitment entered by the San Bernardino County Superior Court (California), arguing they were deprived of their due process and equal protection rights under Cal. Const. art. 1, § 7 when they were temporarily held after the expiration of their initial commitment periods.


OVERVIEW: Appellant mentally retarded patients were committed. At the expiration of their initial commitment periods, the trial court entered temporary hold orders preventing their release. Appellants sought review of the ensuing recommitment orders, asserting the interim hold orders violated their due process and equal protection rights under Cal. Const. art. 1, § 7. Though the claims were moot, the court found no equal protection violation because the statutory distinction between mentally ill and retarded individuals was reasonable. After examining appellants' private liberty and dignitary interests, the risk of an erroneous deprivation of liberty, and the public interest in appellants' continuing confinement, the court found that appellants were not deprived of due process. Although an adversarial probable cause hearing was not required before a continued detention order was issued, appellants were entitled to respond to the grounds for the order. As appellants received such opportunity, their constitutional claims were rejected. The court also rejected appellants' claim that they were entitled to credit for the time they were held, and affirmed the commitment orders.


OUTCOME: Commitment orders were affirmed because appellant mentally retarded patients' constitutional claims were moot and appellants were not entitled to credit for time served. Regardless of mootness, the court determined that appellants were only entitled to respond to the grounds for continuing commitment orders, and were not entitled to adversarial probable cause hearings; further, appellants' equal protection claims were deemed meritless.

Keenan v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 51777


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


126 Cal. App. 3d 576; 177 Cal. Rptr. 841; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2445



October 30, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 18, 1981. Miller, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 3, 1982. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the application should be granted.
Writ granted by Keenan v. Superior Court, 30 Cal. 3d 750, 1982 Cal. LEXIS 147 (1982)



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the trial court to vacate its order denying petitioner's request for notice of the particular evidence to be introduced in support of the charge of aggravated circumstances and to issue a new order granting said request by petitioner. In all other respects the petition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of mandate directing respondent Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) to grant petitioner's discovery motions in his trial for murder.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was charged with various offenses, including murder with special circumstances justifying the death penalty. Respondent trial court denied petitioner's discovery motions and petitioner sought a writ of mandate to compel respondent to grant those motions. The court denied the writ as to information of prosecutorial decisions to charge special circumstances. The court held that the exercise of prosecutorial discretion did not deprive petitioner of his rights under U.S. Const. amends. V and XIV or Cal. Const. art. I, § 15. The court also denied the writ as to the testing of physical evidence. The court held that the trial court's order regarding testing, although it placed limits on the time and place, did not undermine his right to test the evidence. The court granted the writ as to advice of aggravating circumstances. The court held that petitioner was not given a reasonable period of time prior to trial to adequately prepare for the death penalty phase.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner a peremptory writ of mandate directing respondent trial court to vacate its order denying petitioner's request for notice of evidence of aggravated circumstances. The court denied the writ as to the other two discovery orders. The court held that petitioner was not given the evidence of aggravated circumstances at a reasonable time.

Buchan v. Las Virgenes Unified School Dist.


Civ. No. 61776


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


124 Cal. App. 3d 1088; 177 Cal. Rptr. 788; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2291



October 28, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied November 25, 1981, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 23, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C312907, Jerry Pacht, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment denying the petition for a peremptory writ is reversed. The case is remanded for a determination of the amount of unpaid salary and benefits that accrued to appellant from December 13, 1979, to February 1, 1981, less any credits to which respondents are entitled by reason of payments made to appellant or other mitigation of damages as provided by law.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant employee sought review of a judgment by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) denying a petition for a peremptory writ of mandate to compel respondent school district and superintendent of the district to reinstate appellant to his previous position of employment with the school district with full salary and benefits retroactive to the date on which appellant was suspended without pay.


OVERVIEW: Appellant employee, a school personnel director, contended that the suspension of appellant's salary and benefits by the board of education of respondent school district, prior to any finding of mental illness, was inconsistent with the requirements of Cal. Educ. Code § 44942. Respondent school district and superintendent of the school district asserted that their actions were consistent with the requirements of § 44942 because the statute did not expressly define the nature and scope of the psychiatric examination of the employee. The court held that the lack of an express written finding by the psychiatric panel, within the requisite statutory time, that appellant was suffering from mental illness of such a degree as to render him incompetent to perform his duties should have been treated by the board as a finding that the employee was not suffering from such mental illness and, in fact, a conclusion that the employee be permitted to return to his duties. Accordingly, the court reversed the judgment denying the petition for a peremptory writ. The court remanded the case for a determination of the amount of unpaid salary and benefits that accrued to appellant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment denying the petition for a peremptory writ and remanded the case for a determination of unpaid salary and benefits that accrued to appellant employee. The court held that the lack of a finding by a psychiatric panel that appellant had a mental illness should have been treated by respondent school district and superintendent of the school district as a conclusion that the employee should be permitted to work.

Torrance v. Workers' Compensation Appeals Bd.


Civ. No. 59479.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FOUR


124 Cal. App. 3d 1047; 177 Cal. Rptr. 876; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2286; 46 Cal. Comp. Cases 1143



October 27, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Jan. 7, 1982 (See 32 C.3d 371)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner city sought review of an order which dismissed its action against respondent California State Compensation Insurance Fund for contribution for an injury sustained by an employee.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner city sought review of the order which dismissed its action against respondent State Compensation Insurance Fund for contribution for an injury sustained by an employee during the time respondent was an insurer. Petitioner contended that an amendment to Cal. Lab. Code §5500.5 had impaired the obligation of the contract between petitioner and respondent. The court affirmed the order of the Worker's Compensation Appeals Board dismissing the action. The court determined that although the relationship between the petitioner and insurer was contractual in origin, law established the scope of the insurer's liability. The court concluded that the provision to which the petitioner objected was simply a repeal of a provision allowing the petitioner to seek contribution from a prior insurer. The court held that the change in law did not in any sense impair the obligation of any contract.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board, because the change in law which relieved respondent State Compensation Insurance Fund of its obligation had not served to impair any obligation created by contract.

 

1477 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Laguna Publishing Co. v. Golden West Publishing Corp.


Civ. No. 20650.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


124 Cal. App. 3d 967; 177 Cal. Rptr. 703; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2281; 1982-1 Trade Cas. (CCH) P64,650



October 26, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted Nov. 24, 1981 (See 131 C.A.3d 816)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 207112, Walter W. Charamza, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, publisher of a give-away newspaper, challenged the decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which denied plaintiff's application for an injunction in its claim that defendant, owner of common areas within a private community, violated plaintiff's constitutional rights of free speech and free press by denying it access to the community for distribution of its paper.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff, publisher of a give-away newspaper, filed a complaint against defendant, owner of common areas within a private, residential, walled community where only resident-approved access was permitted through guarded security gates, when plaintiff was denied entry for unsolicited, free delivery to the residents of its paper. Another similar paper was given exclusive access to the community. After trial, the jury resolved the issues against plaintiff. On appeal the court reversed, holding that plaintiff's exclusion from the community was unconstitutional discrimination. The court held that defendant, acting with the implicit sanction of the state's police power behind it, discriminated against the free speech and free press rights of plaintiff by excluding it from the community after defendant permitted the unsolicited delivery of plaintiff's competitor to the residents of the community. The court directed the trial court to try the issue of damages arising from the illegality of the exclusion, including whether there was concerted activity in violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 16720(a), and whether there were any actual damages proximately resulting from such exclusion.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's ruling for defendant, owner of common areas in a private community, in plaintiff publisher's suit for discrimination. The court held that defendant violated plaintiff's free-speech and free-press rights when it denied plaintiff access to the property after it gave plaintiff's competitor access to the property for delivery of a free newspaper.

 

1478 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Bilofsky v. Deukmejian


Civ. No. 60462


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


124 Cal. App. 3d 825; 177 Cal. Rptr. 621; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2269



October 21, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 10, 1981, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 16, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 312951, Harold J. Ackerman, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, initiative proponents, sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which entered judgment in favor of defendants, state officials, in plaintiffs' action for a declaration that Cal. Elec. Code § 29770, which prohibited plaintiffs from using names and addresses obtained from initiative petitions to communicate with the persons signing the petitions, was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, initiative proponents, sought to qualify an initiative for placement on a general election ballot. They wished to use the names and addresses of signers of petitions in order to mail communications on their initiatives to the signers, but were prohibited from doing so by Cal. Elec. Code § 29770, as interpreted by 63 Op. Cal. Att'y Gen. 37 (1980). Plaintiffs brought suit against defendants, state officials, for a declaration that § 29770 was unconstitutional. The trial court entered judgment for defendants, and plaintiffs sought review. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment. The court found that because § 29770 was adopted for a proper legislative purpose, to preserve the privacy of persons signing petitions, rather than to impede the initiative process, it was properly examined under a balancing test, and that it had only an incidental effect upon plaintiffs' rights under U.S. Const. amends. I, XIV, and Cal. Const. arts. I, §§ 2, 3, II, § 8. The court also found that the statute was not overbroad, and that the legislature intended to prohibit any use of the information in question other than in qualifying the initiative for the ballot.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of defendants, state officials, in a suit by plaintiffs, initiative proponents, to declare unconstitutional a statute prohibiting plaintiffs' use of the names and addresses of signers of initiative petitions, because the statute was adopted for the legitimate purpose of protecting privacy and only incidentally infringed on plaintiffs' constitutional rights.

M Restaurants, Inc. v. San Francisco Local Joint Exec. Bd. Culinary etc. Union


Civ. No. 40108


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


124 Cal. App. 3d 666; 177 Cal. Rptr. 690; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2253



October 19, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 27, 1982. Bird, C. J., and Newman, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.

DISPOSITION: The order denying the motion to dissolve the preliminary injunction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant unions challenged the order of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied their motion to dissolve an order enjoining them from threatening and committing acts of intimidation and violence and limiting the numbers and spacing of picketers while picketing at respondent restaurant in violation of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 527.3.


OVERVIEW: Appellant unions picketed respondent restaurant for unfair labor practices. During the picketing, appellants blocked the doorways of the restaurant and intimidated customers. Appellants also threatened respondent's president and manager with death. Thereafter, the trial court enjoined appellants from blocking access or deliveries to respondent and from threatening and or committing acts of intimidation and physical violence on anyone working for respondent. The injunction also limited the number and spacing of picketers. Further, the court found that the limitations as to the number and spacing of picketers placed on appellants were reasonable.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order denying appellant unions' motion to dissolve the preliminary injunction enjoining them from threatening and committing acts of intimidation and violence and limiting the numbers and spacing of picketers while picketing at respondent restaurant because the enjoined acts were illegal and the limitations were reasonable.

 

1480 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Anselmo v. Glendale Unified School Dist.


Civ. No. 61858


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


124 Cal. App. 3d 520; 177 Cal. Rptr. 427; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2240



October 14, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 338586, Thomas T. Johnson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is reversed and the cause remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted respondent minor child a preliminary injunction in an action to compel appellant to admit respondent in public school.


OVERVIEW: Parents and respondent, their minor child, were admitted to the United States on the basis of a tourist visa issued under 8 U.S.C.S. § 1101(a)(15)(B). The parents subsequently bought a home. Appellant school district denied the parents' application to have respondent enrolled in public school on the ground of nonresidency. Parents, on behalf of respondent, filed a complaint to compel admission and for a preliminary injunction compelling admission, which was granted. The court reversed the trial court's judgment. It held that the parents did not meet the residency requirement necessary to enroll respondent in public school. Under Cal. Educ. Code § 48200, respondent's residence was his parent's residence, and under 8 U.S.C.S. § 1101(a)(15)(B), his parent's residence could not be in the United States. The court further held that the residency requirement did not violate equal protection because it applied uniformly to all persons and because it addressed a legitimate interest by reasonably related means.


OUTCOME: The court reversed an injunction compelling appellant school district to enroll respondent minor child in public school. Respondent's parents did not meet residency requirements. Under state law, respondent minor's residence was his parent's residence, and under federal law, his parent's residence could not be in the United States because they had a tourist visa. The court also held that the residency requirement did not violate equal protection.

In re Marriage B.


Civ. No. 59767


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


124 Cal. App. 3d 524; 177 Cal. Rptr. 429; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2241



October 14, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied November 9, 1981, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 9, 1981. Kaus, J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. D 954669, Roberta Ralph, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant husband sought review of the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles (California), which entered an interlocutory judgment in a divorce proceeding that ordered appellant to pay child support for a minor son that was born to appellant and respondent wife during their marriage.


OVERVIEW: The lower court entered an interlocutory judgment in a divorce proceeding and ordered appellant husband to pay child support for a minor son that was born to appellant and respondent wife during their marriage. Appellant argued that, although he was the presumed father of the child under state law, because of the enactment of Cal. Evid. Code § 621, he was not precluded from introducing the results of a blood test, which proved that he was not the biological father of the minor child. The court affirmed the order of the lower court holding that the 2-year statute of limitations contained in § 621 had expired because it had been more that 2 years since the birth of the 12-year-old child. The court further held that appellant's rights under the equal protection clause were not violated by the exclusion of the test results because there had been no unequal treatment of men who sought to introduce similar evidence after the expiration of the statute of limitations and that the admission of such evidence after the running of the statute would contravene the policies behind the enactment of § 621.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court, which entered an interlocutory judgment in a divorce proceeding, and the lower court ordered appellant husband to pay child support for a minor son that was born to appellant and respondent wife during their marriage; appellant was time barred from introducing the result of a blood test, which concluded that he was not the minor child's biological father.

Sawyer v. First City Financial Corp.

Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One

PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 409803, Douglas R. Woodworth and Franklin B. Orfield, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The dismissal of Toronto Dominion Bank of California, Charles Klugherz and William McIntosh is affirmed. The judgment dismissing all of the other parties defendant is reversed. Sawyer et al.) to recover their costs on appeal from the remaining defendants.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants landowners challenged summary judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) for respondents, financial corporation, subsidiaries, and development lender on appellants' suit alleging conspiracy to eliminate respondents' obligations, and intentional interference with contract. This second complaint arose from the sale of a 32-acre plot of land to respondent subsidiary; the first resulted in judgment for all defendants.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed dismissal of respondent development lender and named employees, but reversed dismissal as to all other respondents, financial corporation and subsidiaries. The lawsuits arose out of appellants landowners' sale of 32 acres of land to respondent financial corporation and subsidiaries for development purposes in which appellants subordinated their loan to a subsequent deed of trust which was foreclosed on and shortly resold when development plans and financing fell apart. Appellants brought suit in this instance seeking actual and punitive damages alleging conspiracy to create a sham transaction to eliminate their $ 650,000 purchase price obligation to appellant and intentional interference with contract.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed dismissal of respondent development lender and employees, but reversed dismissal for respondents bank corporation and subsidiaries. The court found error in granting summary judgment on res judicata grounds because appellant's landowners' suit was based on a separate and severable cause of action from earlier litigation. The court found that two named employees were properly dismissed.

Salmon Trollers Marketing Assn. v. Fullerton


Civ. No. 50021


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


124 Cal. App. 3d 291; 177 Cal. Rptr. 362; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2216



October 6, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 2, 1981. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Mendocino County, No. 43160, Arthur B. Broaddus, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Director of Fish and Game of the State of California, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Mendocino County (California), which granted a writ of mandate and an injunction in favor of respondent commercial fishermen. Appellant argued that Cal. Fish & Game Code § 7652 was a constitutional delegation of legislative power to appellant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, the Director of Fish and Game of the State of California, closed the commercial salmon fishing season by emergency regulations adopted under Cal. Fish & Game Code § 7652. The trial court invalidated the emergency closure, and granted a writ of mandate and an injunction in favor of respondent commercial fishermen, all on the ground that § 7652 was an unconstitutional delegation of authority of legislative power to appellant. The court found that appellant filed the emergency regulation prefaced by specific facts constituting the need for immediate action under Cal. Gov't. Code § 11346.1. The court found that § 7652 did not violate the constitutional doctrine of separation of powers in Cal. Const. art. III, § 3, as an improper delegation of legislative function to an executive agency. The court found that adequate standards were set forth to guide appellant as he implemented the basic policy decision already made by the legislature. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and found that § 7652 was not overly broad in its grant of power to appellant and offended neither the separation of powers clause nor the due process clause.


OUTCOME: The court rejected the argument of respondent commercial fishermen that the action of appellant Director of Fish and Game of the State of California was an improper delegation of legislative authority to an executive agency. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court.

 

1484 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Chavez


Crim. No. 21596


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


124 Cal. App. 3d 215; 177 Cal. Rptr. 306; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2210



October 5, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] On November 3, 1981, the opinion was modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Napa County, No. 4999, Philip A. Champlin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed convictions entered in the Superior Court of Napa County (California) for the offenses of assault with intent to commit rape, burglary, and assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury, all entered upon negotiated pleas after defendant was found competent to stand trial. Defendant alleged he was denied due process when the trial court failed to appoint an interpreter for him at any stage of the prosecution.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a Spanish-speaking Mexican national, was convicted of assault with intent to commit rape and assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury. Defendant claimed he was denied due process when the trial court failed to appoint an interpreter at all stages of the prosecution. The court found that a non-English-speaking defendant's right to an interpreter was guaranteed by Cal. Const. art. I, § 14. The court found that defendant's attorney functioned as an interpreter on significant occasions, but that the statutory requirement of an oath was ignored on all but one of them. The court held that although it had been held that a non-English-speaking defendant's U.S. Const. amend. XIV rights were satisfied if he was represented by counsel who was qualified to act as his interpreter, in California, with the right to an interpreter having been guaranteed in the Constitution, the right to an interpreter could not validly be waived without an affirmative showing on the record of a waiver which was intelligent and voluntary on the part of the defendant. The court found that no showing of waiver appeared in the record and reversed the judgments of conviction.


OUTCOME: The court determined that defendant was denied his due process rights when the trial court failed to appoint an interpreter at all stages of the proceedings. The court reversed defendant's convictions for assault with intent to commit rape and assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury, but refused to address defendant's claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
