Salinero v. Pon


Civ. No. 46752


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


124 Cal. App. 3d 120; 177 Cal. Rptr. 204; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2203



October 1, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 30, 1981, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 9, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 471984-9, Spurgeon Avakian, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant window washer challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which granted summary judgment and dismissed appellant's personal injury action against respondent architect, and which granted a subsequent motion for nonsuit in favor of respondent building owners.


OVERVIEW: Appellant window washer brought a personal injury action against respondent building owners, who filed a cross-complaint against respondent architect. The trial court granted summary judgment to respondent architect because the action was barred by the statute of limitations under Cal. Civ. Proc. § 337.1, and granted respondent building owners' motion for nonsuit. The court affirmed, because even though appellant could argue the unconstitutionality of § 337.1 because the ruling adversely affected appellant's right to pursue a cause of action against respondent architect, § 337.1 promoted a legitimate state interest by protecting contractors from uncertain future liability, and a rational basis existed for the classifications created by § 337.1. The court held that Cal. Labor Code § 6304.5 stated that statutes proposed by appellant were not admissible in any third party actions by an employee. The court held that negligence per se had not been established because there was no showing of unsatisfactory maintenance of the building by respondent building owners, nor did the peculiar risk doctrine apply because the accident resulted from the "collateral negligence" of a coworker.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, which granted summary judgment to respondent architect, because the classification for purposes of statute of limitations was constitutional, and which granted respondent building owners' motion for nonsuit, because respondent building owners were not negligent per se, and the peculiar risk doctrine did not apply in the personal injury action brought by appellant window washer.

 

1486 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


County of Inyo v. City of L.A.


Civ. No. 13886


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


124 Cal. App. 3d 1; 177 Cal. Rptr. 479; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2193; 11 ELR 21093



September 30, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 28, 1981. Evans, Acting P. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted but for reasons other than those proposed. Respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 9, 1981.



DISPOSITION: We hold that the city's return to the writ of mandate issued as a result of our June 1973 decision fails to comply with the writ. This court has continuing jurisdiction to enforce the writ until it is fully satisfied. ( Code Civ. Proc., § 1097; County of Inyo v. City of Los Angeles, supra, 61 Cal.App.3d at p. 95.) The writ is not discharged but remains in force; the City of Los Angeles and its department of water and power are directed to take reasonably expeditious action to comply with it. Interim restraints upon the rate of extraction of groundwater and upon the decrease of water supplied to Owens Valley uses will remain in effect.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county objected to a second environmental impact report (EIR) submitted by respondent city by way of a return to the writ, which was ordered by the court under the California Environmental Quality Act in previous litigation and which directed respondent to prepare an EIR covering its extraction of subsurface water in the Owens Valley.


OVERVIEW: In previous litigation concerning respondent city's extraction of subsurface waters from the Owens Valley, the court issued a writ of mandate requiring respondent to submit an environmental impact report (EIR) in compliance with the California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA). Respondent's first EIR was rejected as not being in compliance with CEQA, and respondent submitted a second EIR by way of a return to the writ, to which petitioner county objected on the grounds that it failed to provide an accurate, stable, and finite project description, and that it failed to provide reasonable alternatives to the project. The court sustained petitioner's objections and held that where the EIR removed the availability of surface water from examination, it failed to fulfill the mandate of the court to provide an informed analysis of the project and its impact. The court also held that the establishment of a "no-project" alternative, which was predicated upon conditions different than those which preceded the project, distorted the relation of the project to the export of water to respondent. Thus, respondent failed to comply with the previously issued writ, and it remained in force.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the objections of petitioner county to the second environmental impact report (EIR) submitted by respondent city, because the court found that the EIR failed to provide an informed assessment of the project and its impact where it removed the availability of surface water from examination and because the EIR did not include a genuine "no-project" alternative. Thus, the writ issued previously by the court was not discharged.

 

1487 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


California Medical Assn. v. Lackner


Civ. No. 18214


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


124 Cal. App. 3d 28; 177 Cal. Rptr. 188; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2194



September 30, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 268099, Lawrence K. Karlton, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a medical association and physicians, appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which upheld defendant state health services' regulations under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1250, which established procedures for securing informed consent for human sterilization.


OVERVIEW: Defendant state health services promulgated regulations which established procedures for securing informed consent for human sterilization in "acute care" hospitals under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 1250. The lower court denied plaintiffs', a medical association and physicians, preliminary injunction and motion for summary judgment and the regulations took effect. The court affirmed and held that defendant's regulations were reasonable. The court found that although there was no statute that expressly stated that defendant was permitted to adopt informed consent regulations, defendant was statutorily recognized as having competency to regulate sterilization operations. The court held that the regulations were requirements relating to the patient's mental condition and that their purpose was to assure that a patient had the requisite comprehension of the operation, that Cal. Health & Safety Code §§ 1275, 1276, and 1294 authorized defendant to adopt reasonable regulations regarding unnecessary operations, and that the sections provided for the adoption of regulations consistent with the legislative concerns regarding sterilization operations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's order, which upheld defendant state health services' regulations for securing informed consent for sterilizations, and held that the regulations reasonably implemented defendant's regulatory authority over unnecessary operations and were requirements relating to the patient's mental condition.

 

1488 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Leek v. Washington Unified School Dist.


Civ. No. 19665


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


124 Cal. App. 3d 43; 177 Cal. Rptr. 196; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2195



September 30, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 27, 1981, and appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied December 23, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Yolo County, No. 40945, Harry A. Ackley, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, teachers and other professionals, sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Yolo County (California), which sustained a demurrer without leave to amend based on the failure to exhaust administrative remedies under the Educational Employment Relations Act, Cal. Gov't Code §§ 3540-3549.3, and dismissed the action, in favor of respondent bargaining representative.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, teachers and other professionals, contested a provision in a collective bargaining agreement that required professionals who were not members of respondent bargaining representative to either become a member or to pay a representation fee. Appellants claimed that their substantive grievances did not constitute unfair practices as defined by the Educational Employment Relations Act (Act), Cal. Gov't Code §§ 3540-3549.3, and, therefore, were beyond the Public Employment Relations Board's (board) jurisdiction. The court sustained the demurrer and affirmed the dismissal of the action. The court held that the board was not limited to only investigating charges that were defined as "unlawful" under Cal. Gov't Code §§ 3543.5, 3543.6, but had the power to make determinations with respect to charges or violations that were necessary to effectuate the Act's policies under Cal. Gov't Code § 3541.3(h)(i),(j),(n). The court found that appellants were still required to exhaust administrative remedies prior to a constitutional challenge because there was a reasonable probability a board ruling on the nonconstitutional issues would obviate the necessity of a constitutional challenge.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the demurrer without leave to amend and the dismissed of the action, in favor of respondent bargaining representative. The court held that pursuant to the Education Employment Relations Act, the Public Employment Relations Board was invested with the initial jurisdiction over remedies to grievances that were alleged by appellants, teachers and other professionals.

 

1489 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Burkhart v. Dep't of Motor Vehicles


Civ. No. 5968


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


124 Cal. App. 3d 99; 177 Cal. Rptr. 175; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2201



September 30, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Tulare County, No. 95912, David L. Allen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and remanded for further proceedings in accordance with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Department of Motor Vehicles sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Tulare County (California), which granted respondent licensee a preemptory writ of mandate which ordered appellant to reissue respondent's suspended driver's license.


OVERVIEW: Upon the arrest of respondent licensee the arresting officer pursuant to Cal. Veh. Code § 13353, executed a sworn statement that he had reasonable cause to believe that respondent had been driving under the influence of intoxicating liquor. Respondent's driver's license was suspended at a hearing held by appellant Department of Motor Vehicles. At the hearing the officer's statement was entered into evidence dispite respondent's conflicting evidence. Respondent was granted a peremptory writ of mandate by the lower court which ordered appellant to reinstate respondent's driver's license. The lower court stated that the officer's hearsay statement was not sufficient prima facie evidence of any matter as to which there was conflicting evidence. The court reversed and remanded holding that the officer's statement pursuant to § 13353, was an official record and within the meaning of Cal. Veh. Code § 14108, and therefore admissible at appellant's hearing. The court held that neither the Due Process Clause of the state nor the federal Constitutions was violated by the statutory hearing scheme provided for in Cal. Veh. Code §14100 et. seq.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's preemptory writ of mandate which ordered that appellant Department of Motor Vehicles reissue respondent licensee's suspended license because the arresting officer's sworn statement was an official record under the state's vehicle code. The court held that the hearing afforded to respondent under the applicable statutory scheme did not violate the Due Process Clause of the state and federal Constitutions.

 

1490 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


County of Los Angeles v. Superior Court


Civ. Nos. 60619, 60620, 60661, 60662


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


123 Cal. App. 3d 988; 177 Cal. Rptr. 70; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2181



September 29, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 9, 1981, and the petition of real parties in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 27, 1981. Kaus, J., did not participate therein.



DISPOSITION: Let peremptory writs issue commanding respondent court in the within cases to vacate its orders vacating the judgments of paternity and support. The alternative writs are discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county sought a writ of mandate after respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) vacated judgments for paternity and child support which were made pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §11476.1 because real party in interest parents alleged that they had not been advised of or knowingly waived their constitutional rights prior to signing the underlying agreement which formed the basis for the judgment.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest parents were parties to paternity and support judgments based on out of court agreements made pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 11476.1. After their judgment were final, a state appellate court determined that these type of agreements were unconstitutional, but declined to address the issue of retroactivity. Therefore the real parties in interest did not pay arguing that their judgments were unconstitutional. Respondent trial court vacated the judgments and petitioner county sought a writ of mandate. On appeal, the court held that in some cases familial relationships had been established by final judgments rendered pursuant to the statutory procedure prior to the appellate court decision. The court held that there was a strong public interest in the finality of such judgments and therefore, concluded that the appellate court decision did not retroactively invalidate the judgments of paternity and child support in the instant cases.


OUTCOME: The court issued the peremptory writs which commanded respondent trial court to vacate its orders vacating the judgments of paternity and support because familial relationships had been established by final judgments rendered. Therefore, there was a strong public interest in the finality of such judgments and thus, the court would not apply the appellate court decision retroactively to invalidate the judgments.

 

1491 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Diachenko v. State of California


Civ. No. 24306


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


123 Cal. App. 3d 932; 177 Cal. Rptr. 164; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2174



September 25, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 449491, Gerald J. Lewis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of dismissal is reversed and the matter remanded for further proceedings, including determination of the merits of the motion to certify as a class action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant heir sought review of the order of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which dismissed his complaint after granting respondent State of California's demurrer in an action seeking recovery for appellant and others similarly situated whose share of escheated funds was reduced by interest on the recovered inheritance tax under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 14211 and Cal. Code Regs. tit. 18, § 14211.


OVERVIEW: The cash assets of an intestate decedent escheated to, and were deposited with, respondent state under Cal. Prob. Code § 231 and Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1313. When a previously unknown heir successfully petitioned for a share of these funds, the court order deducted inheritance taxes and interest under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 14211 and Cal. Code Regs. tit. 18, § 14211. Appellant heir sought recovery of the interest for himself and all others similarly situated. The trial court granted respondent's demurrer and dismissed the complaint. On appeal, the court reversed because there was no rational purpose for imposing interest on funds that were under respondent's control and generating income for it where respondent state was not deprived of either the beneficial use or income from monies properly owed to it. The court held that the term interest in Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 14211 was the same as in Cal. Civ. Code § 1915. The court held that appellant had not impermissibly split his cause of action because the suit was not trivial, the issue could not have been foreseen, and the motion to certify as a class action should have been determined in the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that dismissed appellant heir's complaint based upon the grant of respondent state's demurrer where appellant sought recovery from funds escheated to respondent from an intestate decedent. The court found that respondent state improperly reduced the recovery by deducting interest on the inheritance taxes it withheld from the heir's award.

Johnson v. Department of Social Services


Civ. No. 20184


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


123 Cal. App. 3d 878; 177 Cal. Rptr. 49; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2168



September 24, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 18, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 283801, Sheldon H. Grossfeld, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants day care operators challenged an order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied them declaratory relief and upheld the constitutionality of Cal. Admin. Code tit. 22, §31239 promulgated by the Department of Social Services.


OVERVIEW: Appellants day care operators wished to contract with parents to administer discipline to children in their charge which would include corporal punishment. Cal. Admin. Code tit. 22, § 31239 prohibited such discipline. Appellants challenged respondent Department of Social Services policy decision to prohibit corporal punishment in licensed day care centers. The trial court upheld the constitutionality of § 31239. The court affirmed and held that § 31239 neither violated equal protection nor infringed upon a fundamental right. The court concluded that parents of children in day nurseries were not "similarly situated" to parents of children in public schools for purposes of the equal protection argument asserted by appellants. The court ruled that the state had an interest in protecting the health and safety of a child from parental discipline decisions which could jeopardize that child's health and safety.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a decision to uphold the constitutionality of a regulation prohibiting corporal punishment in licensed day care centers because it did not violate equal protection or a fundamental right, and the state had an interest to protect the health and safety of children.

California State Employees' Assn. v. Cory


Civ. Nos. 20487, 20433


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


123 Cal. App. 3d 888; 176 Cal. Rptr. 904; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2169



September 24, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application in No. 20433 for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 14, 1982. Bird, C. J., and Kaus, J., did not participate therein. Taylor, J., * Caldecott, J * and Newsom, J., * participated therein. Broussard, Acting C. J., Mosk, J., and Newsom, J., * were of the opinion that the application should be granted.


* Assigned by the Acting Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writs of mandate are discharged and the petitions are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee representative associations sought a writ of mandamus compelling respondent state controller to pay interest on lump sum payments allocated by the state's legislature to employees petitioners represented.


OVERVIEW: Subsequent to a finding upholding the constitutional validity of Cal. S. Bill 91 (1979), legislation granting certain state employees lumps sum salary payments, petitioner employees representative associations filed for a writ of mandate to compel payment of judgment interest on the salaries to which the employees were entitled. However, respondent state controller contended that he was unable to pay such interest. The court acknowledged that petitioners were entitled to an award of prejudgment interest. However, the court held that the fact that Cal. S. Bill. 91 contained no provision allowing for the payment of judgment interest, combined with state law restricting the use of other state funds barred respondent from paying such an award. In so holding, the court followed the weight of caselaw establishing that a writ of mandate would not issue to compel a state officer to perform an act the officer was not permitted to perform. Thus, the court denied petitioners' applications.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner employee representative associations' application for a writ of mandate compelling the payment of interest on lumps sum salary payments allocated by the state legislature, as the legislature did not provide for such interest payments.

 

1494 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Los Angeles v. Amber Theatres, Inc.


Civ. No. 60327.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


123 Cal. App. 3d 715; 176 Cal. Rptr. 850; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2153



September 21, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. Nov. 19, 1981, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 253113, Jerry Pacht, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied appellant's request to enjoin respondents, adult bookstore operators, from operating their business, which appellant alleged involved the operation of a penny arcade in violation of Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code § 12.16. Appellant also sought review of the trial court's preliminary injunction restraining arrests for violation of § 12.16.


OVERVIEW: Respondents, adult bookstore operators, operated motion picture preview booths on the premises of their business. Appellant's action seeking to enjoin the operation of respondents' business on the ground that it violated Los Angeles, Cal., Mun. Code § 12.16's prohibition against penny arcades was denied by the trial court. The trial court issued a preliminary injunction restraining any arrests for violations of the ordinance. On appeal, the court affirmed both orders because the trial court did not abuse its discretion in considering which party would suffer the greater injury and which party would probably prevail on the merits. The court found that the trial court properly preserved the status quo until factual issues about whether respondents violated the ordinance could be resolved. The trial court orders were proper because there was no evidence of actual interim harm to the public which would justify invoking the injunction to enjoin the business, but potential abridgment of respondents' U.S. Const. amend. I free speech rights warranted injunctive relief against enforcement of the ordinance.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders, holding that the trial court did not abuse its discretion by refusing to enjoin respondents, adult bookstore operators, from continuing their business or by prohibiting future arrests for violations of the penny arcade ordinance because it was unclear whether respondents violated the ordinance and enforcement of the ordinance could abridge respondents' First Amendment rights and irreparably injure their business.

 

1495 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Paula P.


Civ. No. 59511


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


123 Cal. App. 3d 734; 176 Cal. Rptr. 708; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2154



September 14, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified September 25, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. A 6925, Peter C. Dowler, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: Judgment of trial court is affirmed in all respects.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother challenged the order of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which freed her minor child from her custody and control under Cal. Civ. Code § 232 because § 232 was vague, there was not substantial evidence to grant the petition, and the wrong standard of proof was applied. Petitioners foster parents appealed the denial of their petition, alleging abandonment, disability, and appellant's inability to provide a home.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners foster parents to appellant's minor child filed a petition for freedom from parental custody and control pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 232. Previously, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. § 600 the minor was adjudicated dependent because appellant was a heroin addict and the minor was born addicted to heroin. The court found that § 232 was not unconstitutionally vague because the words "cruel or neglectful" had been defined in past cases, encyclopedias, and law dictionaries and the definitions were understandable and definite. The court determined that the decision of the trial court, when supported by substantial evidence, was binding upon the court. The court reasoned that because the minor child had never known a home with appellant, and appellant never attempted to see the child, the trial judge properly found appellant neglected the minor from birth. Last, the court held the proper standard of proof to be applied in a § 232 proceeding was a showing of clear and convincing evidence, as used by the trial court. Thus, the court affirmed the judgment of the trial court and did not examine petitioners' contentions because they would not affect the outcome.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which freed appellant mother's minor child from her custody and control. The court held that the statute was not unconstitutionally vague, there was substantial evidence to make the finding, and that clear and convincing evidence was the correct standard of proof. The court did not examine petitioners foster parents allegations, as they did not affect the case's outcome.

People v. Roberts


Crim. No. 11896


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


123 Cal. App. 3d 684; 177 Cal. Rptr. 11; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2150



September 10, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 28, 1981, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 18, 1981. Bird, C. J., Newman, J., and Broussard, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CR 29954, Earl Ben Gilliam, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of commitment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) that granted the state's petition to extend his commitment in a state hospital as a mentally disordered sex offender pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6316.2.


OVERVIEW: Defendant picked up a teenage girl and raped her. When the girl began to scream, defendant either strangled or electrocuted her. Defendant pled guilty to murder and was found to be a mentally disordered sex offender. Defendant was committed to a state hospital pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6300 et seq. When his term of commitment was set to expire, the state filed a petition of extension. The trial court extended defendant's commitment period for two years because it found that defendant presented a substantial danger of harm. Defendant contended Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 6316.2(j) was unconstitutional. On appeal, the court affirmed the decision. The extension of time provided in § 6316.2 came only after a trial in which defendant was afforded all his constitutional rights. The extension was not for an indefinite period, and defendant was returned to the state hospital, not prison.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision because the statute, which provided for an extension of commitment time, was constitutional. Defendant was afforded a trial before the trial court granted the state's petition for extension. The extension was for two years, not an indefinite period of time as alleged by defendant.

 

1497 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


McCourtney v. Cory


Civ. No. 58869


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


123 Cal. App. 3d 431; 176 Cal. Rptr. 639; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2066



September 2, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 22, 1981, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 13, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C211921 Alfred L. Margolis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant state controller sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles (California), which granted plaintiff surviving spouses a declaratory judgment to require payment for life of their allowances, even though the provisions of Judges' Retirement Law, Cal. Gov't Code § 75000 et. seq., that authorized their allowances had specified termination of such allowances upon plaintiffs' remarriage.


OVERVIEW: In the proceeding below, plaintiff surviving spouses had obtained a declaratory judgment in their favor on the basis of their argument that the provisions of the Judges' Retirement Law, Cal. Gov't Code § 75000 et. seq., which terminated plaintiffs' allowances upon remarriage constituted an invalid restraint or marriage and a denial of constitutional equal protection and due process protections. On review, the court disagreed and reversed. The court found that the prospective loss of a spousal allowance upon the remarriage of the surviving partner was a reasonable regulation that did not interfere, significantly or legally, with the right to marry. The court also rejected plaintiffs' equal protection and due process arguments because the legislative classification in question met the test of reasonableness and bore a rational relation to a constitutionally permissible objective. The court concluded by acknowledging that the termination of the spousal allowance was appropriate where the bulk of California legislation dealing with retirement benefits for public employees terminated allowance for individuals in plaintiffs' position upon the spouses' death or remarriage.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the declaratory judgment for plaintiff surviving spouses, finding that plaintiffs' challenge to the provisions of the Judges' Retirement Law that terminated plaintiffs' allowances upon their remarriage was meritless where the subject legislative enactments were not an invalid restraint on marriage or a violation of plaintiffs' equal protection or due process rights.

Armistead v. State Personnel Bd.


Civ. No. 20602


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


124 Cal. App. 3d 61; 177 Cal. Rptr. 7; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2197



September 2, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 28, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 293597, Chris Papas, Judge. +


+ Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state employee sought review from a Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) judgment sustaining the demurrer of respondents state personnel board and others to appellant's complaint requesting reinstatement and reversal of respondents' termination of appellant under Cal. Gov't. Code § 19503.


OVERVIEW: Appellant state employee was terminated by respondents state personnel board and others pursuant to a provision of Cal. Gov't. Code § 19503 mandating automatic resignation when a state employee was absent without leave for five consecutive working days. After failing to obtain reinstatement, appellant filed suit requesting that respondents' decision be set aside and that he be reinstated with back pay. The lower court sustained respondents' demurrer without leave to amend because of appellant's failure to state a cause of action. Appellant sought review. The court found that appellant's return to his place of employment for 45 minutes without returning to work merely to avoid application of Cal. Gov't. Code § 19503 was insufficient to prevent the automatic statutory application. Further, respondents had not misinterpreted the statute. Finally, the statute was constitutional and because it was automatic no mitigation was permissible under the circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment, holding that under the clear provisions of the applicable statute appellant's brief return to his place of work could not prevent the invocation of the automatic resignation statutory provision, particularly where he had sufficient notice of them.

Jones v. Superior Court


Civ. Nos. 52059, 52095


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


123 Cal. App. 3d 160; 176 Cal. Rptr. 430; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2103



August 28, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied September 25, 1981, and the opinion was modified to read as printed above. Petitioners' applications for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied October 28, 1981. Bird, C. J., and Kaus, J., were of the opinion that the applications should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writs are ordered discharged and the petitions for peremptory writ are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners challenged the denial by respondent, the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), of their Cal. Penal Code § 995 motions to dismiss the special circumstances allegations against them and brought petitions for writs of mandamus and/or prohibition under Cal. Penal Code § 999a.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners were charged with first degree murder, Cal. Penal Code § 187. The evidence at the preliminary hearing demonstrated that the victim met her death execution style. The real party in interest, the people, sought imposition of the death penalty and alleged four special circumstances pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17). Petitioners challenged respondent superior court's denial of their motions to dismiss the special circumstances allegations. The court concluded that it would torture the statute to incorporate felony-murder concepts into the Cal. Penal Code § 190.2 (a)(17) phrase "while engaged in," but the phrase "immediate flight after" commission of the sex acts should not be held inapplicable. Therefore, the alternative writs were ordered discharged, and the petitions for peremptory writ were denied.


OUTCOME: The alternative writs were ordered discharged and the petitions for peremptory writ were denied because the real party in interest, the people, provided some rational ground for assuming the possibility that the alleged acts were committed by the petitioners.

Hooper v. Deukmejian


Civ. No. 50576


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


122 Cal. App. 3d 987; 176 Cal. Rptr. 569; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2140



August 26, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 25, 1981, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 21, 1981.

DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff class representative appealed the judgment of Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which sustained defendant state attorney general's and defendant California State Personnel Board's demurrer to and dismissed plaintiff's complaint, which alleged that plaintiff class was entitled to the benefits of marijuana reform legislation.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff class representative filed an action on behalf of individuals who were convicted of maintaining a place for the sale or use of a narcotic when the offense was designated or stipulated to be a lesser-included offense of possession of marijuana. The complaint alleged that the benefits of the marijuana reform legislation (legislation) should apply to plaintiff class and that the contrary policies of defendant state attorney general and defendant California State Personnel Board were unconstitutional. The trial court dismissed plaintiff's complaint. On appeal, the court initially noted that the overall purpose of the legislation was to minimize the lingering social stigma flowing from a relatively minor form of criminal activity. The court also observed that violations of former Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11557 were not covered by the legislation. The court concluded that plaintiff stated a cause of action based upon denial of equal protection because defendants did not provide a rational basis for the classifications created by the legislation. The court reversed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that dismissed the complaint of plaintiff class representative, which alleged that the class was entitled to the benefits of marijuana reform legislation, because the court found that plaintiff's complaint stated a cause of action based on denial of equal protection.

In re Steven S.


Civ. No. 60832


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


122 Cal. App. 3d 683; 176 Cal. Rptr. 195; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2061



August 17, 1981

DISPOSITION: The order appealed from is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, father of a minor who was a ward of the juvenile court, sought review of a decision from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), ordering appellant to repay the costs of maintaining the minor in various county facilities, pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 903. Appellant raised constitutional objections and contended that the superior court failed to determine the financial status of appellant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant, father of a minor who was a ward of the juvenile court, was ordered to reimburse respondent county for the aggregate amount owing and for the ongoing charges of maintaining the minor in various county facilities. The court affirmed the order from the trial court. Cal. Welf. & Inst. The court found that appellant made no claim that he was unable to effect reimbursement and that the only question raised was the rate at which such reimbursement should have occurred, and the aggregate amount owing and the ongoing charge was reduced on respondent's recommendation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order requiring appellant, father of a minor who was a ward of the juvenile court, to repay the county for the costs of maintaining the minor in various county facilities and for ongoing charges. The statute authorizing reimbursement was valid because it was based on the common law obligations of parents to support their minor children.

 

1502 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Wilcox v. Enstad


Civ. No. 6303


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


122 Cal. App. 3d 641; 176 Cal. Rptr. 560; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2057



August 14, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a writ of mandate issue directing the respondent clerk to issue a certificate of insufficiency under section 27215, subdivision (d), and take no further action in processing an election to recall Wilcox on the basis of the petitions filed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner school trustee filed a petition for writ of mandate to compel respondent county clerk to take no further action on a recall petition brought by real parties in interest, district residents, to remove petitioner from office in an original proceeding.


OVERVIEW: Real parties in interest, district residents, filed a recall petition to remove petitioner school trustee from office. Respondent county clerk informed real parties in interest that the petition contained less than the 90 percent of the number of valid signatures required to qualify under Cal. Elec. Code § 27215 and issued a certificate of insufficiency, but informed real parties in interest that they might submit supplemental petitions. Petitioner filed a petition for writ of mandate. The court granted petitioner's application for writ of mandate because respondent county clerk was under a mandatory duty to take no further action on the recall petition, as the random-sample verification procedure showed less than 90 percent of the number of valid signatures required, and § 27215 did not provide for supplementation. The court held that fewer than 90 percent of the number of valid names required were presented to the clerk and the policies favoring the right of recall did not mean that valid procedural requirements could be ignored. The court directed respondent to issue a certificate of insufficiency under § 27215(d).


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner school trustee's petition for writ of mandate because respondent county clerk was under mandatory duty to issue a certificate of insufficiency to a petition filed by real parties in interest, district residents, for recall, and was not authorized to provide supplementation.

 

1503 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Worsley v. Municipal Court


Civ. No. 60707


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


122 Cal. App. 3d 409; 176 Cal. Rptr. 324; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2036



August 10, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied August 27, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C-316375, Richard Alfonso Ibanez, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state, here the real party in interest, appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which struck respondent's conviction of driving under the influence of alcohol in violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 23102(a), because the docket, which used a rubber stamp, inadequately reflected respondent's waiver of his constitutional rights.


OVERVIEW: Respondent was charged with driving under the influence, and it was alleged that he had a prior conviction for the same offense. Respondent ultimately entered a guilty plea and admitted the prior conviction. The following year, respondent filed a motion to strike the second conviction, and the motion was denied. Upon respondent's petition for a writ of mandate, the superior court held that the earlier conviction was unconstitutional because the docket sheet did not reflect that respondent had expressly and explicitly waived his constitutional rights where there was a series of rubber-stamped memorializations. The state, here the real party in interest, appealed. The court reversed, holding that the docket was constitutionally adequate as it showed that respondent had waived his right to counsel, had been informed of his legal rights, and had entered a plea of not guilty. The state's challenge to the constitutionality of Cal. Veh. Code § 23102.2 was moot as purely hypothetical. Finally, the court held that § 23102.2(b) was presumptively valid in the absence of a clear demonstration that the state's burden of proof required was excessively high.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the superior court's judgment striking respondent's prior conviction for driving under the influence of alcohol because respondent's express waiver of his constitutional rights was sufficiently memorialized despite the fact that a rubber stamp was used.

 

1504 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Barme v. Wood


Civ. No. 58673.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


122 Cal. App. 3d 395; 176 Cal. Rptr. 42; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2035



August 7, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Oct. 22, 1981 (L.A. 31484)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SOC 50644, Rosemary M. Dunbar, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Intervenor city appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted summary judgment to defendants, doctors, corporations, and related entities in a medical malpractice suit by intervenor's employee, plaintiff husband, because recovery was precluded by Cal. Civ. Code § 3333.1(b).


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs, husband and wife, sought damages for medical malpractice from defendants, doctors, corporations, and related entities. Intervenor city, plaintiff husband's employer, sought reimbursement for expenses already paid on behalf of plaintiff husband and future expenses. The trial court granted defendants' motions for summary judgment as to the complaint in intervention because Cal. Civ. Code § 3333.1(b) foreclosed recovery by collateral sources from the tortfeasors. On intervenor's appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that § 3333.1(b) ended the subrogation rights conferred by statutory and common law in all actions brought by a party injured by the negligence of a health provider. The court found that the statute did not violate equal protection guarantees because there was a rational relationship between the classification, medical malpractice parties, and the legislative purpose, to contain health care costs. The court further held that there was no due process violation because the statute's effect, barring recovery by collateral sources from the tortfeasor, was not demonstrably arbitrary or offensive to due process of law.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of defendants, doctors, corporations, and related entities, in intervenor city's action to recover expenses disbursed on behalf of plaintiff husband, intervenor's employee, in plaintiff husband's medical malpractice action because a state statute foreclosed recovery by collateral sources against the tortfeasors and the statute did not violate equal protection or due process guarantees.

 

1505 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Kupka v. Board of Administration


Civ. No. 23711


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


122 Cal. App. 3d 791; 176 Cal. Rptr. 214; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2125



August 5, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied September 1, 1981, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 2, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. 194709, Charles Bierschbach, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former officer sought review from the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), in which his petition for writ of mandate, to review a decision of respondent retirement system denying his application for disability pension, was denied. Appellant sought relief under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 473 alleging mistake and excusable neglect.


OVERVIEW: Appellant former officer of the California Highway Patrol filed an application for disability pension with respondent public employees' retirement system (PERS) which PERS denied. Appellant then sought new counsel and, due to a misunderstanding, the writ was filed after the deadline expired. Appellant sought a petition for writ of mandate to review the decision of PERS. Realizing the petition was filed beyond the applicable statute of limitations, appellant did not wait for PERS to invoke the bar of limitations but instead moved for relief under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 473. The trial court denied the motion and judgment was entered accordingly. The court affirmed the denial of the motion, holding that appellant's failure to discover the cause of action did not excuse the untimely filing where the respondent did not fraudulently conceal the cause of action. The showing of mistake or excusable neglect by itself was not sufficient to overcome the bar of limitations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of the motion and judgment entered in favor of respondents because appellant had been afforded due process of law, and application of the statute of limitations to bar the petition was not unconstitutional.

 

1506 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Talamantez v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 24494


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


122 Cal. App. 3d 629; 176 Cal. Rptr. 800; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 2056



July 29, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 22, 1981. Bird, C. J., and Kaus, J., were of the opinion that the application should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The petition for a writ of prohibition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of prohibition pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 995 to compel respondent Superior Court, San Diego County (California), to dismiss the charges of special circumstances in his first degree murder case.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner challenged the procedure by which special circumstances allegations were added by amendment to the information in his first degree murder case after the preliminary hearing was held. The special circumstances alleged were Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(16), intentional killing because of race; Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(ii), murder while engaged in the commission of kidnapping; and Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(18), intentional torture murder. The court held that the facts substantiated the special circumstances. Testimony showed a deliberate hunt for a black victim, a completely unmotivated murder, with prolonged beating, infliction of brutal injuries, and torture. The court held that it was not error to add the special circumstances after the preliminary hearing and that petitioner was not prejudiced in any way because his counsel admitted that he anticipated that the matter would be a death penalty case. The court denied the writ of prohibition.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that there was sufficient evidence to support the state's amendment to the information of special circumstances in petitioner's first degree murder case. The court denied the writ of prohibition.

 

1507 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Darrell P.


Civ. No. 20131


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


121 Cal. App. 3d 916; 175 Cal. Rptr. 682; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1994



July 24, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied October 14, 1981. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Yolo County, No. 6935, James F. Roach, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order of commitment) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant juvenile sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Yolo County (California), that committed him to the California Youth Authority, after a rehearing of an order of a referee that committed appellant to the Youth Authority but suspended the commitment and placed him in the custody of his parents. Appellant contended it was error to have the rehearing without having obtained the prior hearing's transcript.


OVERVIEW: Appellant juvenile challenged the trial court's order that committed him to the custody of the California Youth Authority after a rehearing on the trial court's volition of a prior hearing, conducted by a referee that committed appellant to the Youth Authority but suspended the committal and placed appellant in the custody of his parents. Appellant asserted that the trial court erred in the order of a rehearing without first obtaining a copy of a transcript of the hearing conducted by the referee. The court affirmed and found that Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 253 did not require the trial court to obtain and read a copy of a transcript prior to its order for a rehearing, and Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 347 did not require that hearings conducted by referees were to be transcribed. The court held that to require a reading of a transcript prior to an order for a rehearing would place beyond the trial court's power to grant a rehearing when no transcript was available. The court found that when the trial court ordered a rehearing, appellant was entitled to a hearing de novo before the trial court and that the referee's decision was discarded in favor of independent judicial determination.


OUTCOME: An order of committal after a rehearing of a previous order was affirmed because the statute did not require the trial court to read a transcript of a prior hearing before it ordered a rehearing, and the statute did not require hearings conducted by a referee to be transcribed. The court held that appellant juvenile was entitled to a de novo hearing, and that the referee's decision was to be discarded in favor of judicial determinations.

 

1508 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Marshall


Crim. No. 11801.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


121 Cal. App. 3d 627; 175 Cal. Rptr. 497; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1966



July 16, 1981, As modified July 29, 1981


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Oct. 7, 1981 (Crim. 22267)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CRN 5703, J. Morgan Lester, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which convicted defendant of robbery and murder in the first degree under the presence of special circumstances, and which found that defendant personally used a firearm to commit both crimes.


OVERVIEW: Defendant killed a sporting goods store owner during a robbery and was of convicted robbery and murder in the first degree under the presence of special circumstances. The trial court further found that defendant used a firearm to commit both crimes. He was sentenced to life in prison and sought review. He primarily contended that the trial court erred when it admitted previously recorded testimony of an unavailable witness, failed to sua sponte instruct the jury on the limited use of evidence, and concluded that felony murder could establish both first-degree murder and the special circumstance needed for the death penalty. The court affirmed. It found that the previously recorded testimony of the unavailable witness was admissible, because prior testimony was admissible under Cal. Evid. Code § 1291, and because the state established reasonable diligence when it was unable to procure the witness. The trial court had no duty to sua sponte instruct the jury on the limited use of evidence. Finally, defendant's claim pertaining to the death penalty was not relevant, because he was not sentenced to death.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Defendant's convictions for robbery and murder in the first degree under the presence of special circumstances were upheld, because the trial court did not commit prejudicial error.

Ford Dealers Asso. v. Department of Motor Vehicles

OVERVIEW: Plaintiff dealers' associations brought an action against defendants, state department of motor vehicles (DMV) and others, for declaratory and injunctive relief that seven regulations promulgated by defendant DMV were beyond defendant's power to adopt and, in some instances, were repugnant to the state and federal constitutions. The trial court sustained the challenge. On appeal, the court affirmed, finding that five of the regulations contested were defective. OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Plaintiff dealers' associations were entitled to declaratory and injunctive relief against defendants, state department of motor vehicles (DMV) and others, because five regulations promulgated by defendant DMV were defective, in that they exceeded the scope of the penal statute or were unconstitutionally vague.

Fein v. Permanente Medical Group


Civ. No. 18349.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


121 Cal. App. 3d 135; 175 Cal. Rptr. 177; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1921



June 30, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Sept. 10, 1981 (See 38 Cal. 3d 137)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 265659, Michael J. Virga, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff patient challenged the order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which reduced the jury's award of damages in his suit alleging medical malpractice by defendant health care provider when its employees initially diagnosed plaintiff's heart attack as muscle spasm.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff patient suffered chest pains and was treated by employees of defendant health care provider for muscle spasms. A later examination showed that plaintiff had suffered a heart attack. Plaintiff filed a complaint alleging malpractice. The jury found in favor of plaintiff and entered special findings on the amount of damages. Pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code §§ 3333.1, 3333.2, the trial court reduced the award of damages for noneconomic losses from by half and reduced the award for lost wages to the time of trial by collateral source disability payments. The trial court further ordered that defendant pay future medical expenses not covered by medical insurance provided by plaintiff's employer. Plaintiff appealed. The court held that the statute requiring remittitur was constitutional, and that the damages were proper, but reversed the judgment, holding that defendant was denied the right to a jury panel which was constitutionally selected because the trial court dismissed all potential jurors who received medical care from defendant. The court held that the error of faulty jury instructions was harmless.


OUTCOME: The trial court's order that reduced the amount of damages awarded to plaintiff patient was reversed because, although statute requiring reduction of damages was constitutional, the trial court committed reversible error when it impermissibly excused from jury duty, over objection, all prospective jurors who were members of the health plan serviced by the hospital that provided plaintiff patient the allegedly negligent care.

 

1511 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


International Brotherhood of Electrical Workers v. Gridley


Civ. No. 18431.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


121 Cal. App. 3d 169; 175 Cal. Rptr. 274; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1922



June 30, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Aug. 26, 1981 (See 34 C.3d 191)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Butte County, No. 67344, Jean Morony, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff union and employees sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Butte County (California), which denied plaintiffs' petition for a writ of mandate and complaint for injunctive relief that plaintiffs sought to compel defendant city to meet and confer with plaintiff union and an injunction requiring defendant to reinstate plaintiff employees that were discharged for going on strike.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city had enacted regulations which made participation in a work strike unlawful and subjected the employee to disciplinary action, including discharge. Defendant formally recognized plaintiff union as the representative of plaintiff employees. Plaintiff employees then went on strike and were terminated by defendant. Plaintiffs then filed an action in the trial court after defendant refused to allow plaintiff employees to go back to work. The trial court found that the regulations were validly enacted and that defendant had good cause to terminated plaintiff employees and had properly exercised its discretion. Plaintiffs appealed and the court affirmed. The court held that plaintiff employees did not have a vested interest in their employment after they engaged in the illegal strike. The court held that plaintiff employees had no constitutional right to strike and thus had no right to reinstatement upon discharge for so striking. The court also held that defendant had the power to revoke plaintiff union's recognition. Plaintiff union had actively participated in the strike, and therefore defendant was justified in revoking plaintiff union's recognition.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court that denied plaintiff union and employees' application for a writ of mandate and injunction against defendant city and held that plaintiff employees had no vested interest in or legitimate claim of entitlement to continued employment after engaging in an illegal strike and defendant was justified in revoking plaintiff union's recognition because of its participation in the strike.

 

1512 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Garcia


Crim. No. 37239.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


121 Cal. App. 3d 54; 175 Cal. Rptr. 244; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1911



June 29, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. Oct. 22, 1981, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No.A 558399, Howard J. Thelin, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), convicting him of first-degree murder on ground that the jury did not represent a fair cross section of the community and that he received double punishment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of first-degree murder with special circumstances. On appeal, defendant contended that he did not receive a valid jury trial because the jury that convicted him did not represent a fair cross section of the community. Defendant also contended that the use of a felony to establish first-degree murder and to establish special circumstances requiring life imprisonment without possibility of parole constituted double punishment. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court and held that defendant failed to carry his burden of proof that random selection of jurors from voter registration lists underrepresented a cognizable group. Even if there was an underrepresentation, defendant failed to prove that the exclusion was systematic and exhibited a purposeful discrimination against the group. The court rejected defendant's argument that life imprisonment without possibility of parole for specified felony murder must be restricted to intentional killings. Further, the court concluded that defendant was convicted of only one offense and punished for only one offense and, therefore, was not doubly punished.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment, convicting defendant of first-degree murder, was affirmed as random selection of jurors from voter registration lists demonstrated purposeful discrimination against an underrepresented cognizable group. The court determined life imprisonment without possibility of parole was restricted to intentional killings, and defendant's punishment was not double, as he was convicted of and punished for only one offense.

 

1513 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Kizziah v. Department of Transportation


Civ. No. 49094


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


121 Cal. App. 3d 11; 175 Cal. Rptr. 112; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1907



June 29, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 514446-2, Robert H. Kroninger, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Department of Transportation sought reversal of the judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) in an action brought by respondent individual concerning the constitutionality of statutory provisions providing for the allocation of appellant's revenue to local taxing agencies and the reimbursement of revenue scheme to lessees of appellant's property.


OVERVIEW: Respondent individual sought declaratory and injunctive relief against appellant Department of Transportation. Respondent alleged that Cal. Sts. & High. Code § 104.10 was unconstitutional because it provided for the allocation of appellant's revenue to local taxing agencies, which constituted an unlawful delegation of an exclusive legislative function. Respondent also alleged that an unconstitutional gift of public funds was provided through a system of refunds provided in Cal. Sts. & High Code §104.13. The trial court granted judgment to respondent. The court reversed as Cal. Sts. & High. Code § 104.10 did not contain an unconstitutional delegation of authority. The constitutional restriction on how appellant's revenue was allocated was limited to revenue earned on motor vehicle fuels and vehicles or their use. The statute served a constitutional purpose because it applied to revenues derived from leasing lands, which were for future highway needs. Further, because respondent failed to rebut presumption of facial validity of the statutory scheme, the court refused to find reimbursement to property owners, who no longer leased real estate to the appellant, was unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court in favor of respondent individual because the statute authorizing allocation of appellant revenue from property leased for future highway use to local taxing agencies was not an unconstitutional delegation of authority. The purpose of the statute to provide for highway needs served the constitution.

People v. Los Angeles Palm, Inc.


Civ. No. 60964


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


121 Cal. App. 3d 25; 175 Cal. Rptr. 257; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1908; 25 Wage & Hour Cas. (BNA) 985



June 29, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied September 10, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 304985, Jerry Pacht, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Order affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appeal from an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County in favor of plaintiff and enjoining defendant from crediting tips against wages owed to its employees, thereby engaging in an action of unfair competition.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff alleged that defendant kept inadequate records and made improper wage payments to employees. Plaintiff claimed that defendant credited or deducted tips of its employees from their minimum wages, thereby engaging in an act of unfair competition. Defendant appealed after the trial court enjoined it from crediting tips against wages owed. The appellate court held (1) that it was bound by the trial court's implied factual finding that consent was given to warrantless search of defendant's premises where supported by substantial evidence; (2) the Labor Code did not preclude filing a complaint alleging unfair competition; and (3) improperly crediting tips against the minimum wage skewed employee benefits such as vacation or sick time, inaccurately reflected income for federal and state tax purposes, and avoided payment of additional wages for overtime and split shifts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court order enjoining defendant from crediting employees' tips against minimum wages owed where it constituted unfair competition. The Labor Code did not preclude a filing of the complaint alleging unfair competition.

Long Beach City Employees Assn. v. City of Long Beach


Civ. Nos. 58909, 59325


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


120 Cal. App. 3d 950; 172 Cal. Rptr. 277; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1894



June 25, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petitions of plaintiffs and respondents for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied October 22, 1981. Mosk, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed in its entirety as to union members; it is also affirmed, insofar as it imposes an obligation on nonunion members to pay a reasonable attorneys fee from their share of the "common fund"; the case is remanded solely for the purpose of taking evidence concerning the services rendered by the law firms involved to nonunion members in creating and preserving the "common fund," followed by a determination of what constitutes a reasonable attorneys fee to be imposed on nonunion members.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Interveners nonunion city employees sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, (California), which awarded union retained attorneys three percent of salary increase monies obtained as a result of litigation instituted by respondent unions. Interveners contended they were not bound by the terms of the contract negotiated by the union and its attorney.


OVERVIEW: Intervener nonunion city employees sought review of the judgment awarding union retained attorney three percent of salary increase monies obtained as a result of litigation instituted by respondent unions. Interveners contended nonunion members of the union represented bargaining unit, benefitted by the union initiated litigation, were not bound by the terms of the contract for fees negotiated by the union with its attorneys. The court determined that it would be unjust to allow nonunion employees the benefits of union activity without requiring them to share the burdens. The court concluded there was no basis in statute, or judicial precedent to hold the nonunion members to the terms of the agreement negotiated by the union and its attorneys. Intervenors contended the amount required of them should be determined by an assessment of the time and effort actually expended by the attorneys. The court affirmed the portion of the trial court's judgment that imposed an obligation on nonunion members to pay reasonable attorneys fees from their share of the common fund.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial courts judgment, because it would be unjust to allow intervener nonunion city employees to benefit from union activity and not bear any of the burdens. The court upheld the portion of the trial court's judgment that imposed an obligation on interveners to pay reasonable attorney fees from their share of the common fund.

Skyline Homes, Inc. v. Occupational Safety & Health Appeals Bd.

Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mobile home builder sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California), which denied its petition for a writ of administrative mandate against respondent safety appeals board, which had imposed a penalty for failure to provide safety devices for assembly line employees.


OVERVIEW: An industrial accident occurred on the premises of appellant mobile home builder's premises. A safety compliance engineer visited and cited appellant's assembly line operation for certain safety violations. Appellant objected and appealed to respondent safety appeals board. 1517 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Newble


Crim. No. 10354


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


120 Cal. App. 3d 444; 174 Cal. Rptr. 637; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1837



June 16, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Yuba County, No. 3703, Donald K. Wahlberg, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Yuba County (California), which convicted defendant of mayhem, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 203, and sentenced defendant to five years probation on the condition that he spend nine months in the county jail.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was found guilty of mayhem, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 203. Defendant challenged his conviction and claimed that his conduct did not constitute mayhem within the meaning of § 203. Defendant contended that the statutory definition of mayhem was so unconstitutionally vague that it failed to provide sufficient notice of the prohibited conduct, and that the head should not be considered a member of the body. The court affirmed the trial court's judgment and held that there was no tenable reason for distinguishing prominent facial wounds to a nose, ear, or lip from comparable wounds which happen to miss one of those areas of the head specifically mentioned in § 203. Therefore, the head was a member of the body within the meaning of § 203. The court further concluded that the meaning of disfigure in the context of § 203 was sufficiently definite and certain to inform a person of ordinary intelligence what acts were prohibited. As to defendant, § 203 was sufficiently certain to put him on notice that slashing the victim's face with a sharp instrument so as to leave a permanent three inch scar could reasonably be found to be disfigurement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the trial court, which convicted defendant of mayhem and held that defendant's conviction was proper because defendant's conduct left the victim with a permanent disfigurement on her face, which constituted mayhem.
