Washington Water & Light Co. v. East Yolo Community Services Dist.


Civ. No. 19070.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


120 Cal. App. 3d 388; 174 Cal. Rptr. 612; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1832



June 15, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. Aug. 13, 1981, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Yolo County, No. 38817, James F. Roach and Warren K. Taylor, Judges.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff condemnee private utility appealed the order of the Superior Court of Yolo County (California), which sustained the demurrers of defendants, condemnor public agency and state, and dismissed plaintiff's action for inverse condemnation based on the precondemnation activities of defendants.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff condemnee private utility filed an inverse condemnation action against defendants, condemnor public agency and state, based on damages caused by precondemnation activities of defendants. The lower court sustained defendants' demurrers to all eight counts of the complaint, and entered a judgment of dismissal. Plaintiff appealed, and the court affirmed. The court determined that plaintiff failed to state any valid claims in inverse condemnation. The court found that any damages alleged to have occurred subsequent to the date the condemnation action was filed with the Public Utility Commission were not compensable as an independent inverse condemnation action because the exclusive jurisdiction of the commission precluded the award of damages in the trial court. The court held that plaintiff's available remedy against defendant state was by mandamus or declaratory relief, not by inverse condemnation. As plaintiff's claims of inverse condemnation were not actionable, and other remedies were available, the court held that the dismissal of the action was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that dismissed plaintiff condemnee private utility's action against defendants, condemnor public agency and state, for damages as a result of precondemnation activities because plaintiff had no cause of action for inverse condemnation, and other remedies for relief were available.

 

1519 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Tuttle v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 6258


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


120 Cal. App. 3d 320; 174 Cal. Rptr. 576; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1870



June 12, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of real party in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 19, 1981. Richardson, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.



DISPOSITION: Let a writ of prohibition issue prohibiting further proceedings against petitioners or either of them in case number 11507 in the Superior Court of the State of California in and for the County of San Luis Obispo.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners sought a writ to prohibit further proceedings in respondent Superior Court of San Luis Obispo County (California), in which petitioners' motion to dismiss the information under Cal. Penal Code § 995 was denied. Petitioners were charged with cultivation of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11358 and possession of marijuana for sale in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11359.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners filed a motion to dismiss the information against them pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 995 on the grounds that the nighttime service of the warrant was improper, that the warrant was not issued on probable cause, that an aerial search was unlawful, and that the judge erred when he failed to allow disclosure of a material informant. Respondent trial court denied petitioners' motion, and petitioners' filed a writ of prohibition. On review, the court granted petitioners' writ, prohibited further proceedings by respondent, and ordered that all items seized upon the execution of the search warrant be suppressed. The court held that there was probable cause to issue the search warrant and that petitioners' argument that aerial surveillance violated their reasonable expectations of privacy was without merit. Nevertheless, the court held that under Cal. Penal Code § 1533 service of a warrant outside the statutorily allowed time could only be obtained upon a showing of good cause. The court held that the affidavit contained only general statements about drug dealers and firearms and was not sufficient to support nighttime service.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioners' writ to prohibit further proceedings against them in respondent trial court. The court held that petitioners' motion to dismiss the information should have been granted because the affidavit, filed to obtain the search warrant, failed to articulate facts which showed good cause for a nighttime service of the warrant and, thus, violated the statutory provision against nighttime service.

 

1520 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Harris v. Trojan Fireworks Co.


Civ. No. 23938


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


120 Cal. App. 3d 157; 174 Cal. Rptr. 452; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1818



June 8, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 30, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Bernardino County, No. 194523, Don A. Turner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, car accident victims and their families, appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of San Bernardino County (California), which sustained the demurrer of defendant employer, and dismissed plaintiffs' wrongful death and negligence action.


OVERVIEW: After attending a company party where he had become intoxicated, an employee of defendant employer attempted to drive home and was involved in an accident. Plaintiffs, car accident victims and their family, brought a wrongful death and negligence action against defendant, and the lower court dismissed the complaint upon defendant's demurrer. Plaintiffs appealed, and the court reversed. The court determined that applying respondeat superior standards of business activity and foreseeability, there was sufficient connection between the employment and the employee's negligent act to justify holding defendant liable for injuries caused by the employee's conduct. The court ruled that plaintiffs pleaded sufficient facts to support a finding that the employee driver's intoxication occurred at the company party, and his attendance at the party, as well as his state of intoxication, occurred within the scope of his employment. The court also held that it was foreseeable that he would attempt to drive home while intoxicated, and be involved in an accident. Therefore, the court ruled that the lower court erred in dismissing the action against defendant.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that dismissed the wrongful death and negligence complaint brought by plaintiffs, car accident victims and their family, against defendant employer because there was sufficient connection between the employment and the intoxicated employee's negligent act to justify holding defendant financially responsible for the injuries resulting from the car accident.

 

1521 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Fr.


Crim. No. 21370.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


120 Cal. App. 3d 41; 174 Cal. Rptr. 432; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1804



June 3, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Sept. 23, 1981, Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 137965, V. Gene McDonald, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: State appealed a ruling of the Superior Court of San Mateo County (California), which granted defendant's writ of habeas corpus based on the preliminary hearing to annul the judgment that had convicted defendant of lewd conduct pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 647(a).


OVERVIEW: Defendant pled guilty to lewd conduct in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 647(a). He was placed on probation, and after all terms of probation were completed, defendant filed a writ of habeas corpus because the conviction on his record precluded him from receiving certain employment opportunities. The trial court granted the writ, and the state appealed. On appeal, the court held that a writ of habeas corpus was not appropriate because defendant was not in custody, and he cited no authority recognizing extension of the writ beyond instances of constructive custody connected with pending criminal proceedings or the possible reimposition of actual custody. The court also held that the trial court erred in relying on a case which shortened the reach of the statute punishing lewd conduct by applying it only if the actor knew or should have known of the presence of persons who may be offended by his conduct. However, the retroactive effect of this case was specifically limited, and defendant's situation did not comply with those limitations. Thus, the court reversed the grant of the writ.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's grant of a writ of habeas corpus to defendant because his probation was complete and there was no chance of an imposition of custody, thus habeas corpus did not apply to defendant's situation. Furthermore, the court held that case law, which shortened the reach of the statute punishing lewd conduct, did not apply retroactively to this case because defendant's situation did not comply with the limitations.

 

1522 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Hinze v. Superior Court of Marin County


Civ. No. 49988.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


119 Cal. App. 3d 1005; 174 Cal. Rptr. 403; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1799



June 2, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. Aug. 13, 1981, and Repoorter of decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner high school student was denied injunctive and other relief by respondent Superior Court, Marin County (California), after he was suspended from his high school, a real-party-in-interest, for wearing a badge that said "Fuck the Draft." The court granted an alternative writ of mandate to petitioner for the purpose of inquiring into his First Amendment right to wear the badge throughout the school day.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner high school student began wearing a badge that said "Fuck the Draft" to school every day. His high school, a real-party-in-interest, suspended petitioner after he continued to wear it despite being told not to. Petitioner sought injunctive and other relief, which was denied, by respondent trial court. The court granted an alternative writ of mandate to inquire into petitioner's First Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. I, right to wear the badge throughout the school day. The court found that petitioner's suspension was authorized under Cal. Educ. Code § 48900 and the related administrative regulation, Cal. Code Regs. tit. 5, § 300, because he habitually engaged in vulgarity. The court found that petitioner did not stand in the same position as an adult, who was not in a school environment, with respect to his free speech rights. The court found that the restrictions on petitioner's free speech were reasonable in light of the school environment's special characteristics. The court denied petitioner's peremptory writ and discharged the alternative writ.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner's peremptory writ of mandate and discharged the alternative writ, finding that a high school's restrictions on vulgarity were reasonable given the special characteristics of the school environment, and petitioner high school student's right to free speech was not violated by ordering him not to wear a badge containing profanity throughout the school day.

 

1523 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Grimshaw v. Ford Motor Co.


Civ. No. 20095


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


119 Cal. App. 3d 757; 174 Cal. Rptr. 348; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1859; CCH Prod. Liab. Rep. P8999



May 29, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 18, 1981, and the petitions of appellant Ford Motor Company and appellants Gray et al. for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied September 10, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, Nos. 197761, 199397, Leonard Goldstein, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment in Gray v. Ford Motor Co. is affirmed.



CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant car manufacturer challenged an order of the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which denied its motion for a directed verdict as to punitive damages in an action for negligence and strict liability arising from an accident in which a car burst into flames. Appellee passenger cross-appealed a remittitur, while appellee estate of the deceased driver challenged denial of leave to amend its complaint to seek punitive damages.


OVERVIEW: Appellant car manufacturer challenged denial of its motion for a directed verdict and an amended trial court judgment. Appellees were the estate of the deceased driver of a Pinto car that burst into flames in an accident, and a 13-year-old passenger who was severely burned. Appellees had sued on theories of negligence and strict liability, alleging that appellant knew from crash-test results about design defects in the Pinto's fuel system. Appellant assailed the judgment as a whole, assigning numerous errors and irregularities. Appellant primarily contended that the punitive damages award was statutorily unauthorized and constitutionally invalid. Appellee estate cross-appealed, claiming that the statutory bar against punitive damages in a wrongful death action was unconstitutional. The court affirmed the judgment because it was not a denial of equal protection to preclude punitive damages in a wrongful death action. Finding appellant's contentions either lacked merit or failed to demonstrate prejudice, the court held that the punitive damages award was reasonable and just, and declined to modify the judgment by reducing the amount of the remittitur.


OUTCOME: The judgment awarding punitive damages was affirmed as reduced because the reduced punitive damage award was reasonable and just, and was not excessive in light of its deterrent purpose, appellant's wealth, and the size of the compensatory awards. The court also held that a rational justification existed for the legislative denial of the right to seek punitive damages for wrongful death actions.

 

1524 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bracamonte


Crim. No. 38214


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


119 Cal. App. 3d 644; 174 Cal. Rptr. 191; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1774



May 28, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied August 13, 1981. Richardson, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 587635, Ricardo A. Torres, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment appealed from is reversed and the cause is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views above expressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of conviction of burglary of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), alleging error in the denial of his pretrial motion for a separate trial on the validity of alleged prior convictions and in the denial of a motion to suppress an in-court identification.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of burglary and of having committed four alleged prior felony convictions. He appealed, asserting that the trial court erred in denying his motion for separate trials on the issue of guilt and the issue of alleged priors and in denying his motion to suppress an in-court identification. The court ruled that a bifurcated trial was consistent with the right to a fair trial of the validity of the prior convictions. It would have prevented the jury being advised of the alleged prior convictions unless it first found defendant guilty of the charged offense. It concluded that the unitary procedure resulted in prejudice that was an abuse of discretion and deprived defendant of due process of law by revealing his prior criminality without advancing any legitimate state interest. It held that the trial court had properly denied the motion to suppress the in-court identification because the photographic procedure was fair.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of conviction of burglary because defendant was entitled to separate trials on the issues of guilt and the validity of alleged prior convictions. The case was remanded for further proceedings.

 

1525 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Giouzelis v. McDonald


Civ. No. 41859


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


119 Cal. App. 3d 436; 174 Cal. Rptr. 58; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1757



May 22, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Napa County, No. 28132, William L. Blanckenburg, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff accident victim sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Napa County (California), which entered a judgment in favor of defendant driver in plaintiff's action for damages arising out of an automobile accident.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff accident victim was injured as the result of an accident involving a car in which he was riding as a passenger and a car operated by defendant driver. Plaintiff brought an action against both drivers. He settled with his driver for a nominal amount. The first trial against defendant ended with a hung jury. In the retrial, the trial court instructed the jury to consider the comparative negligence of plaintiff's driver and defendant. The jury rendered a verdict for defendant, and plaintiff sought review of the judgment. The court affirmed the trial court's decision. The court held that the procedure which the trial court chose for preserving the issue posed by plaintiff's settlement with his driver was not likely to have confused or misled the jury to such an extent as to require reversal, and that no judicial authority precluded the jury's consideration of the conduct of the settling tortfeasor in determining whether defendant was guilty of negligence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of defendant driver in plaintiff accident victim's action for damages, because the trial court's instruction to the jury to consider the comparative negligence of defendant and the driver of plaintiff's vehicle did not so confuse the jury as to require reversal.

 

1526 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Fields


Crim. No. 21302


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


119 Cal. App. 3d 386; 174 Cal. Rptr. 49; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1753



May 21, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified May 28, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Solano County, No. 13837, William E. Jensen, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order dismissing the action is reversed, and the cause is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged an order of the Superior Court of Solano County (California) that granted respondents' motion to dismiss. The state argued that the fruits of a probationary search were not voided by the reversal of the conviction underlying respondent's probation.


OVERVIEW: The trial court dismissed charges against respondents on the basis that the only evidence supporting the charges had been suppressed. The state appealed. The court evaluated whether the fruits of a probationary search must be suppressed because subsequent to the search, the conviction underlying the terms of probation was reversed. Respondent pleaded to possession of marijuana and was granted probation upon the condition that he submit his person and property to search and seizure by a probation officer or police officer without a warrant. An anonymous telephone caller reported that respondent had marijuana in his home and respondent was later charged. The conviction for possession of marijuana was reversed on the ground that the evidence in that case had been illegally obtained. Thereafter the trial court granted respondents' motion to dismiss. An individual is subject to all the disabilities of a judgment, commitment or sentence, which, though voidable, had not been judicially voided. The dismissal judgment was reversed and remanded because respondent's consent to the warrantless search was not vitiated by the subsequent reversal of his conviction.


OUTCOME: The judgment granting respondents' motion to dismiss was reversed because the reversal of the underlying conviction leading to probation did not void respondent's consent to a warrantless search by police officers as a condition of probation.

 

1527 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Sussli v. City of San Mateo


Civ. No. 46085


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


120 Cal. App. 3d 1; 173 Cal. Rptr. 781; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1800



May 14, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied June 12, 1981. Racanelli, P. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 8, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Mateo County, No. 210801, Preston Devine, Judge. *


* Retired Presiding Justice of the Court of Appeal sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant candidate sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court, San Mateo County (California), which upheld respondent city's local ordinance prohibiting the posting of campaign signs on all public property and its attendant sanction of summary removal. Appellant argued that the local ordinance constituted an impermissible abridgement of the right of free speech guaranteed under provisions of the federal and state constitutions.


OVERVIEW: Appellate candidate was running in an election for city council and, thus, placed campaign signs supporting his candidacy on public property. Respondent city sent a letter to all candidates, including appellant, informing them the sign posting on public property was prohibited by local ordinance section 25.04.090. When the candidates refused to comply with respondent's letter, city personnel undertook removal of the offending signs, resulting in the institution of suit for injunctive and declaratory relief. Appellant argued that the ordinance constituted an impermissible abridgement of the right of free speech guaranteed under the federal and state constitutions. The court held the lower court properly concluded that the ordinance was constitutionally permissible because although a governmental purpose may not be achieved by means that broadly stifle fundamental personal liberties when the end can be more narrowly achieved, respondent's purpose of traffic safety and aesthetic considerations could not be more narrowly achieved except by an ordinance prohibiting sign posting on public property. As such, the court affirmed the lower court's judgment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment finding respondent city's local ordinance banning all sign posting on public property as constitutionally permissible, holding that although a government purpose may not be achieved by means that broadly stifle fundamental personal liberties, respondent's purpose of traffic safety and aesthetic considerations could not be more narrowly achieved except by an ordinance prohibiting sign posting on public property.

 

1528 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Wagner


Crim. Nos. 38719, 38720, 38721


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


119 Cal. App. 3d 90; 173 Cal. Rptr. 766; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1731



May 13, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioners' applications for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied July 29, 1981. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the applications should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The conviction of petitioner Parker of count III (violation of Pen. Code, § 404.6) is set aside. The order to show cause is discharged, and the petitions for habeas corpus are denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, three protesters, sought writs of habeas corpus alleging various deprivations of constitutional guarantees after they had been convicted by a jury in municipal court of unlawful assembly and failure to disperse, and one petitioner had been convicted of rout and inciting a riot. Petitioners' appeal of their convictions had been unsuccessful.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners, three protesters, were involved in a counter-demonstration against the Ku Klux Klan. The demonstration became violent and the police ordered the group to disperse. Petitioners were arrested after they failed to disperse and continued to protest. After a jury trial in municipal court, petitioners were convicted of unlawful assembly and failure to disperse. One petitioner was also convicted of rout and inciting a riot. Petitioners appealed and their convictions were affirmed. They then sought writs of habeas corpus, alleging various deprivations of constitutional guarantees, and the actions were consolidated. The court denied the petitions for habeas corpus and held that while the trial court erred in not allowing defense counsel to use the words "free speech" and "free assembly" during summations, that error was not prejudicial because defense counsel was able to argue the elements of free speech and free assembly, and because that error did not contribute to the convictions. The court held that due process of law required a fair trial, but not a perfect trial. The court set aside the riot conviction, however, as being unsupported by the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court denied habeas corpus relief and held that petitioners, three protesters, did not sufficiently allege any constitutional violations, or in the alternative, the allegations were not prejudicial and did not contribute to their convictions of unlawful assembly and failure to disperse. The court set aside one petitioner's riot conviction and held that it was unsupported by the evidence.

 

1529 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Santiago County Water Dist. v. County of Orange


Civ. No. 23047


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


118 Cal. App. 3d 818; 173 Cal. Rptr. 602; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1705



May 5, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 19, 1981, and the petition of real parties in interest and respondents for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 1, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 314919, Oretta F. Sears, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court denying the Water District's petition for a writ of mandate is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant water district challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Orange County, (California) denying their petition for a writ of mandate. Appellant challenged the sufficiency of an environmental impact report (EIR) that was approved by respondent county board of supervisors. Appellant had filed suit to compel the county to set aside its approval of the EIR.


OVERVIEW: Appellant water district challenged filed a petition for a writ of mandate in the trial court. Appellant had initially filed suit to challenge the sufficiency of an environmental impact report (EIR) that was approved by the respondent county board of supervisors, and to compel the county to set aside its approval of the EIR. The trial court denied the appellant's writ. Appellant sought review. The court noted that a prejudicial abuse of discretion standard was used to review the adequacy of the county's actions in preparing the EIR. The court found that an EIR had to contain facts and analysis, not just the bare conclusions of a public agency. The court found appellant's contentions that respondent's EIR's were inadequate and that appellant had communicated with respondent about deficiencies in EIR's over a period of time. The court reversed the denial of the writ of mandate to the appellant. The court found appellant's action was timely, that laches did not apply, and that strong public policy supported allowing a public agency to bring suit to protect the environment.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court denying the appellant water district's petition for a writ of mandate was reversed. The court found that appellant had made consistent expressions of dissatisfaction with the environmental documents prepared by the county, thus the upholding of an estoppel or waiver defense harmed or contravened the strong California public policy of protecting the environment.

 

1530 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Department of Alcoholic Bev. Control v. Alcoholic Bev. Etc. Control Appeals Bd.


Civ. No. 61616


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


118 Cal. App. 3d 720; 173 Cal. Rptr. 582; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1694



May 4, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petitions of respondent and real party in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court were decided July 24, 1981. Newman, J., was of the opinion that the petitions should be granted.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Board is vacated and the decision of the Department revoking RPI's license is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court issued a writ of review, pursuant to Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 23090, to consider a claim by petitioner Department of Alcoholic Beverage Control that respondent Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board exceeded its jurisdiction when it reversed petitioner's decision to revoke real party in interest's liquor license.


OVERVIEW: Respondent board reversed petitioner department's decision to revoke a liquor license on the grounds that due process and Cal. Gov't Code § 11517(c) required petitioner to comply with real party in interest's request for interpersonal documents. The court held that respondent exceeded its jurisdiction and vacated respondent's decision. The court found that while the administrative procedure act provided for discovery, those provisions were only applicable prior to the initial hearing before the administrative law judge, and that the discoverable matters, as listed in Cal. Gov't Code § 11507.6, did not include the matters demanded by real party in interest. The court found that real party in interest was afforded due process because it was provided with notice of the hearing and because petitioner prepared adequate findings that were supported by the evidence to be found in the administrative record. The court further held that there was nothing in the constitutional guarantee of due process which required or authorized an inquiry into matters outside of the record which might bear on petitioner's reasoning or mental processes.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the decision of respondent board and affirmed the decision of petitioner department to revoke real party in interest's liquor license. The court determined that real party in interest was afforded due process because it had notice of the hearing and petitioner's findings were supported by the evidence in the administrative record.

 

1531 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Donlan v. Weaver


Civ. No. 23543


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


118 Cal. App. 3d 675; 173 Cal. Rptr. 566; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1689; 69 Oil & Gas Rep. 230



May 1, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. 322847, Ronald H. Prenner and Robert C. Todd, Judges.



DISPOSITION: The California statute is not unconstitutional on either of the bases urged. The judgment of dismissal is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) sustaining defendants' demurrer to plaintiffs' complaint that sought to terminate defendants' right of entry to or occupation of the surface and surface zone of certain lots.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiffs were the owners of four lots that were subject to an oil and gas lease executed by the predecessors of plaintiffs and defendants. Plaintiffs commenced an action under Cal. Civ. Code § 772.030, which authorized the owner of a lot to institute an action to terminate the lessee's right of entry to the surface or surface zone of the leasehold land under specified circumstances. Defendants generally demurred to the complaint on the grounds that § 772.030 violated the impairment of contracts clauses of both U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 and Cal. Const. art. I, § 9. The trial court sustained the demurrer. On appeal, the court held § 772.030 did not unconstitutionally impair the obligation of contracts. The court reasoned that the interest of the state to be served by enactment of the legislation was substantial and significant. Defendants also contended that § 772.030 violated the substantive due process guarantees of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Cal. Const. art. I, § 15. The court disagreed and held that property rights were subject to reasonable regulation by legislation validly enacted under the police power.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the trial court sustaining defendants' demurrer. The court held that the statute plaintiffs brought their action under did not violate the impairment of contracts clauses of the state and federal constitutions. The court reasoned that the impairment caused by the statute was sufficiently necessary to the public welfare as to justify the impairment.

 

1532 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Barnes v. State Bd. of Equalization


Civ. No. 19846


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


118 Cal. App. 3d 994; 173 Cal. Rptr. 742; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1723



May 1, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 26, 1981, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied July 8, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, No. 270312, Eugene T. Gualco, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The summary judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff taxpayer appealed an order of the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) that granted summary judgment in favor of defendant State Board of Equalization in plaintiff's action for declaratory relief and for refund of taxes on the grounds that plaintiff had failed to exhaust his administrative remedies.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff taxpayer filed a complaint for declaratory relief and refund of sales and use tax in which he alleged that he had erroneously overpaid such taxes and had filed a claim for a refund from defendant State Board of Equalization, which had been denied. Defendant filed a motion for summary judgment in the trial court on the ground that plaintiff had failed to allege facts constituting a cause of action against the defendant and had failed to exhaust his administrative remedies. The trial court granted defendant's motion for summary judgment, and the court affirmed the summary judgment. The court held that plaintiff failed to exhaust the administrative remedies available to him when he never responded to defendant's request for factual documentation of his claim for a tax refund, leaving defendant unable to render a decision on the merits of the tax refund claim. The court held that plaintiff's failure to exhaust administrative remedies left the trial court without jurisdiction over plaintiff's claim and that there were no issues of fact or of law which could be decided by the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed summary judgment in favor of defendant State Board of Equalization and against plaintiff taxpayer. The court held that plaintiff failed to exhaust his administrative remedies when he never responded to defendant's request for factual documentation of his tax refund claim, which served as a jurisdictional bar to the trial court proceedings.

 

1533 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Groch v. City of Berkeley


Civ. No. 46595


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


118 Cal. App. 3d 518; 173 Cal. Rptr. 534; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1671



April 30, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified May 6, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, No. 511875-9, Robert H. Kroninger, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants city and officials sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), granting respondents taxpayers' request to declare appellants' zoning and neighborhood preservation ordinances, Berkeley, Cal., Ordinances 4641-NS (1973), and Berkeley, Cal. Ordinance 5044-NS, § 20.2 (1978), unconstitutional and enjoin enforcement. Appellants claimed the ordinance scheme was within appellants' authority and master plan.


OVERVIEW: Appellants city and officials established zoning and neighborhood preservation ordinances, Berkeley, Cal., Ordinances 4641-NS (1973), and Berkeley, Cal. Ordinance 5044-NS, § 20.2 (1978), to regulate development consistent with appellants' master plan and a citizen referendum. Respondents taxpayers brought a taxpayer suit, seeking an injunction which was granted by the trial court, alleging that the ordinances were an unlawful delegation of legislative authority to the board of adjustments and the standards were so vague as to render the permit requirements constitutional. Appellants sought reversal of the trial court's granting of the injunction, which was granted, because the ordinance scheme was within appellants' authority to establish a development plan that included regulations that were enforced through permits being issued and the guidelines established for the board of adjustments were sufficiently concise. The court further held that the permitting and regulating scheme established by Ordinance 4641-NS, and Ordinance 5044-NS, § 20.2, were constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and order of the trial court granting respondents taxpayer's request for an injunction against appellants city and officials because the ordinance scheme, which regulated land development, was concise and consistent with appellants' authority.

 

1534 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Alpha Standard Investment Co. v. County of Los Angeles


Civ. No. 59913


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


118 Cal. App. 3d 185; 173 Cal. Rptr. 328; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1638



April 20, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 17, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 295024, Harry L. Hupp, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landlord appealed an order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) upholding the constitutionality of an ordinance which imposed rent increase regulations in lesser amounts of rent increases upon landlords who had increased rent during a period and greater rent increases on those who had not.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff landlord who had raised residential rent sought to have an ordinance limiting rent increases and distinguishing between landlords who had raised rent and those who had not declared unconstitutional as a bill of attainder. Plaintiff had raised its rent on residential units, and was limited by the ordinance to a lower future percentage rent increase than landlords who had not raised their rent on residential units. Because of the difference in future treatment of landlords, such as plaintiff, who had raised rent lawfully in the past and those who had not, plaintiff sought to classify the legislative ordinance as a violation of the U. S. Constitution, Bill of Attainder Clause. The lower court held that limitation on future residential rent increases was a legitimate legislative goal and was not a penalty for the past lawful raising of residential rent. The court affirmed, holding that the ordinance limiting future rent increases to landlords who had recently raised rent to lower percentages than those who had not was not in violation of the U. S. Constitution, Bill of Attainder Clause.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court ruling that restricting the percentage of rent increase to a lower percentage for residential landlords such as plaintiff landlord and those similarly situated who had raised rents recently did not violate the U. S. Constitution, Bill of Attainder Clause.

 

1535 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Gow v. Mitchell Brothers' Santa Ana Theater


Civ. No. 23408


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


118 Cal. App. 3d 863; 173 Cal. Rptr. 476; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1708



April 16, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Orange County, No. 315593, Lester Van Tatenhove, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order granting the preliminary injunction is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant theater appealed from the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which issued a preliminary injunction in favor of the people pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 731 that prevented defendant from exhibiting or selling films that the trial court held were obscene by clear and convincing evidence.


OVERVIEW: The people, pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 731, brought suit against defendant theater to abate a public nuisance, which was alleged to be the exhibition of obscene films. Defendants appealed from an order granting a preliminary injunction. The court affirmed the injunction and held that the trial court's action was well within constitutional boundaries. The court also held that defendants failed to make the required showing that the trial court abused its discretion in granting the injunction. The court held that the injury alleged by the people was irreparable in nature because, if the films in question were later found to be obscene, the exhibition would have been a wrong of a repeated and continuing character, and the trial court's finding that the films were obscene by clear and convincing evidence showed that it was probable that the people would have obtained the permanent injunction it sought. Finally, the court held that it was unable to say that the trial court abused its discretion in granting a preliminary injunction, but ultimately held that it did not.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the preliminary injunction granted by trial court against defendant theater, which prevented defendant from exhibiting obscene films. The court concluded that the threatened injury to plaintiff was irreparable, the people would have prevailed on the merits, and because the trial court concluded that the films were obscene by clear and convincing evidence, the trial court had not abused its discretion.

 

1536 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Agricultural Labor Relations Bd. v. California Coastal Farms, Inc.


Civ. No. 47320.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


117 Cal. App. 3d 971; 173 Cal. Rptr. 215; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1615



April 14, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Aug. 12, 1981 (See 31 C.3d 469)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Monterey County, No. 74978, Richard M. Silver, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant agricultural employer sought review of an order by the Superior Court of Monterey County (California), which granted a preliminary injunction that allowed respondent union representatives limited access to appellant's property during a strike, for the purpose of meeting and talking with nonstriking farm workers.


OVERVIEW: Respondent union representatives picketed appellant agricultural employer. Respondent, the Agricultural Labor Relations Board (ALRB), requested relief pursuant to Cal. Lab. Code § 1160.4, and the superior court issued an order granting respondent union representatives limited access to appellant's property for a specified time before work and during lunch to meet with nonstriking employees. On appeal, the court reversed, and held that "just and proper" temporary relief as permitted by Cal. Lab. Code § 1160.4 did not include the grant of even limited strike access to a grower's property absent a valid rule or regulation by respondent ALRB which permitted such access. The court cited Cal. Code of Regs. tit. 8, § 20900(d), which stated that the legislative purpose of bringing certainty and fair play to an unstable and potentially volatile condition in the California fields could best be served by adoption of rules on access, and that case-by-case adjudication or an overly general rule would cause further uncertainty. Although this rule applied only to the preelection period, the court held that the need to regulate access by rule was even greater during a strike.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order granting the preliminary injunction as an abuse of discretion, because respondent, the Agricultural Labor Relations Board, had promulgated no rule allowing union representatives access to an agricultural employer's property during a strike.

 

1537 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Rice v. Alcoholic Bev. Etc. Appeals Bd.


Civ. No. 60228


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


118 Cal. App. 3d 30; 173 Cal. Rptr. 232; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1620



April 14, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of real party in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 10, 1981.



DISPOSITION: The decision of the Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board is annulled with directions to affirm the decision of the department.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner alcohol board director appealed the decision of respondent Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board (California), reinstating the license of a liquor store, the real party in interest, which was suspended because a clerk sold a bottle of beverage to an obviously intoxicated person.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner alcohol board director suspended the license a liquor store, the real party in interest, because its employee sold alcoholic beverages to an obviously intoxicated person. Respondent Alcoholic Beverage Control Appeals Board reversed because it was not shown the purchaser was intoxicated by reason of alcohol. The matter came before the court for interpretation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25602, that is, whether the purchaser of the alcoholic beverage who was obviously intoxicated was required to be intoxicated by reason of alcohol. A clerk sold the beverage to a customer who was "obviously intoxicated" although his blood-alcohol tests performed after arrest showed him not to be drunk by reason of alcoholic consumption. The court reversed and reinstated the decision of the alcohol control board revoking the license, because it was not necessary that the reason for obvious intoxication be alcohol or that it be assessed by the clerk.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and reinstated the decision of the alcohol board suspending the license of a liquor store, real party in interest, because it was not necessary that alcoholic beverages be the cause of obvious intoxication in a customer.

 

1538 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Container Corp. of America v. Franchise Tax Bd.


Civ. No. 48990


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


117 Cal. App. 3d 988; 173 Cal. Rptr. 121; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1616



April 14, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied May 4, 1981, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 17, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 673492, Daniel M. Hanlon, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California), which denied partial refund of corporate franchise taxes paid to respondent Franchise Tax Board, pursuant to Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 25101, on income derived from or attributable to appellant's foreign subsidiary corporations.


OVERVIEW: Appellant corporation sought review of the denial of its request for a partial refund of corporate franchise taxes paid to respondent Franchise Tax Board pursuant to former Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 25101. The court affirmed, holding that appellant was properly treated as deriving income from sources both within and outside California as appellant and its foreign subsidiaries were a unitary enterprise. The court applied a three-part test to determine the unitary nature of appellant's business, unity of ownership, unity of operation, and unity of use, and found that all three were satisfied. Petitioner and its subsidiaries engaged in the same business activities, and their administrative construction provided a strong inference of a unitary business. The tax did not violate petitioner's due process rights as petitioner availed itself of the substantial privilege of carrying on business in, and the tax was applied to an activity with a substantial nexus with, California. The apportionment did not yield a distorted result, did not violate U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 2, and did not impede the conduct of United States foreign relations and trade.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the denial of petitioner corporation's request for a partial refund of corporate franchise taxes paid to respondent Franchise Tax Board because petitioner and its foreign subsidiaries were a unitary business enterprise, thereby subjecting petitioner to being treated as deriving income from both within and outside of California for corporate franchise tax purposes.

 

1539 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Kelly


Crim. No. 35988.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


117 Cal. App. 3d 944; 173 Cal. Rptr. 106; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1613



April 13, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] As Modified April 30, 1981, on denial of petition for rehearing; Hg. granted Aug. 6, 1981 (Crim. 22137)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-194642, Bernard Lawler, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his conviction and sentence, imposed by the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), for murder of the first degree, with a sentence of life imprisonment without possibility of parole.


OVERVIEW: Appellant and two other men kicked and beat their robbery victim so severely that he later died. Appellant was convicted of murder of the first degree. The special circumstance allegation, that the murder was committed while appellant was engaged in the commission of robbery within the meaning of Cal. Penal Code § 190.2(a)(17)(i), was found to be true. The district attorney elected not to seek the death penalty. Appellant was committed to state prison for life without the possibility for parole. The court found that intent to kill was not required for the sentencing enhancement to apply, only that the killing occurred during the commission of a robbery. The court held that appellant was not being punished twice for the same offense, only that he was being punished more severely for killing his victim in addition to robbing him. The court also held that the punishment of imprisonment without parole was not disproportionate to the offense committed based on the purpose of the statute, internal state comparisons, and comparisons with the rest of the nation. Therefore, the court affirmed appellant's conviction and sentence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for murder of the first degree and sentence of life imprisonment without possibility of parole because the sentence was within constitutional parameters.

 

1540 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Harbert v. Deukmejian


Civ. No. 58270


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


117 Cal. App. 3d 779; 173 Cal. Rptr. 89; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1597



April 8, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 3, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 273151, Arnold Levin, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), declaring that Cal. Penal Code § 12021 did not apply to respondent, who was the recipient of a full and unconditional presidential pardon, in view of the Federal Supremacy Clause, U.S. Const. art. VI, cl. 2.


OVERVIEW: Respondent was convicted of mailing threatening letters in violation of 18 U.S.C.S. § 338a. Respondent was granted a full and unconditional pardon by the President of the United States. Respondent secured employment as a security guard, a position requiring him to carry a gun. Appellants, the state attorney general and others, refused respondent permission to carry a gun pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 12021, which criminalized gun possession by one convicted of a felony. Respondent brought an action for declaratory relief, and the trial court held in favor of respondent. Appellants sought review of the judgment. The court ruled that under the Federal Supremacy Clause, U.S. Const., art. VI, cl. 2, a state could not validly deny to a person holding a federal pardon the full effect of that pardon. Further, the court held that a state could not punish for an offense for which a person was given a federal pardon. The court affirmed the judgment in favor of respondent, concluding that the federal pardon released respondent from all disabilities resulting from his federal conviction and that the state could not, under the Supremacy Clause, disregard that release.


OUTCOME: Judgment in favor of respondent was affirmed where the federal pardon granted to respondent prevented the state from disregarding the federal pardon to disable him from the right to carry a gun.

California Union Ins. Co. v. Central National Ins. Co.


Civ. Nos. 57822, 59316


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


117 Cal. App. 3d 729; 173 Cal. Rptr. 35; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1592



April 3, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of appellant Central National Insurance Company for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 27, 1981.

DISPOSITION: The orders of dismissal are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant insurance companies sought review of orders of dismissal entered by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County, California, after court sustained demurrers to their complaint against respondent California Insurance Guarantee Association.


OVERVIEW: Appellant insurance companies and another insurance company, which was not a party on appeal, wrote legal malpractice policies for insured law firm that were in effect at various times. After former clients of insured recovered a malpractice award, one appellant satisfied the entire judgment. Complaint for declaratory relief was filed to determine liability among insurance companies. Insurance company that was no longer a party on appeal was adjudicated to be insolvent. Appellants claimed that respondent, California Insurance Guarantee Association, was required to pay their claims against insolvent insurance company. Court disagreed since appellants' claims against respondent were specifically excluded by statute. Moreover, court found statute was constitutional since legislature's intention to protect the public, not provide a fund for protection of insurance companies, was rational.


OUTCOME: Orders of dismissal were affirmed; respondent was a legislative creation to provide protection to the general public, not to the insurance industry. Legislature made a constitutionally permissible choice not to permit recovery of claims such as those of appellants in this case.

 

1542 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Fischbach & Moore, Inc. v. State Bd. of Equalization


Civ. No. 58675


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


117 Cal. App. 3d 627; 172 Cal. Rptr. 923; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1582; 28 Cont. Cas. Fed. (CCH) P81,430



April 1, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C182214, Harry V. Peetris, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court gave plaintiffs nothing. To the extent this reflected plaintiffs' liability for the original amount of tax it was correct. But to the extent it denied plaintiffs' claim for return of interest, it was erroneous. We therefore reverse the judgment, remand the cause, and direct the trial court to enter judgment in favor of plaintiffs for $ 179,225.29 plus interest at the legal rate from February 2, 1977.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff contractors sought review of an order from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which found in favor of defendant state stabilization board, in determining that sales and use tax was owed by plaintiffs on their construction contract for a state agency, together with interest. Plaintiffs contended that transmission lines were not within the taxable classification of real property.


OVERVIEW: At issue was whether materials purchased by plaintiff contractors, for construction of an electrical transmission line for a U.S. agency, were taxable under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code §§ 6001, 6051, 6201. Plaintiffs argued the unreasonableness of the classification of transmission lines as real property and that defendant state equalization board was estopped to collect the tax because of its prior ruling that no tax was due. Plaintiffs' purchases of cable, insulators, and fabricated steel towers, for which they gave resale certificates, were purchases of materials consumed by plaintiffs in the performance of a contract to improve real property. As the users of those materials, plaintiffs were required to pay use tax. The state was not estopped from collecting a tax which was owing, even though a state's representative may have previously adopted an incorrect interpretation of the law and advised the public that no taxes would become due on a particular transaction. State officers and agencies had no power to estop the state from collecting a validly owed tax. However, while a taxpayer's liability for the original tax remained, its liability for penalties and interest was excusable.


OUTCOME: To the extent the judgment reflected plaintiff contractors' liability for the original amount of sales and use tax due to defendant state equalization board, it was correct, as they had performed on a contract to improve real property, which was taxable. However, to the extent the judgment denied plaintiffs' claim for return of interest, it was erroneous, as such penalty was excusable. Therefore, the court reversed for entry of a calculated sum.

California Medical Assn. v. Lackner


Civ. No. 18052


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


117 Cal. App. 3d 552; 172 Cal. Rptr. 815; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1575



March 31, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petitions of respondents California Medical Association and Adrouny et al. for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied May 27, 1981. Tobriner, J., was of the opinion that the petitions should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Sacramento County, Nos. 264696, 264878, 265863, 265130, William A. White, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, director of the state department of health services, sought review of a writ of mandate, granted by the Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) to respondents, health care providers and a medical association, requiring appellant to amend regulations raising Medi-Cal reimbursement rates, to make the new rates retroactively applicable to the providers' reimbursement for their services under the old rate schedule.


OVERVIEW: After the state legislature adopted Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code §§ 14075 - 14080, establishing a new schedule for reimbursing physicians and dentists for services rendered to Medi-Cal patients, respondents, health care providers and professional associations, filed suit to compel appellant, the director of the state department of health services, to give effect to the retroactive increase called for in the new rate schedule. The superior court granted a peremptory writ of mandate requiring appellant to amend his promulgated regulation to make the new rates retroactively applicable to services rendered by respondents, and appellant sought review. The court reversed the judgment. It held that the relationship between the state and respondents was contractual, because the state's offer through the applicable regulations, and respondents' acceptance, constituted an implied contract. The court held that retroactive application of higher reimbursement rates would thus contravene Cal. Const. art. IV, § 17, that prohibited payment of extra compensation to state contractors for services already rendered, as well as U.S. Const. art. I, § 10, prohibiting legislative impairment of contracts.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment, holding that the legislation, to the extent that it was retroactive, violated Cal. Const. art. I, § 17 because it provided payment of extra compensation to respondents as state contractors for services already rendered, and also violated U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 because it constituted legislative impairment of contract.

Ebel v. City of Garden Grove


Civ. Nos. 23649, 23656


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


120 Cal. App. 3d 399; 176 Cal. Rptr. 312; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1833



March 18, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied April 14, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, Nos. 331562, 332083, Ronald H. Prenner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed, and the trial court is directed to enter a new and different judgment, one enjoining enforcement of the ordinance noted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, adult bookstore owners, sought review of judgment by the Superior Court of Orange County (California), which concluded that Garden Grove, Cal., Ordinance No. 1116 (1970), requiring a conditional use permit for the operation of a business that sold adult materials was constitutionally valid on its face and within permissible parameters of defendant city's police power.


OVERVIEW: Defendant city threatened to arrest plaintiff adult bookstore owners for any sale of adult materials without a conditional use permit, which was required by Garden Grove, Cal., Ordinance No. 1116 (1970). Plaintiffs sought injunctive and declaratory relief from the trial court to enjoin defendant from enforcing the permit requirements and to declare that plaintiffs did not need conditional use permits to operate their bookstores. The trial court held that the ordinance was constitutionally valid on its face and was a permissible use of defendant's police power. The trial court also found that plaintiffs failed to exhaust their administrative remedies in that they had not applied for the permits. On appeal, the court reversed and held that Ordinance No. 1116, was unconstitutional on its face because it lacked objective guidelines for the issuance of permits required for regulation of activities protected by U. S. Const. amend. I. The court held that plaintiffs' fear of arrest was sufficient to show irreparable injury; thus, plaintiffs could seek to enjoin enforcement of the statute without first seeking a permit.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment, which had upheld the constitutionality of defendant city's ordinance requiring a conditional use permit for the sale of adult material in plaintiff adult bookstore owners action for injunctive and declaratory relief. The ordinance was unconstitutional on its face because there was no assurance that a permit would issue under any circumstance.

In re John R.


Civ. No. 22766


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


116 Cal. App. 3d 940; 172 Cal. Rptr. 387; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1558



March 16, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified April 2, 1981. Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied June 24, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. J-111070, A. Bruce Ferguson, Referee.



DISPOSITION: The Youth Authority is directed to compute and award John R. predispositional behavioral credits in accordance with this opinion. As modified the orders are affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, who was convicted of attempted murder pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 187 and 664, sought review from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) that committed him to the Youth Authority. Defendant contended that the court was limited to the statutory term for assault with intent to commit murder pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 217, predispositional behavioral credits, and that he was denied due process and equal protection.


OVERVIEW: Defendant who shot his father in the face while he slept sought review conviction of attempted murder. Defendant contended that the court was limited to the statutory term for assault with intent to commit murder, predispositional behavioral credits, and that he was denied due process and equal protection The court on review held that the juvenile court did not err in applying the nine-year base term of the general attempt statute on the facts where both attempted murder and assault with intent to murder were alternatively charged and the juvenile court found the multiple shooting to be attempted murder and dismissed the assault charge. The court also held that defendant was entitled to whatever behavioral credits he had earned during predispositional confinement. The court found that there was no constitutional prohibition to prevent retaining jurisdiction over a juvenile offender until age 21 even though by so doing disparate lengths of jurisdiction could be imposed on juveniles who were committed for identical crimes. The order as modified was affirmed and the Youth Authority was ordered to compute and award defendant predispositional behavioral credits.


OUTCOME: The order convicting defendant of attempted murder was affirmed as modified. The court ordered the Youth Authority to compute and award defendant predispositional behavioral credits. The court also held that the juvenile court did not err in applying the term of the general attempt statute and that there was no constitutional prohibition to prevent retaining jurisdiction over a juvenile offender until age 21.

People v. Lopez


Crim. No. 37347


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


116 Cal. App. 3d 600; 172 Cal. Rptr. 236; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1476



March 6, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied March 27, 1981, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied May 8, 1981. Caldecott, J., * participated therein. Bird, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, Nos. A 345494, A 344898, Betty Jo Sheldon, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant defendant challenged the order of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which denied his motion to suppress evidence on the grounds that Cal. Veh. Code § 2805 was constitutional as applied to the case.


OVERVIEW: Appellant defendant's motion to suppress evidence was denied and appellant entered pleas of nolo contendere to receiving stolen property under Cal. Penal Code § 496 and grand theft under Cal. Penal Code § 487(1). On appeal, the court affirmed the convictions and held that Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5 allowed a lower court to have announced one ground on which to suppress evidence, continued the hearing, and later considered an alternate ground contemporaneously advanced in further support of or in opposition to such suppression. When there had not been a full hearing on the matter and there had only been a tentative ruling, the later hearing was not an impermissible relitigation of the original motion. The court also held that Cal. Veh. Code § 2805 was constitutional because it allowed for warrantless searches made under reasonable circumstances, within constitutional limitations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant defendant's conviction and held the motion to suppress evidence was properly denied, since there was no prior litigation of the matter and the search was a constitutional warrantless search of appellant's business premises.

 

1547 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Bye


Crim. No. 11726


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


116 Cal. App. 3d 569; 172 Cal. Rptr. 186; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1473



March 5, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. MH61686, Raul Rosado, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Having considered the scope of the section 1368 procedure, having contrasted it with the more stringent potential results of the LPS Act (involuntary civil commitment) and MDSO procedures, and having recognized the significant difference in purpose between the section 1368 procedure and those procedures compared, we hold appellant has been afforded due process of law under both article I, section 7, subdivision (a) of the California Constitution and the Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution. The statutory "preponderance of the evidence" standard of section 1369, subdivision (f) is approved.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which entered a jury finding that defendant was not competent to stand trial by a preponderance of the evidence after a pre-trial hearing to determine defendant's mental competency pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 1368, 1369.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's criminal proceedings were suspended, and a jury trial was held to determine defendant's mental competency pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §§ 1368, 1369. Based on the state's evidence, the jury found defendant not competent to stand trial by a preponderance of the evidence. On appeal, the court affirmed and held that a § 1369 hearing was not criminal in nature because defendant was not charged with a criminal act, was not subject to criminal proceedings if found insane, the jury would not necessarily have to determine his guilt, and commitment would not necessarily stigmatize him as having committed a crime. The court held that due process did not mandate use of the beyond a reasonable doubt burden of proof because defendant could not be subjected to long-term conservatorship proceedings as a result of the initial § 1368 finding of incompetence. The court also noted that the § 1368 proceeding was designed only to protect the accused. The court emphasized the mandatory reviews under the statutory scheme that ensured that a defendant would not be committed longer than the lesser of three years or the maximum lawful term of incarceration for the original offense charged.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment on the jury finding that defendant was incompetent to stand trial by a preponderance of the evidence because the proceeding was not criminal in nature and thus due process did not mandate use of the reasonable doubt burden of proof.
