Jordan v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 25163


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


116 Cal. App. 3d 202; 172 Cal. Rptr. 30; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1440



February 24, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The petition for a writ of mandate is granted.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee sought a writ of mandate to compel the trial court (California) to set aside its order sustaining third party's demurrer to petitioner's complaint-in-intervention on the grounds that the action was barred by the statute of limitations.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner employee was injured in the course of his employment. Petitioner's employer's carrier paid disability and medical benefits, and two days prior to the one year anniversary of the accident, the carrier sued the third party upon whose premises petitioner was injured for reimbursement of the monies paid to petitioner. Three weeks later, petitioner filed a complaint-in-intervention against the third party for damages for his injuries. The trial court granted the third party's demurrer to petitioner's complaint on grounds that the action was barred by the statute of limitations. On appeal, the court granted petitioner's request for writ of mandate, finding that Cal. Lab. Code § 3853 permitted intervention in the action at any time before trial on the facts.


OUTCOME: Petitioner employee's request for writ of mandate requiring the trial court to set aside its order sustaining third party's demurrer to petitioner's complaint-in-intervention in an action timely initiated by petitioner's employer was granted because the action was not barred by the statute of limitations.

 

1549 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Madden


Crim. No. 4097


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


116 Cal. App. 3d 212; 171 Cal. Rptr. 897; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1441



February 24, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of appellant Madden for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 29, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Stanislaus County, No. 154047, Norman S. Reid, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Each judgment of conviction of appellant Madden on two counts of forcible oral copulation and of appellant Vernacchio on one count of forcible oral copulation is reversed. The conviction of appellant Madden on two counts of sodomy is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged their convictions in the Superior Court of Stanislaus County (California) of forcible oral copulation and sodomy. Appellants alleged that the trial court erred in failing to instruct the jury that it had to agree upon the particular act or acts on which it convicted appellants.


OVERVIEW: Appellants were alleged to have committed several acts of forcible oral copulation, but were not charged with all the acts that were alleged. The prosecutor did not make an election with respect to which act or acts of oral copulation appellants were charged. The jury was not instructed that it had to agree on the particular act or acts on which it convicted appellants. The court held that, because the court could not say that the jurors agreed unanimously upon the act constituting the offense charged, there was no assurance that a miscarriage of justice did not occur. The court held that the trial court erred in failing to instruct the jury on the necessity of agreement and that the error was reversible error. The court reversed appellants' convictions of forcible oral copulation and ordered that the jury be properly instructed in a retrial.


OUTCOME: The court held that the trial court erred in failing to instruct the jury that it had to agree on the particular act or acts on which it convicted appellants. The court reversed the convictions of appellants on charges of forcible oral copulation and affirmed the conviction of sodomy.

 

1550 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Mirmirani


Crim. No. 36726.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION FIVE


115 Cal. App. 3d 765; 171 Cal. Rptr. 562; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1394



February 5, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Apr. 2, 1981 (30 C.3d 375)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A-142563, Armand Arabian, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution sought review of a judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which granted defendant's motion to dismiss, based on lack of evidence, the information that charged him with two counts of making terrorist threats in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 422(a).


OVERVIEW: The prosecution challenged a judgment that granted defendant's motion to dismiss the information that charged him with two counts of making terrorist threats in violation of Cal. Penal Code § 422(a), based on lack of evidence. Defendant argued that there was insufficient evidence to prove his specific intent to achieve social or political goals by his threat, and probable cause to sustain the information did not exist. The court reversed the judgment and held that probable cause existed because defendant's threats to kill police officers' children that made reference to the "Islamic Code," and his statement that within three days of taking the life of each child he and his wife would be out of the country, could reasonably be understood to be those of a person who subscribed to a foreign political philosophy. The court held that the threats proscribed by § 422(a) were not constitutionally protected speech. The court held that § 422 was sufficiently precise to inform one contemplating the communication of such a threat that he did so at the risk of violating the law, and was neither overbroad nor vague.


OUTCOME: The judgment granting defendant's motion to dismiss was reversed because the statute prohibiting terrorist threats was neither overbroad nor vague, and the references made by defendant in his threats were reasonably interpreted as those made by a person who subscribed to a foreign political philosophy.

 

1551 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


City of Chula Vista v. Pagard


Civ. No. 18268


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


115 Cal. App. 3d 785; 171 Cal. Rptr. 738; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1396



February 5, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 18268, George A. Lazar, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant commune members appealed from a summary judgment entered in plaintiff city's favor by the San Diego County Superior Court (California), finding that plaintiff's zoning ordinance did not violate defendants' constitutional right of privacy. The trial court found that defendants' communes were public nuisances and that plaintiff had a right to legislate the number of unrelated persons who could live together in a single-family dwelling.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff city passed a zoning ordinance that allowed only three unrelated persons to live together in a certain area where defendant pastor maintained communal households in single-family residences. After finding that defendants' communes were public nuisances that violated the zoning ordinance, plaintiff sought an injunction. Defendants sought to enjoin the enforcement of the zoning ordinance. Finding that the zoning law infringed no fundamental constitutional right and that a determination of the number of unrelated persons living within a single-family dwelling was a legislative matter, the trial court entered summary judgment for plaintiff because defendants' households constituted a public nuisance. Defendants appealed. The court reversed and remanded, holding that plaintiff's "rule of three" ordinance violated defendants' constitutional right of privacy because it improperly distinguished between "family" members and groups of more than three other persons. Plaintiff did not suggest any rational basis for this clear interference with the exercise of a constitutional right. Furthermore, the inspection requirement (twice yearly) lacked any rational base.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's entry of summary judgment for plaintiff city, holding that plaintiff's zoning ordinance violated defendant commune members' constitutional right of privacy because it allowed a finding of a public nuisance that was irrationally based on a distinction between "family" members and groups of over three unrelated persons, rather than a rational basis such as overcrowding.

 

1552 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Superior Court (Dillon)


Civ. No. 47966


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


115 Cal. App. 3d 687; 185 Cal. Rptr. 290; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1386



February 4, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The peremptory writ of mandate will issue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution sought review by writ of mandate from a decision of the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), which held that real party in interest offender could be sentenced to juvenile facility under Cal. Penal Code § 190, which was reenacted after the criminal offense was committed.


OVERVIEW: The prosecution sought review by writ mandate from a decision of trial court that held that real party in interest offender could be sentenced to juvenile facility pursuant to new provisions of Cal. Penal Code § 190, which was reenacted after the criminal offense was committed. The court held that the lower court erred because the reenacted § 190 did not extend "ameliorative benefits." The court found the purpose of the new § 190 was to increase the punishment for first and second degree murder and its application to real party interest would be an ex post facto law; real party interest could not avail himself to part of § 190 to enable him to go to a juvenile facility without being subject to the other part that called for a longer period of incarceration.


OUTCOME: The court held that the lower court erred when it committed real party in interest to juvenile facility under statute reenacted after commission of the crime.

 

1553 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Beach v. Western Medical Enterprises, Inc.


Civ. No. 49104


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


116 Cal. App. 3d 153; 171 Cal. Rptr. 846; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1436



February 2, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 189627, Richard E. Arnason, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant nursing home operator sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Contra Costa County (California), which enforced civil penalties by respondent director of the state department of health under terms of the Long-Term Care, Health, Safety, and Security Act of 1973, Cal. Health & Safety Code, §1417 et seq. where there were inoperable nurse call buttons and patients were not provided with equipment to prevent bed sores.


OVERVIEW: Appellant operated a nursing home. Respondent director of the state department of health was required to inspect that facility under the Long-Term Care, Health, Safety, and Security Act of 1973, Cal. Health & Safety Code, § 1417 et seq. Respondent's representative inspected the facility. Respondent cited appellant with a class "A" violation of Cal. Admin. Code tit. 22, § 72629(a) for not having operable nurse call buttons and with a class "B" violation of Cal. Admin. Code tit. § 72325(a) as patients were not provided with equipment to prevent bed sores. Appellant resisted the class "A" penalty and respondent filed a lawsuit for enforcement. The lower court determined that the inoperable call buttons presented an imminent danger to the patients and affirmed the class "A" citation. The court affirmed the lower court's decision because it was reasonable to infer that a risk of harm was inherent in a situation where a patient cannot call for help. The court held that there was no requirement that death or serious harm actually result before a citation was issued. The court concluded that the statutory requirement of proper patient care included equipment to prevent bed sores.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's judgment which enforced civil penalties assessed by respondent director of the state department of health under the Long-Term Care, Health, Safety, and Security Act of 1973 because it was reasonable to infer that a risk of harm was inherent where a patient cannot call for help. Appellant nursing home was required under statute to provide proper patient care, which included equipment to prevent bed sores.

 

1554 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Marriage of Denny


Civ. No. 58060


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


115 Cal. App. 3d 543; 171 Cal. Rptr. 440; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1374



January 29, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. D 902310, Marvin A. Freeman, J.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed and remanded for the limited purpose of providing to wife a hearing under Civil Code section 4801.5 on the issue of spousal support. In all other respects, the judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant wife sought review of an interlocutory judgment of dissolution of marriage in the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) from respondent husband in an action involving the determination of community and separate property, spousal support, and a social security disability credit.


OVERVIEW: Appellant wife challenged a judgment of dissolution of marriage from respondent husband. They stipulated that the donut shop business belonged to respondent before the marriage. Appellant sought review on the issues of community property and credits for social security payments. The trial court also terminated spousal support to appellant after testimony that appellant was living with another man without hearing proffered testimony on the reason for cohabitation. The court reversed the trial court's judgment on the spousal support termination and remanded for a hearing in accordance with Cal. Civ. Code § 4801.5. Once respondent established cohabitation, appellant was afforded an opportunity to present rebuttal evidence on the issue of decreased need for support. The court affirmed the rest of the judgment and found that it was proper for the trial court to refuse evidence concerning the decreased value of respondent's business during the marriage where the value was no greater at the time of the separation than at the time of the marriage. Respondent, as a noncustodial parent, was entitled to a credit for social security disability payments he made on behalf of the minor children.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's judgment on the matter of respondent husband's termination of spousal support and remanded the matter for a hearing on the issue of appellant wife's cohabitation with another person because appellant was entitled to a hearing on a decreased need for spousal support. The court affirmed the remainder of the dissolution of marriage judgment.

 

1555 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Cortez


Crim. No. 35918


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


115 Cal. App. 3d 395; 171 Cal. Rptr. 418; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1326



January 28, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Ventura County, No. CR 14343, Robert Lee Shaw, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order granting probation) is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed his convictions by the Superior Court of Ventura County (California) for an act of oral copulation with a prisoner while confined in the county jail, acting in concert in performing an act of oral copulation by force or violence and against the will of a prisoner, and participating in a forcible act of oral copulation with a prisoner in violation of Cal. Penal Code §§ 288a(c), (d), (e).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged by the prosecution with an act of oral copulation with a prisoner while confined in county jail, acting in concert in performing an act of oral copulation by force or violence and against the will of a prisoner, and participating in a forcible act of oral copulation with a prisoner. The victim in each case was the same prisoner. Three co-defendants were charged with the offenses. Defendant appealed his convictions and alleged that the jury charge on distrust of accomplice testimony was erroneous, that Cal. Penal Code § 288a was unconstitutional, and that there were improper multiple convictions. The court affirmed the conviction and stated that because the trial court instructed the jury on how to determine whether a co-defendant was an accomplice, and because defendant did not call him as his witness, the instructions were proper. Defendant had no standing to attack the constitutionality of § 288 as it applied to consenting adults because his alleged violation pertained to force. Also, the proscription against multiple convictions for greater and lesser offenses did not apply because the charges against defendant referred to separate acts.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for oral copulation and held that the trial court adequately instructed the jury with regard to the rules relating to accomplices because the proposed accomplice was not a witness for the defense. Further, because the charges in two oral copulation counts referred to separate acts by defendant, the convictions on both counts were proper.

 

1556 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Morales


Crim. No. 11967


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


115 Cal. App. 3d 456; 171 Cal. Rptr. 425; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1332



January 28, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 17, 1981, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 25, 1981. Richardson, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted. As Modified February 18, 1981.



DISPOSITION: We conclude the Legislature may not limit the 1980 amendment to Welfare and Institutions Code section 3201 to persons who committed their offenses after July 29, 1980, and accordingly section 9 of Statutes 1980, chapter 822, is invalid. The amended statute therefore applies to Morales and its provisions control the calculation of his commitment. Because, however, he has already been released from that commitment, no further order from this court is necessary, and the petition for writ of habeas corpus is therefore denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed for release under habeas corpus, alleging that he was unconstitutionally confined in the California Rehabilitation Center (CRC) for a longer period than the determinate sentence for the underlying criminal offense, because the amendment to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3201 did not include him and others when it was applied prospectively only.


OVERVIEW: Defendant sought habeas corpus release, stating that he was being unconstitutionally confined at the California Rehabilitation Center (CRC) beyond his determinate term because the amendment to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 3201 was not being applied to his case. By legislative amendment to § 3201, the term of a CRC commitment for those who committed crimes after its effective date was limited to the determinate sentence for the underlying offense. The amendment applied prospectively only. The court found that the date of incarceration was not a rational difference between classes of persons, and concluded that the legislature could not limit the amendment to persons who committed their offenses after its effective date, and accordingly that portion of the amendment was invalid. The court held that the discriminatory classification could be corrected by invalidating the impermissible exception and by extending the statutory provisions to all addicts subject to CRC. Because defendant had already been released from commitment, the petition for writ of habeas corpus was denied.


OUTCOME: The court concluded that the legislature should not have limited the amendment to the statute to apply prospectively only and that portion was invalid. However, defendant had already been released from commitment and so the petition for writ of habeas corpus was denied.

Pringle v. City of Covina


Civ. No. 58388


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


115 Cal. App. 3d 151; 171 Cal. Rptr. 251; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1303



January 26, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 20, 1981, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 25, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 284321, Thomas T. Johnson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order) is remanded to the superior court for further proceedings consistent with the views expressed herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant theatre sought review of a denial by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) of its request for a preliminary injunction to enjoin respondent city from arresting or prosecuting or instituting civil actions against appellant under its adult entertainment zoning ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Appellant theatre showed a variety of films, some of which included those with an X rating from the American Motion Picture Association. Respondent city enacted an ordinance which attempted to prohibit the showing of such films. The trial court denied appellant's request for a preliminary injunction to prohibit respondent from enforcing the ordinance against it, and appellant sought review. Appellant contended that the ordinance constituted an infringement on its right to free speech and that the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague. The court determined that the ordinance, as written, was not applicable to appellant. The court found that the ordinance prohibited the showing of X-rated films in establishments where such films were the main offering, and that appellant showed a variety of films, only a small percentage of which fell into the prohibited category. The court remanded the case for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court remanded the case to the trial court, which had denied appellant theatre's request to enjoin respondent city from enforcing its adult entertainment ordinance against appellant, for further proceedings consistent with the decision. The court determined that the ordinance as written would not apply to appellant.

 

1558 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Rodriguez


Crim. No. 11533.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


116 Cal. App. 3d 1; 171 Cal. Rptr. 832; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1422



January 26, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. Apr. 3, 1981, and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. CRE 44675, Thomas G. Duffy, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his convictions, entered in the Superior Court, San Diego (California), for assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury upon a police officer, obstructing a police officer, remaining present at the place of an unlawful assembly after having been warned to disperse, carrying a switchblade knife, and battery upon a police officer.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a guest at a party, which had generated several noise complaints from neighbors due to the loud band. Several police units arrived on the scene, including a police helicopter. The police ordered the guests to disperse. As an officer stepped into the kitchen through an outside door, defendant threatened him with an unplugged electric knife. Defendant was charged with remaining present at the place of an unlawful assembly and several other charges, including possession of a switchblade knife, and was convicted of all charges. The court held that the trial court erred in instructing the jury that a violation of Cal. Bus. & Prof. Code § 25658, furnishing alcohol to persons under 21, supported the unlawful act element of the crime of unlawful assembly. The court also held that the trial court's instruction as to intent was ambiguous and unclear. The court found that the evidence did not support the contention that the party posed a threat of violence. The court further found that the discovery of defendant's switchblade was the direct result of the police officers' unlawful entry into the party. Therefore, the court reversed all of defendant's convictions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's convictions because the party did not constitute an unlawful assembly, the police unlawfully entered the party, and defendant's switchblade was discovered as the result of an illegal search.

Georgie Boy Manufacturing, Inc. v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 60249


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


115 Cal. App. 3d 217; 171 Cal. Rptr. 382; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1310



January 26, 1981 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] A peremptory writ of mandate is hereby issued directing the trial court to vacate its prior order overruling Georgie Boy's demurrer and to issue a new and different order sustaining Georgie Boy's demurrer, and granting its motion to strike allegations relating to punitive damages.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner manufacturer sought a peremptory writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) to vacate its order overruling petitioner's demurrer to the wrongful death action brought by real party in interest heir of decedent, which contained a prayer for punitive damages under Cal. Civ. Code § 3294.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest, heir of decedent, filed a complaint for wrongful death against petitioner manufacturer, and alleged that decedent died of carbon monoxide asphyxiation in a motor home manufactured by petitioner. Among other damages, real party sought punitive damages under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 3294. Petitioner filed a demurrer and argued that punitive damages were not available in wrongful death actions under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 377. The trial court held that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 377 was unconstitutional and violated the equal protection of the laws. The court first held that mandate was proper because the trial court's order was not appealable. The court also held that Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 377 was constitutional under the rational basis standard. The court refused to apply a stricter standard on real party's contention that her right to punitive damages was fundamental. By enacting Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 377, the legislature limited the availability of punitive damages and a decedent's heirs were precluded from recovering damages for injuries that were personal to decedent. The legislature's concern for the danger of excessive recoveries was a legitimate purpose.


OUTCOME: The court granted petitioner manufacturer's peremptory writ of mandate and ordered respondent superior court to sustain petitioner's demurrer and to strike allegations relating to punitive damages. The court held that the statute precluding real party in interest, heir of decedent, from recovering punitive damages was constitutional under the rational basis standard.

MCA, Inc. v. Franchise Tax Board


Civ. No. 59021


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


115 Cal. App. 3d 185; 171 Cal. Rptr. 242; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1307



January 26, 1981

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff corporation appealed a judgment of the Superior Court, Los Angeles County (California), entered in favor of defendant franchise tax board, in plaintiff's action to recover corporation franchise taxes.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff corporation's business was the production and worldwide distribution of motion picture and television films distributed through licensing agreements with foreign exhibitors. Plaintiff also licensed the reproduction and sale of phonograph records in foreign countries. Most of the foreign countries imposed a tax on plaintiff on the gross amount of film rentals and record royalties paid to plaintiff by the foreign licensees. On its California franchise tax return, plaintiff claimed a deduction under Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 24345 (§ 24345) for the foreign taxes. Defendant board of franchise taxes disallowed the deduction on the ground that the foreign taxes were measured by income. Plaintiff filed suit, and the trial court ruled in favor of defendant. On appeal, the court affirmed. The foreign taxes paid by plaintiff were not deductible under § 24345 because the taxes were measured by gross income.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of defendant franchise taxes board in plaintiff corporation's suit to recover corporation franchise taxes; plaintiff was not entitled to a deduction for foreign taxes paid that were measured by gross income.

Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three

DISPOSITION: The convictions for robbery under counts two and four are reduced to convictions for assault with a deadly weapon, the eight-month enhancement of count four is stricken, and as modified the judgment is affirmed. The Department of Corrections is directed to compute appellant's conduct credit administratively.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court, Santa Clara County (California) that convicted and sentenced him for four counts of robbery, Cal. Penal Code § 21; two with use of a deadly weapon, Cal. Penal Code §12022(b); and two with use of a firearm, Cal. Penal Code § 12022.5.

Penal Code §12022(b), and use of a firearm in the commission of the other two counts, Cal. Penal Code § §12022.5. On review, appellant argued that there was evidence of only two robberies, sentencing errors were made, his trial counsel was ineffective, and instructional errors were committed. In partial agreement with appellant, the court reduced two of appellant's robbery convictions to convictions for assault with a deadly weapon, struck one eight-month gun use sentencing enhancement, and directed the corrections department to compute appellant's conduct credit. The court rejected appellant's other arguments, however, holding, inter alia, that appellant failed to meet his burden of establishing ineffective assistance of counsel and that the trial court committed no errors in instructing the jury.

City of Imperial Beach v. Escott


Civ. No. 22490


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division One


115 Cal. App. 3d 134; 171 Cal. Rptr. 197; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1301



January 23, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 11, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 442787, Louis M. Welsh, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant city challenged the judgment from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California), which sustained respondent adult bookstore owner's demurrer, without leave to amend, finding that the requirements of the conditional use permit demanded by appellant were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Respondent, adult bookstore owner, refused to submit to a conditional use permit required by appellant city pursuant to its zoning ordinance. Appellant filed suit against respondent to enjoin its operation as a public nuisance. The trial court entered a judgment of dismissal. Appellant challenged the judgment. The court found that the ordinance sought to regulate the operation of an adult bookstore, and used its zoning ordinance to do what the law said it could not do under licensing statutes. Statutes which authorized public officials to license conduct protected by the U.S. Const. amend. I, such as operation of an adult bookstore, must set forth definite, objective guidelines for the issuance of such licenses. The sections of the ordinance were "subjective" and incapable of precise "objective" measurement. Thus, the standards were found to be unconstitutionally vague, uncertain, overly broad, and a prior restraint against U.S. Const. amend I protected activity. Accordingly, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment that sustained respondent adult bookstore owner's demurrer because appellant's zoning ordinance was unconstitutionally vague, overbroad, and an unconstitutional prior restraint.

Lopez v. Southern Cal. Permanente Medical Group


Civ. No. 59349


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


115 Cal. App. 3d 673; 171 Cal. Rptr. 527; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1384



January 23, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellants' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 1, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. SOC42310, Rosemary M. Dunbar, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants injuried parties challenged the judgment from the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which dismissed the respondent state prior to trial, granted respondent doctor's motion for nonsuit and found respondent hospital not negligent in failing to comply with statutory reporting requirements arising from an automobile accident caused by an epileptic driver.


OVERVIEW: Appellants injured parties were involved in an automobile accident caused by a driver who allegedly suffered an epileptic seizure. Suit was filed against him and respondents, state, doctor, and hospital. Respondents, doctor and hospital, were alleged to have failed to have reported the driver's epileptic condition pursuant to Cal. Health & Safety Code § 410(1). Respondent state was alleged to have failed to revoke the driver's license even though it was aware he was an epileptic. The respondent state was properly dismissed as it had not been aware of the driver's epilepsy until after the accident occurred. The respondent doctor's nonsuit was properly granted as there was no evidence which could support a verdict in appellant's favor against him. Respondent doctor, an orthopedic surgeon, had not treated the driver for epilepsy but for a back condition. He had no obligation to report the epilepsy of driver as respondent doctor had not diagnosed the driver. Respondent hospital was not negligent in failing to comply with statutory reporting requirements as it reasonably presumed it had already been reported. Accordingly, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment as respondent state was properly dismissed, the grant of respondent doctor's motion for nonsuit was proper, and respondent hospital was not negligent in failing to comply with statutory reporting requirements.

Citizens against Forced Annexation v. Local Agency Formation Com.


Civ. No. 59877.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


114 Cal. App. 3d 992; 170 Cal. Rptr. 884; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1369



January 21, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Apr. 29, 1981 (See 32 C.3d 816)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Local Agency Formation Commission of Los Angeles County sought review of an order of the trial court (California), which granted respondent citizens a preliminary injunction in their action which sought to prevent annexation of an unincorporated area of their town. Appellants contended that the election regarding annexation was unconstitutional and the issuance of an injunction was erroneous.


OVERVIEW: Respondent citizens brought an action against appellant Local Agency Formation Commission of Los Angeles County, which sought to prevent the annexation of certain unincorporated areas of their town and which challenged the proposed annexation on the ground that the authorizing statute, Cal. Gov't Code § 35228(b), was unconstitutional because it only allowed residents of the area being annexed to vote on whether the annexation should go forward. The trial court issued a preliminary injunction prohibiting further action on the annexation. Appellant sought review of that determination. The court reversed the order granting the preliminary injunction and remanded to the trial court with directions to enter an order denying the preliminary injunction. The court held that Cal. Gov't Code §§ 35228(b) and 35231 served a compelling state interest and were necessary to achieve that interest, and thus, were constitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order granting the injunction to respondent citizens, and held that the statutes authorizing annexation were constitutional and survived strict scrutiny. The court directed that the injunction against proceeding with the annexation, to appellant local agency formation commission, should be vacated, and the motion to impose same denied.

 

1565 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People ex rel. Gow v. Mitchell Brothers' Santa Ana Theater


Civ. No. 19940


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


114 Cal. App. 3d 923; 171 Cal. Rptr. 85; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1364



January 20, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petitions of all the parties for a hearing by the Supreme Court were denied May 13, 1981. Reversed by Supreme Ct. and reiterated except for the point reversed in People ex rel. Cooper v. Mitchell Bros.' Santa Ana Theater, (See 128 C.A.3d 937)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 256530, Marvin G. Weeks, Judge. *


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The $ 5,000 discovery sanction and those parts of the "Judgment After Trial By Court" enjoining the defendants from advertising or exhibiting the eleven films found by the trial court to be obscene beyond a reasonable doubt and denying the City's requests for a constructive trust on the defendants' box office receipts, a revocation of the defendants' license, and the closure of defendants' theater for one year are affirmed. The "Judgment on Verdicts in Open Court" awarding $ 76,400 in compensatory damages to the City and those parts of the "Judgment After Trial By Court" ordering access for the City's officers to all parts of the defendants' theater for two years, the payment by the defendants of $ 100,000 into a trust fund for the City's future attorney fees, and the destruction of the prints of [***2] the films declared obscene are reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff People and defendant film producers sought review of judgments from the Superior Court of Orange County (California) finding certain of defendants' films obscene and granting part of the relief requested by plaintiff.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff People brought an action against the owners and operators of defendant film producers' theater to abate a public nuisance pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 731. It was alleged that the showing of obscene films constituted a public nuisance. Plaintiff sought revocation of defendants' licenses and permits, permanent enjoinment from showing the named films, destruction of the named films, forfeiture of box office receipts, closure of the theater for a year and sale of its fixtures, and compensatory and punitive damages, costs and attorney's fees. The jury found some films obscene. On appeal, the court held that the only purpose of a § 731 action by a city attorney was to abate a public nuisance. If defendants were permanently enjoined from advertising or exhibiting the films that were found obscene, the public nuisance would be abated. The other remedies were excessive. The court affirmed parts of judgment enjoining defendants from advertising or exhibiting the obscene films, but reversed the awarding of damages, fees, and destruction of prints of obscene films.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed parts of the judgment for plaintiff People enjoining defendant film producers from advertising or exhibiting the obscene films, but reversed the awarding of damages, fees, and destruction of prints of obscene films. The only purpose of the action was to abate a public nuisance.

 

1566 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Roberts


Crim. No. 4110


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


114 Cal. App. 3d 960; 170 Cal. Rptr. 872; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1367



January 20, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 18, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Kern County, No. 19323, Walter Osborn, Jr., Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a decision of the Superior Court of Kern County (California) convicting him of robbery and assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 245(a). Appellant argued that § 245(a) was unconstitutional in that it was so vague in its definition of the constituent elements of great bodily injury that it did not provide adequate guidance for the fact finder.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was convicted by a jury of robbery and assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 245(a). On appeal, appellant argued that § 245(a) was unconstitutional in that it was so vague in its definition of the constituent elements of great bodily injury that it did not provide adequate guidance for the fact finder. The appellate court rejected appellant's argument and affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court found that the term "great bodily injury" was sufficiently certain and definite to meet the constitutional requirements. The court held, therefore, that § 245(a) was not impermissibly vague. The court also rejected appellant's contention that the jury should have been instructed sua sponte as to the meaning of the words "great bodily injury." The court found that the kicking on the head and torso of the victim while he was on the ground appeared to be unmistakably an assault that a jury could reasonably find was likely to produce great bodily harm. The court therefore affirmed appellant's convictions.


OUTCOME: The appellate court affirmed a trial court judgment convicting appellant of robbery and assault by means of force likely to produce great bodily injury, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 245(a). The court held that § 245(a) was not impermissibly vague such that it did not provide adequate guidance for the fact finder of the constituent elements of great bodily injury.

Muth v. Educators Security Ins. Co.


Civ. No. 45675


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


114 Cal. App. 3d 749; 170 Cal. Rptr. 849; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1358



January 19, 1981 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 344662, John E. Longinotti, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The "order discharging an attachment" is affirmed. The superior court will take such additional proceedings as are not inconsistent with the views we have expressed. The parties will temporarily stand their respective costs of the appeal, pending final superior court determination of the action.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff creditor sought review of judgment from the Superior Court of Santa Clara County (California) that discharged an attachment that was issued to be levied upon defendant insolvent insurance company's property after plaintiff elected to accelerate payment of a promissory note. Defendant was a Nebraska corporation, but the property was located in California.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff creditor challenged a trial court order discharging an attachment issued against defendant insolvent insurance company's property, which secured a promissory note. Plaintiff elected to accelerate payment of the note after defendant's conservator tendered an interest payment late along with a letter that stated that the payment was not an acknowledgement of defendant's obligations under the note and that it was negotiating an agreement for the reinsurance of defendant's life insurance policies in exchange for currently held assets. The court affirmed the trial court order discharging the attachment, but remanded the case to the trial court and directed that plaintiff be permitted to pursue further proceedings on the main relief sought - judgment on the promissory note. The court held that defendant's attempt to unilaterally deny plaintiff recourse to its real property situated in California was contrary to California law and public policy. The court held that such an issue of illegality or abridged public policy could be raised for the first time on appeal and that defendant could not use its assets to reinsure policy liability without consent of its creditors.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court order discharging an attachment on defendant insolvent insurance company's property which secured a promissory note, but remanded to the trial court and directed that appellant creditor be permitted to pursue further proceedings for judgment on the promissory note because defendant sought to enforce a contract that was void as against public policy as to creditors in the California.

 

1568 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Martin


Crim. No. 10826


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


114 Cal. App. 3d 739; 170 Cal. Rptr. 840; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1357



January 16, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied April 29, 1981. Bird, C. J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Riverside County, No. CR-14997, Roland Wilson, Judge. *


* Retired judge of the municipal court sitting under assignment by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: Judgment affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which convicted defendant for murder, attempted murder, robbery, and burglary. Defendant claimed that the jury was improperly instructed as to the test for criminal insanity after defendant was found to be sane but a sociopath.


OVERVIEW: Defendant criminal sought review of his conviction for murder, attempted murder, robbery and burglary after entering a plea of not guilty by reason of insanity. Defendant was found sane at the time of the commission of the offenses, but was deemed a sociopath. The lower court instructed the jury in the language of the second paragraph of the American Law Institute's (ALI) test of criminal insanity. Defendant claimed it was error to so instruct the jury. On review, the court found that the adoption of the second paragraph of the ALI test, which provided that the terms mental disease or defect did not include an abnormality manifested only by repeated criminal or otherwise anti-social conduct, was a public policy decision. To exclude deliberate and persistent offenders from responsibility from criminal action without a diagnosable mental disease or defect would have been absurd, and a sociopath was properly excluded from the definition of mental disease or defect. The conviction was affirmed, because it was proper to instruct the jury of the above in an appropriate case.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed. Defendant's convictions for murder, attempted murder, robbery, and burglary were upheld, because it was not error to instruct the jury that, as to the test for criminal insanity, a mental disease or defect did not include an abnormality manifested by repeated criminal conduct.

 

1569 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Hull v. Cason


Civ. Nos. 38801, 40034, 41036


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


114 Cal. App. 3d 344; 171 Cal. Rptr. 14; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1282; 25 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P31,627



January 7, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Rehearing Denied 114 Cal. App. 3d 344 at 376.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Alameda County, Nos. 451337-451339, Robert K. Barber, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is reversed. The superior court, and the parties or any of them, may take such further proceedings as are not inconsistent with the views we have expressed. A cross-appeal by plaintiffs from the portion of the judgment denying an award of attorney fees having become moot, it is dismissed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant nonminority persons challenged an order of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California), which imposed minority-person quotas on the hiring and promotion practices of appellee officials of a city fire department after the court found historical discrimination against minorities. Appellants argued that the quotas discriminated against nonminority persons.


OVERVIEW: Appellant nonminority persons challenged an order that imposed minority-person quotas on hiring and promotions by appellee officials of a city fire department based on the trial court's findings of long-existing discriminatory patterns in the employment of firefighters. The trial court entered the order even though appellees had long since ended the historical discrimination in hiring and promotion practices, and the city was making a good faith endeavor to ameliorate and overcome the bitter effects of that discrimination. Appellants contended that the U.S. Const. amend. XIV and the Civil Rights Act of 1964, 42 U.S.C.S. § 2000e-2(a)(1), did not permit the state or its courts to racially discriminate against nonminority persons, who themselves were individually without fault, in order to ameliorate the effects of past racial discrimination against minority persons generally. The appellate court agreed and reversed the order, holding that Title VII prohibited racial discrimination against nonminorities upon the same standards as would be applicable if they were minorities. The court also held that a quota was not less offensive when it served to exclude a racial majority.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed because there was no evidence of present-day discrimination against minority persons, and no valid justification was demonstrated for imposing a judicially fashioned affirmative relief to ameliorate or dissipate the effects of past discriminatory practices. The court held that the preferential treatment of minorities in employment was invalid if it deprived nonminorities of a benefit because of race.

 

1570 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Toubus v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 49876


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


114 Cal. App. 3d 378; 170 Cal. Rptr. 697; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1283



January 7, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitioner's application for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 11, 1981.



DISPOSITION: The alternative writ is discharged; the petition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a petition for writ to compel the state to set aside the information charging him with possession for sale of cocaine, trafficking in cocaine, and possession of amphetamines.


OVERVIEW: An informant and undercover police officer went to defendant's residence to arrange a drug transaction. Defendant showed the pair a bag containing cocaine. The pair left the residence and returned with another agent, who was the purported purchaser. The door had been left ajar, so police officers entered the residence and arrested defendant. Defendant was charged with possession for sale of cocaine, trafficking, and possession of amphetamines. Defendant filed a petition to compel the state to set aside the information on the grounds that his arrest was illegal. On appeal, the court denied defendant's petition. Defendant admitted the undercover officers into his home. The fact that the officers gained admittance based on defendant's mistaken belief that they were going to purchase cocaine was irrelevant. The search warrant based upon defendant's arrest was proper because there a substantial basis for concluding that contraband was to be found on defendant's premises.


OUTCOME: The court denied defendant's petition because the arrest of defendant in his own home and the warrant based upon such arrest were lawful. Defendant gave the undercover officer's permission to enter his home. Based on the officer's observations, there was competent evidence upon which to base the search warrant.

Trent Meredith, Inc. v. City of Oxnard


Civ. No. 59339


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


114 Cal. App. 3d 317; 170 Cal. Rptr. 685; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1281



January 6, 1981 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied February 5, 1981, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 11, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 69118, Robert Lee Shaw, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff subdivider appealed the judgment of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California) in favor of defendant city in plaintiff's action challenging the constitutionality of an ordinance enacted pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 65970 et seq. requiring real property developers to pay fees or dedicate land to local school districts to relieve conditions of overcrowding causeD by development.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff subdivider brought an action against defendant city, contending that an ordinance requiring real property developers to pay fees or dedicate land to local school districts to relieve conditions of overcrowding caused by development was unconstitutional. Plaintiff appealed the decision of the trial court in favor of defendant. On appeal, the court affirmed the trial court judgment, holding that the ordinance was not a "special tax" within the meaning of Cal. Const. art. XIII A, § 4, which would have required two-thirds voter approval for imposition. The court further held that the ordinance was enacted pursuant to Cal. Gov't Code § 65970 et seq. and that § 65970 et seq. was a valid exercise of the state's police power aimed at the subject of overcrowding in schools. The court also held that the imposition of § 65970 et seq. was not an ad valorem tax.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court in favor of defendant city holding that the enabling statute which permitted the enactment of local ordinances requiring real property developers to pay fees or dedicate land to local school districts was a valid exercise of the state's police power aimed at overcrowding in schools.

Subgrade Constr. Corp. v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


Civ. No. 19552.


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


118 Cal. App. 3d 493; 173 Cal. Rptr. 514; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1668; 46 Cal. Comp. Cases 470



Apr. 29, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - ORDERED NOT PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. den. June 24, 1981 and Reporter of Decisions directed not to publish this opn. in the Official Reports (Cal. Const., art. VI, § 14; rule 976, Cal. Rules of Ct.).


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, employer and dependents, sought a writ of mandate to review the decision of respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (California), which denied reconsideration of its supplemental decision on dependency benefits when it awarded a portion of the dependency benefits to respondent Department of Industrial Relations pursuant to Cal. Lab. Code § 4706.5.


OVERVIEW: The father of petitioner dependants from compensable injuries sustained during employment. Petitioner dependants applied for dependency benefits with respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board. The board found partial dependency and, in a supplemental decision, awarded respondent Department of Industrial Relations a portion of the dependency benefit under Cal. Lab. Code § 4706.5. In denying reconsideration of the supplemental decision, respondent board adopted the reasoning of the workers' compensation judge that petitioners had potentially meritorious arguments that § 4706.5 was unconstitutional. The court held that § 4706.5 was constitutional because Cal. Const. art. XIV, § 4 authorized the legislature to provide for payment of an award to the state in case of death of an employee without dependents. However, the court found that respondent department was not entitled to a portion of the dependency benefits because § 4706.5 only applied when an employee died leaving no dependants. The court directed respondent board to enter an order denying the claim of respondent department.


OUTCOME: On writ of review, the supplemental decision of respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board granting a portion of dependency benefits to respondent Department of Industrial Relations was annulled, because the plain language of the statute authorizing a portion of dependents to respondent department was not applicable when an employee died leaving any dependents.

People v. Gonda


Crim. No. 19102.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division One


119 Cal. App. 3d 67; 173 Cal. Rptr. 398; 1981 Cal. App. LEXIS 1729



Apr. 27, 1981 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted May 27, 1981 (See 138 C.A.3d 774)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 97359, S. Lee Vavuris, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged their convictions by the Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco (California) on numerous counts involving unlawful offering and selling franchises without prior registration under Cal. Corp. Code § 31110, selling franchises by means of untrue statements under Cal. Corp. Code § 31201, and grand theft under Cal. Penal Code § 487.


OVERVIEW: Appellants, found guilty on various counts related to their business and franchisees, involving unlawful offering and selling franchises without prior registration under Cal. Corp. Code § 31110, selling franchises by means of untrue statements under Cal. Corp. Code § 31201, and grand theft under Cal. Penal Code § 487, challenged the convictions. The court reversed the convictions, concluding that certain procedural and jury instructional errors deprived appellants of a fair trial. The court held that the jury was required to find an intent to sell securities and an intent to defraud when considering the theft charges. The court found that the evidence in support of the theft allegations was feeble, and a misplaced jury instruction telling the jury that ignorance of the law was no defense, was prejudicially confusing to the jury, warranting reversal. The court also found that the trial court erred when it refused to allow defendants' to produce evidence that they relied on counsel advice that certain conduct was lawful in defense of the charges of failure to register a franchise for sale.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the convictions, finding that jury instructions on ignorance of the law given for the theft counts was prejudicially confusing, and defendants should have been permitted to introduce evidence that they relied on counsel advice about the lawfulness of a matter for the charges of failure to register a franchise.

Board of Medical Quality Assurance v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 5836


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


114 Cal. App. 3d 272; 170 Cal. Rptr. 468; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2636



December 31, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] The petition of real party in interest for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 18, 1981.



DISPOSITION: Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue directing the Superior Court of Fresno County to conduct a hearing to determine if the stay order issued on June 18, 1980, in action No. 256506-7 should be continued pursuant to Code of Civil Procedure section 1094.5, subdivision (h), as herein construed. Insofar as it seeks relief not granted above, the petition for writ of mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner Board of Medical Quality Assurance sought a writ of mandate to vacate the order from the Superior Court of Fresno County (California), that issued a temporary stay of petitioner's revocation of the medical license of real party in interest physician under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5(h)(1) pending the review of petitioner's revocation decision.


OVERVIEW: After petitioner Board of Medical Quality Assurance revoked real party in interest physician's license, real party sought review of the decision and requested an order temporarily staying the revocation pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. § 1094.5. The trial court stayed petitioner's revocation order pursuant to Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1094.5(h)(1) because real party made a prima facie showing of a viable defense. Petitioner sought a writ of mandate to vacate the order because the trial court erroneously interpreted § 1094.5(h)(1). In response, real party argued that the stricter standard in § 1094.5(h)(1) denied him equal protection. The appellate court found that the proper standard of § 1094.5(h)(1) mandated a preliminary assessment of the merits of real party's petition for review and a conclusion that he was likely to obtain relief. The stricter standard of § 1094.5(h)(1) was rationally related to protecting the influential relationship between a physician and the patients. The appellate court issued a peremptory writ of mandate and directed the trial court to conduct a hearing to determine if the stay order should be continued pursuant to the proper standard of § 1094.5(h)(1).


OUTCOME: The appellate court issued a peremptory writ of mandate because the trial court applied the wrong standard when it stayed petitioner Board of Medical Quality Assurance's decision to revoke real party in interest physician's license and directed the trial court to make a preliminary assessment of the merits of real party's petition for review and conclude that real party was likely to obtain relief on his petition for the stay to continue.

Music Plus Four, Inc. v. Barnet


Civ. No. 21524


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


114 Cal. App. 3d 113; 170 Cal. Rptr. 419; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2623



December 29, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 13, 1981, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied March 18, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Orange County, No. 313765, Alicemarie H. Stotler, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The ordinance satisfies equal protection standards. The judgment enjoining enforcement of the ordinance is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a chief of police and other representatives of the city of Westminster, California, challenged a decision of the Superior Court of Orange County (California) in favor of respondent corporation issuing a preliminary injunction enjoining enforcement of ordinance No. 1878 of the city of Westminster.


OVERVIEW: Respondent corporation, which operated retail stores, brought an action against defendants, a chief of police and other representatives of the city of Westminster, California, to enjoin ordinance No. 1878 of the city, which required the exclusion of minors from any room where certain items, including drug paraphernalia, were sold or displayed. Respondent obtained a preliminary injunction enjoining both civil and criminal enforcement of the ordinance on the grounds that the state had preempted the field and that the ordinance was unconstitutionally vague, among others. The court reversed the judgment enjoining the ordinance, holding that the state had not preempted the field of regulation of businesses displaying drug paraphernalia to minors. The court further held that government regulation designed to protect children from exploitation by traffickers in drugs and drug-related products was a goal of the highest priority and that the ordinance was reasonably necessary to the accomplishment of that compelling government interest. The court also held that the ordinance was not impermissibly vague and that it satisfied the equal protection and due process standards.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court that enjoined the applicable ordinance in favor of respondent corporation. The court held, inter alia, that government regulation designed to protect children from exploitation by traffickers in drugs and drug-related products was a goal of the highest priority and that the ordinance was reasonably necessary to the accomplishment of that compelling government interest.

City of Los Angeles v. Wilshire Crest Medical Group


Civ. No. 57741


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


113 Cal. App. 3d 887; 170 Cal. Rptr. 325; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2598



December 23, 1980

DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles (California), which granted judgment in favor of respondent city in an action to collect the amount of tax owed by appellant.


OVERVIEW: Appellant taxpayer used the cash method of accounting to measure the amount of business tax owed on his gross receipts that he reported to respondent city. Respondent audited appellant's business records under the accrual method of accounting and levied an assessment for the difference in the amount paid by appellant and determined that appellant owed taxes. The assessment was affirmed in an administrative hearing and respondent sought recovery of the assessment in the trial court. The trial court entered judgment in favor of respondent and appellant sought review and asserted that the use of the term gross receipts in Los Angeles Cal. Municipal Code § 21.14 was ambiguous. On review the court held that gross receipts included both the dollar amounts received by appellant and the total amount charged for the service provided by appellant. The court found that the statute was not ambiguous and affirmed judgment for respondent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in favor of respondent city. Appellant taxpayer owed the business tax assessed by respondent because appellant's gross receipts included both money received and charges for services performed by appellant.

People v. De Anda


Crim. No. 37215


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


114 Cal. App. 3d 480; 170 Cal. Rptr. 830; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2640



December 22, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied January 7, 1981, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied February 18, 1981.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Ventura County, No. CR 14405, Charles R. McGrath, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which committed him to a mental facility for 90 days pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1026 after he was found not guilty of various charges by reason of insanity.


OVERVIEW: Appellant stabbed his wife and then stabbed himself, and he was found not guilty of resulting charges by reason of insanity. The trial court ordered appellant to confinement in a mental facility for 90 days pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1026, because it was not convinced that appellant had regained his sanity. Appellant challenged the confinement order. On review, the court determined that appellant still required medication and counseling, that appellant had been acquitted of an offense that was extremely violent and posed a serious threat of bodily injury to another, and that neither the trial court nor the county mental health director had the discretion to release appellant on an outpatient status prior to the 90-day institutional evaluation required by Cal. Penal Code § 1026. The court found that the statute was not unconstitutional and that 90 day confinement did not constitute a harsh result. The court affirmed appellant's confinement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's confinement to a mental facility for a period of 90 days, having determined that the trial court had no discretion in the matter, and that the 90-day stay was mandatory as a matter of law.

Crawford v. Board of Education


Civ. No. 60000


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Two


113 Cal. App. 3d 633; 170 Cal. Rptr. 495; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2575



December 19, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 14, 1981, As Modified January 15, 1981 and the Petition of Plaintiffs and Respondents for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied March 11, 1981. Bird, C. J., was of the Opinion that the Petition should be Granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. C 822854, Paul W. Egly, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The orders of May 19, 1980, and July 7, 1980, are vacated, and the matter is remanded to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with this opinion. It is not our intent to disrupt the operation of the schools during the present semester. Since our decision will not become final for 30 days, the parties have ample time to take whatever legal action they think desirable prior to the commencement of the next school semester. Our previous orders dated August 6, 1980, and September 4, 1980, staying certain features of the trial court's plan, are continued in force until further order of the court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Board of Education of the City of Los Angeles sought review of the decision of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which required appellant to prepare a reasonably feasible plan to alleviate the harmful consequences of racial segregation in the schools within its district.


OVERVIEW: The trial court entered orders in which it declined to rule on the constitutionality of Proposition 1, Cal. Const. art. I, § 7, regarding pupil school assignment and transportation, and in which it required substantial mandatory reassignment and transportation of pupils in appellant Board of Education of the City of Los Angeles's district. Appellant sought review of the orders. The issues before the court were whether Proposition 1 violated the U.S. Constitution, and whether it should be applied to appellant's attempts to desegregate the school district. The court found that Proposition 1 was valid under the U.S. Constitution and that its application to desegregate the school system was proper under U.S. Const. amend. XIV. The court reversed the trial court, which had required appellant to establish a reasonably feasible plan to alleviate harmful segregation, and remanded the case to determine a plan under Proposition 1.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the orders relating to pupil reassignment and transportation to remedy segregation in appellant Board of Education of the City of Los Angeles' district, and remanded the matter to the trial court. The court held that the constitutional proposition at issue did not violate the federal constitution and that, as the proposition applied to the desegregation plan for appellant, it was constitutional under the Fourteenth Amendment.
