County of Placer v. Corin


Civ. No. 19620


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


113 Cal. App. 3d 443; 170 Cal. Rptr. 232; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2559



December 17, 1980 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a peremptory writ of mandate issue commanding respondent to mail appropriate notices of assessment on and collect such assessments from the owners of real property in the Tierra Heights Sewer Assessment District A-79 as provided by law.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county sought a writ of mandate in the Court of Appeal, Third Appellate District (California), to require respondent county treasurer to serve and collect special assessments from property owners for sewer improvements. The issues were whether special assessments and the acceptance of federal grants by petitioner were sufficiently similar to "proceeds of taxes" to be prohibited under Cal. Const. art. XIII B.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner county sought a writ of mandate to require respondent county treasurer to serve and collect special assessments for sewer improvements. The court held that proceeds from special assessments were not within the not restricted to language of proceeds of taxes in Cal. Const. art. XIII B, § 8(c). There was a distinction between a tax levied by the legislature for the general public good and a special assessment levied within a limited area to force the payment of a benefit which would enhance the value of the property taxed. Strictly speaking, special assessments were not taxes at all. With the exception of state subventions, the prohibition within art. XIII B, § 8(c) was on general assessments. This conclusion was supported by the ballot arguments in favor of the initiative. The doctrine of ejusdem generis could not be used to include them. Art. XIII B was not designed to close any loopholes in Cal. Const. art. XIII A. Under ejusdem generis, federal grants were not sufficiently similar to state subventions to be included within the "proceeds of taxes." Construing federal grants as "proceeds of taxes" would not further the purposes of art. XIII B. The court issued the writ.


OUTCOME: The court issued petitioner county's writ to respondent county treasurer, holding that the proceeds derived from special assessments were not included within the not restricted to language of "proceeds of taxes" within the constitutional article, and as such, were validly collected. As used in the article, "proceeds of taxes" contemplated only those impositions which raised general tax revenues for the entity raising them.

 

1580 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Barnum


Crim. No. 20215


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


113 Cal. App. 3d 340; 169 Cal. Rptr. 840; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2548



December 16, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Alameda County, No. H 387, Martin N. Pulich, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of conviction is affirmed. The case is remanded to the trial court with directions to resentence appellant pursuant to Penal Code section 12308 as it read at the time of the offense.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of a judgment of the Superior Court of Alameda County (California) that convicted appellant of first degree attempted murder, upon his plea of nolo contendere, and sentenced appellant pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 664.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was found guilty of attempted murder, stipulated to be of the first degree on his plea of nolo contendere, and was sentenced to five years in prison, pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 664. The appeals court affirmed the conviction and reversed and remanded the sentence. The court held that the trial court did not err when it denied appellant's motion to suppress evidence that was seized pursuant to a search warrant. Sufficient facts were contained in the underlying affidavit to justify resolving the doubt in favor of the magistrate's finding of probable cause. Appellant's contention that the warrant as issued was unconstitutionally overbroad was not supported by the evidence. Appellant's sentence pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 664 was in error. As Cal. Penal Code § 12308 was intended for the punishment of appellant's specific kind of criminal attempt, Cal. Penal Code § 664 should not have been used in fixing punishment for appellant's conduct. The case was remanded to the trial court for resentencing pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 12308.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed a judgment of the superior court that convicted appellant of first degree attempted murder, upon his plea of nolo contendere. The court reversed appellant's sentence, holding that the trial court erred in applying a general sentencing statute when Cal. Penal Code § 12308 provided for the punishment of appellant's specific kind of criminal attempt. The case was remanded for resentencing.

 

1581 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Hustedt v. Workers' Comp. Appeals Bd.


Civ. No. 59010.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA, SECOND APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION TWO


113 Cal. App. 3d 217; 169 Cal. Rptr. 787; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2537



December 12, 1980 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hg. granted Feb. 23, 1981 (See 30 C.3d 329)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner attorney filed an extraordinary writ to prohibit respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board (California) from proceeding further in two pending actions to have petitioner found in contempt and to have petitioner removed, suspended, or denied privilege to appear in proceeding before respondent, pursuant to Cal. Lab. Code § 4907.


OVERVIEW: Respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board brought actions against petitioner attorney to have petitioner found in contempt and to have petitioner removed, suspended, or denied privilege to appear in proceeding before respondent, pursuant to Cal. Lab. Code § 4907. Petitioner filed an extraordinary writ to prohibit respondent from proceeding further in the two pending matters. The appeals court denied the petition, holding that Cal. Lab. Code § 4907 was not unconstitutional when it granted respondent the power to discipline licensed attorneys by way of contempt or otherwise. The court held that respondent's power to suspend or revoke the privilege of any person to appear before it in a representative capacity did not constitute a regulation of the practice of law and, thus, did not unconstitutionally impinge on the authority of the Supreme Court. Accordingly, the petition was denied and the contempt action was to proceed without prejudice.


OUTCOME: The court denied petitioner attorney's extraordinary writ to prohibit respondent Workers' Compensation Appeals Board from proceeding in actions to have petitioner found in contempt and removed, suspended, or denied privilege to appear in proceeding before respondent, holding that respondent's power to suspend or revoke the privilege of any person to appear before it was not a regulation of the practice of law and was not unconstitutional.

 

1582 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Allen v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 5613


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


113 Cal. App. 3d 42; 169 Cal. Rptr. 608; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2519



December 9, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A Petition for a Rehearing was Denied January 7, 1981, and on January 8, 1981, the Opinion was Modified to Read as Printed Above. Petitioner's Application for a Hearing by the Supreme Court was Denied February 5, 1981. Bird, C. J., and Mosk, J., were of the Opinion that the Application should be Granted.



DISPOSITION: The petition for writ of prohibition and/or mandate is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Criminal petitioner sought a writ of prohibition and/or mandate directing respondent Superior Court of Kern County (California) to strike the special circumstance allegation that the murder charged was especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel, manifesting exceptional depravity.


OVERVIEW: Criminal petitioner was charged with willful, unlawful, felonious and malice aforethought murder. The information further alleged the murder was especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel, manifesting exceptional depravity pursuant to Cal. Penal Code §190.2 (a)(14)). Following arraignment he filed a demurrer to the information on the grounds that the special circumstance allegation could not be filed in the absence of charging first degree murder, and it was unconstitutionally vague under due process requirements. The court held that the special-circumstance allegations did not have to be stricken because the prosecution failed to charge petitioner with first degree murder. The court also held that the special circumstance contained in § 190.2 (a)(14) was not vague under either U.S. Const. amend XIV or the due process clause of the California constitution. The petition for writ of prohibition and/or mandate was denied.


OUTCOME: The court denied criminal petitioner's writ of prohibition and/or mandate to strike the special circumstance allegation that the murder charged was especially heinous, atrocious, or cruel, manifesting exceptional depravity. The special-circumstance allegations did not have to be stricken because the prosecution failed to charge petitioner with first-degree murder, and the statute was constitutional.

 

1583 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Pratt


Crim. No. 37534


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


112 Cal. App. 3d 795; 170 Cal. Rptr. 80; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2611



December 3, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified December 8, 1980. A petition for a rehearing was denied December 30, 1980. Dunn, J., * was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


* Assigned by the Chairperson of the Judicial Council.



DISPOSITION: The order to show cause is discharged and the petition for writ of habeas corpus is denied. 45



45 For the sake of completeness, we are not unaware of two actions defendant Pratt has pending in federal courts, one of which is in Washington, D.C. under the FOI Act seeking further disclosure of FBI files pertaining to Cointelpro and the defendant herein. The other action is in a prison civil rights suit in the United States District Court in San Francisco in which FBI files have been subpoenaed from August 1968 to August 1972 pertaining to Pratt.

Our conclusions and holdings herein are based on the information and documentation supplied this court by defense counsel, the California Attorney General's office and the FBI as of the date of filing this opinion. Should additional evidence be uncovered in the future from the above sources which adequately and legally support defendant's contentions, our determinations herein do not, of course, preclude the filing of a new petition seeking appropriate relief. ( In re Swain, supra, 34 Cal.2d 300.)

Re Dissenting Opinion: The dissenting opinion concludes that defendant Pratt should be granted an "evidentiary hearing" because he was not afforded "due process of law" in that his trial in 1972 was not fundamentally fair. Such a conclusion clearly is not supported by the record and is predicated on a selective reading of the record and misconstruction and misapplication of controlling law.

"Due process," simply stated, requires notice and an opportunity to be heard by an impartial tribunal under applicable rules of general law. The in-depth and exhaustive analysis of the total record in conjunction with the application of settled rules of law which govern original habeas corpus proceedings as discussed in this majority opinion amply demonstrates that defendant Pratt's conviction was in full accordance with "due process" requirements and that he has simply failed to make a sufficient showing to justify any relief whatsoever.

The dissenting opinion has bought hook-line-and-sinker defendant's arguments in his petition which when dissected and analyzed have as little substance as a handful of fog. Reliance on some nebulous concept that a fair trial was denied defendant based solely on allegations which are wholly speculative and conjectural and admittedly unproven not only defies logic but ignores the standards set by the Supreme Court in In re Imbler, supra, 60 Cal.2d 554. Again, mere allegations of "governmental improprieties" based on pure surmise and speculation sans supporting facts are insufficient to support the granting of relief. (See In re Lawler, supra, 23 Cal.3d 190.)

Defendant's petition for habeas corpus in an original proceeding before this court is primarily directed at "governmental improprieties" on the federal level by the FBI. Inquiry at an evidentiary hearing into the nine items listed at the end of the dissenting opinion would be nothing more than a "fishing expedition" into state waters burning up additional court time and resulting in an unnecessary expenditure of public funds. Defendant already has two "lines" into federal waters by way of an FOI Act case and a civil rights action in federal courts in Washington, D.C., and San Francisco. As previously noted if those "fishing expeditions" uncover evidence of a sufficient quantity and quality to adequately and legally support his contentions, he is not precluded from filing a new petition seeking appropriate relief. ( In re Swain, supra, 34 Cal.2d 300.)

The dissent's items 1, 2 and 3 listed for inquiry at an evidentiary hearing pertain to FBI informants in the defense camp during the trial. The dissent's argument in relation to Barber v. Municipal Court, supra, 24 Cal.3d 742, is unintelligible. We need not repeat here the total of six major distinguishing factors and reasons listed in the body of this majority opinion demonstrating that Barber is clearly inapplicable to the instant case. In response to the dissent's reliance on Barber, we add that such a hearing would be useless since it would be held to answer questions with a presently known answer and a known result. There is no dispute that FBI informants (as distinguished from local law enforcement informants) were in the defense camp and no doubt that the FBI would refuse to disclose their identities in order to protect the FBI informants' lives. Moreover, Barber's sole remedy is a per se dismissal of the underlying charges. This in no way authorizes a carte blanche evidentiary hearing on matters unrelated to the issue of informants in the defense camp being privy to discussions between defendant and counsel concerning defense trial strategy or tactics. Nor does the dissent explain how California can constitutionally apply to a federal agency its ruling in Barber which admittedly is at odds with the United States Supreme Court's decision in Weatherford v. Bursey, supra, 429 U.S. 545. Again, critical here is the fact that while the FBI informants may have had access to defense strategies the information obtained was of such a general nature as to be of no aid to the prosecution and was not detrimental to the defense; in any event it was not transmitted to the local prosecuting attorney for use during the trial.

It would appear, therefore, as indicated in footnote 43, ante, that the only purpose in seeking to have a California court order the FBI to disclose the names of its informants is a tactical maneuver by Pratt's counsel to attempt to obliterate the distinction between federal and state informants and to persuade some trial court to blaze a new trial by dismissing the underlying murder charge against Pratt and thus gain his release from prison irrespective of his jury conviction of the "tennis court murder."

The dissent's item 4 pertains to prosecution witness Julius (Julio) Butler which is discussed in great detail in the body of this majority opinion and needs no further amplification except that, as noted, Butler did not consider himself an informant (snitch) and Sergeant Rice testified that Butler furnished information to the L.A.P.D. Moreover, assuming, arguendo, that Butler did lie in respect to his informant status, the case of People v. Ruthford, supra, 14 Cal.3d 399, relied on in the dissent was an appeal case as distinguished from a case arising out of a petition for habeas corpus, it does not stand for the proposition that any false testimony is reversible error. Furthermore, if on appeal a defendant must establish the substantial materiality of the suppressed evidence, which was not established here, a lesser standard certainly should not be applied to a habeas corpus postconviction proceeding. Nor does the dissenting opinion explain how Butler's denial of the fact that he was an FBI informant (if he was one) relate to Butler's interest in testifying truthfully or falsely as to Pratt's admissions to him, the truth of which was corroborated by other prosecution witnesses.

The dissent's item 5 poses the question: "Was the petitioner a subject focused on for 'neutralization' by Cointelpro prior to trial in 1972?" Of course, he was.

There is no question the Pratt became the subject of Cointelpro's efforts to "neutralize" him after his assumption of a leadership position in the LA BPP in April 1969. However, there is no showing whatsoever, either in the Church Committee Report or before this court, that Cointelpro's counterintelligence measures and techniques for "neutralization," the use of leaflets and fake letters which were employed "to deny unity of action in the effort of the LA BPP" by splitting the BPP and turning the leaders against each other included a technique of "framing" Pratt of the "tennis court murder." In fact the Church Committee Report states that all Cointelpro activities were officially terminated on April 27, 1971, * over a year before Pratt's trial in 1972. Nor does the dissent otherwise establish how the fact that Pratt was an object of Cointelpro as described in the body of this majority opinion was at all relevant to the "tennis court murder" of which he was convicted.

* The Church Committee Report states the "all Cointelpros were officially terminated on April 27, 1971." The Committee requested that the FBI provide it with a list of any "Cointelpro-type" actions since April 28, 1971. The Bureau located and furnished the Committee two instances of posttermination Cointelpro-type operations. One instance involved an FBI contact with the editor of a Southern newspaper to arrange to have reporters interview KKK members and write an article based on those interviews while furnishing information on Klan use of the polygraph to "weed out FBI informants." The result of the subsequent publication of the Klan activities resulted in a number of Klan officials ceasing their activities. The other instance involved an anonymous letter and derogatory newspaper clipping sent to a BPP officer in the Northeast to discredit a Panther leader's activities. The committee discovered a third instance where an attorney's political background was furnished to a friendly newspaper intended to discredit an attorney and his client. (P. 13.) None pertain to the BPP on the west coast and none pertain to Pratt and none involve "framing" anyone.

As to item 6, it is common knowledge that law enforcement agencies at various levels of government (city, county, state and federal) share or pass along information about specific crimes and criminal activity to each other. Such a procedure not only conforms to long accepted practice and is completely proper in all respects but is highly desirable in the war against rampant criminal activity. Here, there is no showing by defense counsel that the prosecution failed to supply any information at its disposal to which the defense may have been legally entitled.

In addition, although it is somewhat unclear for what purpose the dissent relies upon People v. Mejia (1976) 57 Cal.App.3d 574 [129 Cal.Rptr. 192], such reliance is misplaced as there is no showing that the FBI or the prosecution separately or in concert deported, hid or failed to produce any material witnesses. The Court of Appeal in Mejia (57 Cal.App.3d at p. 581) stated: "If the federal government, unilaterally and without the knowledge and aid of state authorities, deports aliens who later turn out to be material witnesses in state court criminal proceedings, the distinction urged by the People might well be valid. That is not the case presented here. There can be no question but that state action initiated and was at least partially responsible for the fact Velasquez and Arce became unavailable as witnesses . . . ."

The dissent by its item 7 refers to a matter not properly before this court in these original proceedings. It apparently refers to an intelligence report from the L.A.P.D. dated March 28, 1971, which had been sealed at the defense request. This document was viewed in camera by this panel on its own motion on August 27, 1980. The portion which the dissent evidently refers to pertains to the second suspect in the "tennis court murder" (presumably the person named "Tyrone" who was introduced to Butler by Pratt on the evening preceding the robbery-murder). This report was supplied Judge Kathleen Parker by Pratt's defense counsel for purposes of the defendant's previous petition for habeas corpus. Presumably to avoid public exposure of the contents in the report which shows conduct inconsistent with Pratt's claimed innocence, defense counsel Stuart Hanlon obtained an ex parte protective order from Federal Magistrate Frederick J. Woelflen releasing the document for an in camera inspection by Judge Parker only. The order contained the request by Magistrate Woelflen that his court be contacted "before releasing said document to the prosecution or to any other persons." This court, in view of the dissent's reference to the second person involved in the Santa Monica shooting to which the FBI and the L.A.P.D. had some knowledge, requested permission telephonically of Magistrate Woelflen to disclose the contents of the protected document. This request was granted by Magistrate Woelflen as it would have no effect on the pending federal case.

The above referred to intelligence report dated March 28, 1971, contains a brief statement that "(source deleted) advised that a brother named (deleted), last name unknown, assisted Elmer Pratt in the robbery and murder on the Santa Monica tennis court." The source recalled that the second suspect had been arrested in a stolen Cadillac but the cases were dismissed, mentioned the area in which the suspect hung out and a girl friend but ends with the statement: "When Pratt was indicted for the shooting, (deletion) received orders to split from Los Angeles." The balance of the lengthy intelligence report refers to another murder "on order from National Headquarters, Berkeley, California" because the victim had been "screwing around with (deletion) and because he was dropping too many 'Bennies,' screwing up him [sic] mind, and a security risk to the Black Panther Party." The main portion of this lengthy report, in addition to the mention of the murder of a special officer and bombings by Black Panthers (names deleted), contained information of elaborate plans between defendant Pratt and others outside the jail to effect Pratt's and other Black Panthers' escape from the county jail, included the use of weapons against police officers if necessary and for a switch to "cold" cars (other vehicles) a short distance from the area of escape to "hideout pads"; although there was evidence of a "dry run" prior to the date scheduled for the jail break, the escape plan was never executed. The report also includes information from informants indicating a plot to kidnap public officials to be "released upon receipt of a one-way ticket via Airline Algeria for (names deleted)."

Since the second suspect (presumably Tyrone) had "split from Los Angeles," there is no showing that the prosecution knew how he could be located. As the evidence is undisputed that two male Black Panthers were involved in the "tennis court murder," defendant Pratt would be in a better position than the police to locate the other suspect who "split." In addition, it is obvious that the information contained in the report is indicative of Pratt's guilt rather than of his innocence in that he made plans to escape from jail.

The dissent's item 8 selects on question addressed to victim Kenneth Olsen and his answer out of the lengthy report's transcript and refers to a declaration by Deputy Public Defender Laurence E. Rivitz indicating that Mr. Olsen had previously identified two other suspects in a lineup.

The dissent acknowledges that the issue of the Rivitz declaration was raised in 1972 at defendant's motion for a new trial and rejected by the trial court. The issue was not specifically raised on appeal (see Appen. B) but was apparently before Judge Parker again on defendant's recent petition for habeas corpus before her court which was denied. Defendant has not included that issue in his original proceedings before this court. The dissent has, therefore, resurrected the issue although it is not properly before this court. In any event, this information was known to Pratt at the time of his appeal and was not raised. Habeas corpus is not a vehicle for raising contentions which could have been made on appeal but which were not. The burden is upon the defendant to give a satisfactory reason for not raising the issue on appeal ( In re Walker (1974) 10 Cal.3d 764 [112 Cal.Rptr. 177, 518 P.2d 1129]) and even if the allegations could be considered under the rules applicable to habeas corpus the defendant's unreasonable and unexplained delay in raising the issue precludes relief. (See In re Wells (1967) 67 Cal.2d 873 [64 Cal.Rptr. 317, 343 P.2d 613].)

As to the dissent's item 9, see footnote 43, ante.

A basic concept built into our Constitution is that if changes are to be made in our form of government and its major duly constituted institutions that such changes are to be brought about only by peaceful legitimate means through the political process at the ballot box and not by bullets and bombs. The record before us reflects that the FBI perceived the BPP as a violence-prone black (racist) hate organization whose means of revolutionary changes included the use of bullets and bombs and literally at war with law enforcement officers (see Appen. D ). As the Church Committee Report indicated, the FBI had few if any guidelines to go by and took the initiative by putting into place Cointelpro to meet that internal threat.

Defendant Pratt's petition in the instant case is hollow. It follows the too often typical pattern in today's upside-down system of criminal justice where a defendant himself charged with or convicted of such "illegal acts," such as murder, attempted murder and robbery in the instant case, seeks to focus attention on the alleged "illegal acts" of law enforcement officers. Without commenting on the propriety of the techniques and measures used by Cointelpro, relevant here is the fact that there is no showing that FBI's Cointelpro techniques included the "framing" of defendant Pratt for the "tennis court murder."

In the case at bench defendant Pratt was only entitled to a fair trial, not a perfect trial. The record reflects that he was fairly tried and justly convicted by due process of law.

Finally, the dissent by referring to selective portions of the record instead of the total record and ignoring controlling precedent has apparently succumbed to the discredited "ideal of perfectibility" which is "the concept that with the expenditure of sufficient time, patience, energy, and money it is possible eventually to achieve perfect justice in all legal process." Such a "noble ideal has consistently spawned results that can only be described as pandemoniac" in our criminal justice system.

"[In] our pursuit of the will-o-the-wisp of perfectibility, we necessarily neglect other elements of an effective procedure, notably the resolution of controversies within a reasonable time, at a reasonable cost, with reasonable uniformity, and under settled rules of law.

". . . .

"The ideal of perfectibility denies the existence of price and cost, and, at least, in criminal procedure, relies heavily on the argument that no sacrifice is too great when human life or liberty is involved. Better that a hundred guilty men should go free than that once innocent man be convicted, is the rallying cry of the perfectionists. But this slogan gets us no further than does its obverse -- better that one life should be sacrificed that a hundred others may be saved. The plain fact of the matter is that in human affairs we balance the cost of human life against other considerations in almost everything we do, and it is incorrect to say that the sacrifice of human life to attain particular ends is never justified. The real question is one of relative values -- is the end in view worth the price it is likely to cost?

"But, the perfectionists argue, no sacrifice is too great to assure that in a given case perfect justice will be done. Ignored is the sacrifice of the legal order itself and of the life, liberty, and property of those that the legal order is designated to protect. Ignored also is the necessity that the procedure we follow lend substance to the moral and ethical idea that those who take up the sword shall perish by the sword." (Fleming, The Price of Perfect Justice -- The Adverse Consequences of Current Legal Doctrine on the American Courtroom (1974) pp. 6-9.)

 [***2]


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a habeas corpus petition for release from prison after his convictions for first-degree murder, under Cal. Penal Code § 187, two counts of first-degree robbery, under Cal. Penal Code § 211, and assault with intent to commit murder, under Cal. Penal Code § 217, alleging that witnesses at his trial committed perjury, there was an FBI spy in his defense group, and the FBI withheld exculpatory evidence.


OVERVIEW: Defendant filed an original habeas corpus petition seeking his release from prison following his convictions of first-degree murder, under Cal. Penal Code § 187, two counts of first-degree robbery, under Cal. Penal Code § 211, and assault with intent to commit murder, under Cal. Penal Code § 217. Defendant alleged that the Federal Bureau of Investigation (FBI) had a spy on his defense team, which mandated a dismissal of the charges, and that he was entitled to a new trial or evidentiary hearing because two prosecution witnesses lied, and that the FBI concealed evidence that corroborated defendant's alibi. The court denied the petition for habeas corpus. The court found that the facts of the case did not mandate a dismissal because any leak of information from the defense was of a general nature and procured by the FBI, a federal agency not under the court's purview. The court held that the allegations of perjury by the prosecution's witnesses were unfounded. The court decided that the FBI did not have defendant under surveillance nor did it conceal evidence to exculpate defendant, but in fact, any information the FBI had tended to inculpate defendant.


OUTCOME: The court denied the habeas corpus petition, holding that the facts of the case indicated that the prosecution had no advance knowledge of the defense team's strategy; that the prosecution witnesses did not give perjured testimony; and that defendant did not prove that newly discovered evidence that the FBI had exculpatory evidence undermined the entire case against him.

 

1584 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Knueppel


Crim. No. 36969


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


117 Cal. App. 3d 958; 173 Cal. Rptr. 466; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2657



November 26, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Municipal Court for the Downey Judicial District of Los Angeles County, No. M40134, F. Lawrence Plotkin, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order of the municipal court sustaining defendant's demurrer and dismissing the complaint is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed the order of the Municipal Court for the Downey Judicial District of Los Angeles County (California), which sustained defendant's demurrer and dismissed the charges against defendant for soliciting without a permit under Downey, Cal., Municipal Code § 4500.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with soliciting without a permit in violation of Downey, Cal., Municipal Code § 4500. The court sustained defendant's demurrer and dismissed the complaint, and the state appealed. The court affirmed the lower court's order because § 4500 unduly interfered with the exercise of free speech under U.S. Const. amend. I. The court determined that the permit requirements of the ordinance failed to specify what standards other than truthfulness of the application could be used to assess the probability of fraud, and left that assessment to the absolute discretion of the chief of police. The court held that this type of broad administrative discretion had been repeatedly condemned, and the police chief's power under § 4500 rendered the ordinance unconstitutional. Therefore, the complaint against defendant based on § 4500 was properly dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the lower court that sustained defendant's demurrer and dismissed the charges for soliciting without a permit because the municipal ordinance under which defendant was charged violated the First Amendment guarantee of free speech.

 

1585 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Superior Court (Scott)


Civ. No. 60395


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


112 Cal. App. 3d 602; 169 Cal. Rptr. 412; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2486



November 25, 1980 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] The appellate department is directed to vacate its opinion and judgment herein, and to remand the underlying criminal prosecution to the municipal court for further proceedings therein consistent with the views expressed in this opinion.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The prosecution sought review from the granting by respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) of real party in interest driver's motion to suppress under Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5 due to the failure of arresting officers to give him a chemical blood test instead of a breath test.


OVERVIEW: After real party in interest driver was arrested for violation of Cal. Veh. Code § 23102, misdemeanor drunk driving, his repeated requests for a chemical blood test were denied and he was given a breath test. His motion to suppress pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 1538.5 was denied by the trial court but was granted upon review. On the state's appeal the court found that the suppression motion was actually a common law suppression motion because it rested on claimed constitutional violations other than unconstitutional search and seizure. Further, the denial of the blood test was a denial of due process where it prevented the obtaining of necessary evidence. Thus, the breath test results were inadmissible at trial. However, there was no unconstitutional search and seizure. Finally, the court found that a denial of a common law suppression motion was unreviewable pretrial, so the order was reversed and remanded for further proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded, holding that the denial of the blood test requested by real party in interest was a denial of due process preventing his preparation of his defense, but finding that no unconstitutional search and seizure had occurred and that the suppression motion was unreviewable pretrial.

 

1586 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Hughes


Crim. No. 20628


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Three


112 Cal. App. 3d 452; 169 Cal. Rptr. 364; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2468



November 21, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Santa Cruz County, No. 68634, Christopher C. Cottle, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a judgment of the Superior Court of Santa Cruz County (California), which sentenced him to a total of four years in prison after he entered a plea of nolo contendere to a charge of forgery.


OVERVIEW: Appellant's sole contention on appeal was that the base term of two years for forgery should not have been enhanced with an additional two years pursuant to Cal. Penal Code § 12022.6(b), which provided for such enhancement where the value of the property taken exceeded $ 100,000. The court found that § 12022.6 in combination with Cal. Penal Code § 470 resulted in varying treatment of persons who committed forgery but for equal protection purposes the statutes in question did not create classifications but defined "different offenses." The court found that the aggregation of the 23 forgeries originally charged into one count and the consequent enhancement under section 12022.6 did not violate Cal. Penal Code § 654, because the notion that appellant engaged in an indivisible course of conduct and committed but one offense was a question of fact that appellant waived by his nolo contendere plea. The court affirmed and found that the four-year sentence was not cruel and unusual punishment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment, which sentenced appellant to four years in prison. The court concluded that the sentencing enhancement statute was valid and did not violate appellant's constitutional rights.

 

1587 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Nicholas


Crim. No. 18506


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


112 Cal. App. 3d 249; 169 Cal. Rptr. 497; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2452



November 19, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Modified November 24, 1980. A petition for a rehearing was denied December 19, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Contra Costa County, No. 21386, Richard E. Arnason, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment entered in the Superior Court, Contra Costa County (California), which convicted him of two counts of first degree murder and two counts of first degree robbery, while armed and using a firearm, and finding him sane as to each count.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of two counts of first degree murder and two counts of first degree robbery, while armed and using a firearm, and he was found sane. Defendant challenged. The court held that the arrest was lawful because the officers had probable cause, and a warrant was not required. Defendant's confession was illegally obtained because the officers used coercive tactics and impliedly threatened him. Furthermore, defendant's Miranda rights were violated because he invoked his privilege against self-incrimination when he requested to turn off the tape recorder and to speak with an officer alone in complete privacy. However, the admission of the confession was harmless because of the overwhelming evidence and the many properly admitted confessions. The jury was not required to agree on the theory by which it found defendant guilty for the verdict to have been unanimous. There was no error in the failure to give an instruction on the correct insanity test during the sanity phase. When the jury rejected defendant's diminished capacity defense, they necessarily rejected the evidence that might have supported an insanity verdict. Accordingly, the judgment was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for two counts of first degree murder and two counts of first degree robbery, while armed and using a firearm, and the finding that he was sane because defendant's arrest was lawful, and the admission of the illegally obtained confession was harmless. Furthermore, the jury's verdict was unanimous as to guilt, and there was no error in failing to instruct the jury on the correct test for insanity.

 

1588 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Mazzola v. City and County of San Francisco


Civ. No. 45054


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Two


112 Cal. App. 3d 141; 169 Cal. Rptr. 127; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2442; 105 L.R.R.M. 3360



November 17, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied December 17, 1980, and respondents' petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied January 21, 1981. Richardson, J., was of the opinion that the petition should be granted.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of the City and County of San Francisco, No. 713522, John E. Benson, Judge.



DISPOSITION: Judgment is reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant airports commissioner sought review of an order of the Superior Court, San Francisco County (California), which denied his petition for a peremptory writ of administrative mandamus compelling respondent city to set aside its decision removing him from office for official misconduct.


OVERVIEW: Appellant airports commissioner also served as the business manager and financial secretary-treasurer of a local of the plumber's union. During his term as commissioner, appellant recommended that members of his union go on strike and supported the strike when it occurred. Respondent city removed appellant from office on the grounds of official misconduct. The trial court denied appellant's petition for peremptory writ of administrative mandamus. The court held that the appeal was not moot, even though appellant's term had expired. The court held that the term "official misconduct" was neither unconstitutionally vague nor uncertain. However, the court determined that appellant was not guilty of official misconduct because there was no relationship between appellant's alleged wrongdoing in supporting the strike and his duties as airports commissioner. The court held that respondent impliedly approved on any conflict of interest because respondent appointed appellant as airports commissioner with full knowledge of his union position. The court found the concept of fiduciary duty inapplicable to appellant's actions. Therefore, the court reversed the judgment of the trial court.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's denial of appellant airports commissioner's petition for a peremptory writ of administrative mandamus compelling respondent city to reinstate him because appellant did not commit official misconduct related to his support of a strike of the plumber's union.

People v. Matthews


Crim. No. 36420


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division One


112 Cal. App. 3d 11; 169 Cal. Rptr. 263; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2431



November 13, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. A 019584, Roy J. Brown, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of his convictions by the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) for illegal transportation of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11360(a).


OVERVIEW: Appellant's car was shipped to the United States from out of the country. A customs inspector went to the area where cars were held for routine inspection before being released into the country. His trained dog alerted him, which indicated the dog had picked up a scent of narcotics. The officer called for an inspector, who drilled two holes in the frame of the car and discovered hashish. All but a small amount of the contraband was removed, and the car was released to appellant for a controlled delivery. Upon arrival at his home, appellant was arrested. After he was convicted of illegal transportation of marijuana in violation of Cal. Health & Safety Code § § 11360(a), appellant sought review. Upon review, the court determined that the search of the vehicle was reasonable because it took place in a border area, where neither warrant nor probable cause was required. The court determined that appellant's statements, which were made after he was confronted by the evidence, were admissible. The court affirmed appellant's conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed appellant's conviction for illegal transportation of marijuana after determining that the search of appellant's vehicle in a border area was reasonable, that there was no evidence of bad faith, and that appellant's voluntary statements, made after he was confronted with the evidence, were admissible.

Conejo Valley Unified School Dist. v. William Blurock & Partners, Inc.


Civ. No. 57894


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


111 Cal. App. 3d 983; 169 Cal. Rptr. 102; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2426



November 12, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Ventura County, No. 68853, Steven J. Stone, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The order denying the petition for an order to compel arbitration under section 1281.2 and to consolidate proceedings under section 1281.3 is reversed, and the matter is remanded for proceedings consistent with the views set forth herein.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant school district challenged an order of the Superior Court of Ventura County (California), which denied its petition to compel arbitration with respondent architectural firm and to consolidate arbitration proceedings.


OVERVIEW: After a construction company filed a claim against appellant school district, claiming there were misrepresentations in plans prepared by respondent architectural firm, appellant sought indemnification from respondent and requested arbitration. Respondent argued that it was not required to arbitrate because no dispute had arisen under its agreement with appellant. The court held that if the construction company prevailed on its complaint, appellant could then force respondent to arbitrate a dispute as to whether respondent's plans caused the damages suffered by the construction company, and logic required for the dispute to be resolved in one proceeding. The court reversed because the retroactive operation of Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 1281.3, permitting consolidation of separate arbitration proceedings, did not deprive respondent of any vested right.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the denial of appellant school district's petition to compel arbitration because appellant could force respondent architectural firm to arbitration if a construction company prevailed on its complaint against appellant based on plans prepared by respondent.

 

1591 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Hillman v. Britton


Civ. No. 4030


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


111 Cal. App. 3d 810; 168 Cal. Rptr. 852; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2408



November 5, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Fresno County, No. 219700-2, Leonard Irving Meyers, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment of the trial court denying the preliminary injunction is reversed and remanded with directions for the preliminary injunction to issue as prayed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged a ruling of the Superior Court of Fresno County (California) that denied their request for a preliminary injunction against the enforcement of respondent city's solicitation licensing ordinance.


OVERVIEW: Respondent city passed an ordinance that required a license for charitable and religious solicitation. Without applying for a license, appellants engaged in charitable solicitations to support their church. When their efforts were interrupted by the threat of criminal action, appellants filed a civil rights suit to enjoin enforcement of the ordinance. The trial court denied appellants' request for a preliminary injunction, finding that the ordinance was valid on its face even though some of the standards justifying rejection of an application were not constitutionally acceptable. Appellants sought review. After finding that the threatened prosecution of appellants gave them standing to challenge the ordinance, the court reversed and remanded by holding that most of the ordinance's challenged standards were unconstitutionally vague and overbroad. Moreover, procedural safeguards were not provided to insure speedy issuance or denial of the application and swift and adequate remedy by appeal. Thus, appellants established a substantial likelihood that they would prevail on the merits of their cause. Therefore, appellants would suffer irreparable harm if the injunction did not issue.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the trial court's denial of appellants' application for a preliminary injunction against the enforcement of respondent city's solicitation licensing ordinance, holding that the threat of misdemeanor prosecution conferred standing upon appellants and the ordinance's vague standards violated appellants' constitutional rights to free speech. Moreover, the ordinance did not contain adequate procedural safeguards.

 

1592 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. North


Crim. No. 19393.


Court of Appeal of California, First Appellate District, Division Four


111 Cal. App. 3d 694; 168 Cal. Rptr. 775; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2395



November 4, 1980 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REVIEW


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Hearing Granted February 5, 1981 (See 29 Cal. 3d 509)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Santa Clara County, No. 70306, James A. Wright, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Santa Clara County Superior Court (California), that convicted him of second degree burglary, Cal. Penal Code § 459, arguing the evidence against him should have been suppressed because it was discovered by a private citizen acting jointly with a police officer in a warrantless search.


OVERVIEW: After defendant's motion to suppress evidence was denied, defendant pled guilty and was convicted of second degree burglary, Cal. Penal Code § 459. Defendant appealed, arguing the evidence against him was illegally obtained by a private citizen acting jointly with a police officer in a warrantless search. The court held that the private citizen was a participant in a joint operation with police because there was official participation in the warrantless search of defendant's vehicle. The court found that because the private citizen's entry into defendant's car furthered a state interest in addition to his own interest, it was part of the joint operation and constituted state action. As the private citizen's intrusion into defendant's car was prohibited under both the federal and California Constitutions, and as the private citizen's actions constituted state action, the evidence was illegally seized and should have been suppressed. Defendant's conviction was therefore reversed.


OUTCOME: Order convicting defendant of second degree burglary was reversed because a private citizen's unconstitutional search of defendant's car constituted state action, and evidence was thus illegally seized from defendant's car; the evidence should have been suppressed.

 

1593 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Calva Products v. Security Pacific Nat. Bank


Civ. No. 21580


Court of Appeal of California, Fourth Appellate District, Division Two


111 Cal. App. 3d 409; 168 Cal. Rptr. 582; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2365



October 28, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Riverside County, No. 114778, Robert K. Garst, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants corporations sought review of an order of the Superior Court of Riverside County (California), which entered a summary judgment in favor of respondent bank in appellants' action against respondent for conversion of cattle.


OVERVIEW: Appellant corporations purchased cattle from a dairy farm and contracted the farm to raise the cattle. Respondent bank had loaned the farm money and had a perfected security interest in the farm's cattle. Respondent subsequently foreclosed on the loan and took possession of the cattle for liquidation sale. Appellants put respondent on notice that they owned some of the cattle seized. Appellants then brought an action for conversion and respondent filed a motion for summary judgment. The trial court approved the motion and appellants sought review. On review the court affirmed. The court held that respondent's perfected security interest in the cattle prevailed over appellants' ownership interests in the cattle pursuant to Cal. Civ. Code § 2980.5. Further, respondent did not have notice of appellants' interests because they failed to record their agreement with the farm, as required by Cal. Civ. Code § 2980.5. The court also held that, contrary to appellants' argument, the provisions of Cal. Civ. Code § 2980.5, were constitutional and did not violate due process.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the summary judgment granted in favor of respondent bank, because respondent's perfected security interest in the cattle seized took priority over appellant corporations' ownership interests. Further, appellants failed to record their interest as required by statute.

Koshman v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 19480


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


111 Cal. App. 3d 294; 168 Cal. Rptr. 558; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2351



October 22, 1980 



DISPOSITION:  [***1] Let a writ of mandate issue directing the trial court to vacate its order of March 13, 1980, and enter a new order granting petitioner's motion to quash service of the subpoena. The alternative writ, having served its function, is discharged.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner mother sought writ of mandate to compel respondent Superior Court of Sacramento County (California) to quash subpoena for petitioner's medical records in a child custody dispute.


OVERVIEW: Real party in interest father sought subpoena for general medical records of petitioner mother in his change-of-custody proceedings in respondent Superior Court of Sacramento County. The general medical records were of a hospitalization for a condition known as "walking pneumonia." Respondent allowed discovery of the medical records by real party in interest. Petitioner sought writ of mandate to prevent discovery by the father of her general medical records, claiming that the physician-patient privilege prevented the discovery. Granting the writ of mandate quashing service of the subpoena for medical records, the court held that the physician-patient privilege was designed to protect personal relationships and other interests where public policy deems privilege more important than evidence. Therefore the physician-patient privilege protected the medical records from discovery in the parties' child custody dispute.


OUTCOME: The court granted the writ of mandate and directed that the subpoena for medical records of petitioner mother in child custody dispute be quashed because the physician-patient privilege protected the records from discovery.

Hooks v. State Personnel Bd.


Civ. No. 3988


Court of Appeal of California, Fifth Appellate District


111 Cal. App. 3d 572; 168 Cal. Rptr. 822; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2384



October 10, 1980 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Superior Court of Kern County, No. 149460, John Michael Nairn, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff correctional officer appealed the decision of the Superior Court of Kern County (California), which denied his petition for mandate seeking reconsideration of his dismissal for a possession conviction that occurred prior to his reinstatement from a prior dismissal.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff correctional officer was dismissed for alleged discourteous conduct. Prior to his reinstatement by the appellate court, plaintiff was arrested and convicted on his plea of guilty to possession of marijuana under Cal. Health & Safety Code § 11357 as a misdemeanor. Plaintiff's reinstatement with back pay went back to a date prior to the arrest. Plaintiff was dismissed for the possession conviction, defendant board denied his petition for reconsideration, and the trial court denied his petition for mandate. The court held that plaintiff was an employee throughout his absence following his first dismissal because the reinstatement related back to the date of dismissal. The court also found that the possession conviction was rationally related to his corrections work for dismissal as failure of good behavior under Cal. Gov't Code § 19572. The court found that the administrative agency did not abuse its discretion in dismissing plaintiff and affirmed the trial court's denial of plaintiff's petition for mandate.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed finding that reinstatement made plaintiff an employee when he was arrested for possession of marijuana, the conviction was rationally related to plaintiff's corrections work, and dismissal was not an abuse of the administrative agency's discretion.

 

1596 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Charles R. v. Superior Court


Civ. No. 58993


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Four


110 Cal. App. 3d 945; 168 Cal. Rptr. 284; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2278



October 6, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 27, 1980.



DISPOSITION: The petition is denied.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought a writ of mandate from an order of respondent Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that found petitioner unfit to be tried as a juvenile and remanded him to the procedures of the adult courts. The court issued the opinion to demonstrate its reasoning to the Supreme Court of California regarding the timeliness of the lower court's order denying petitioner's rehearing.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner, a minor, was charged with assault with the intent to murder and assault with a deadly weapon. The state moved for a finding of unfitness under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 707. Petitioner was found unfit. Petitioner's counsel requested a rehearing under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 252. One of three court reporters requested an extension of time to file his portion of the fitness hearing transcript. It was extended until four days past the statutory time period in which a ruling on a petition for rehearing was to be acted upon. The statute required that if an application for rehearing was not granted, denied, or extended within 20 days following the date of its receipt, it was deemed granted. On the same day on which the transcript was delivered, the 24th day, the petition for rehearing was denied. Petitioner contended that the petition for rehearing should have been deemed granted by operation of law on the 20th day, and that the lower court's order extending the time period was without good cause. The court disagreed and denied the writ of mandate. The court found that the reporter needed four days to complete the transcript, a showing of good cause for extension.


OUTCOME: The court denied the petition for writ of mandate. The court held that good cause for the extension of the 20-day deadline on which to rule on a petition for rehearing was found in the court reporter's inability to file the transcript within the 20 days. Therefore, a four-day extension was permissible and did not require an automatic granting of petitioner's petition for rehearing in the lower court.

 

1597 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


In re Rudolfo A.


Crim. No. 37010


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Three


110 Cal. App. 3d 845; 168 Cal. Rptr. 338; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2333



October 2, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 30, 1980, and the opinion and judgment were modified to read as printed above.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No J 818815, Herman Herzbrun, Temporary Judge. *


* Pursuant to Constitution, article VI, section 21.



DISPOSITION: The judgment (order) appealed from is reversed, and the cause is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the views above expressed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, a minor, challenged the judgment of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California), which sustained a petition under Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602, alleging that he unlawfully trespassed upon the grounds of a public school in misdemeanor violation of Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code § 63.94, and placed defendant on home probation.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, a minor, was placed home on probation pursuant to Cal. Welf. & Inst. Code § 602 for having violated Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code § 63.94. The trial court found that defendant had unlawfully trespassed upon the grounds of a public school, a misdemeanor. Defendant argued that the municipal code provision was unconstitutional because it was in conflict with the general law which fully occupied the field and because it was vague, thereby violating his rights to due process of law. The court reversed the lower court's judgment. The court held that the general laws with respect to trespass upon school grounds did not preempt the field so as to preclude local regulation of trespassory conduct. However, defendant's demurrer to the petition should have been sustained. Los Angeles, Cal., Municipal Code § 63.94 imposed a criminal sanction for "trespass in or upon the grounds of any public school." It did not, however, define "trespass." There were numerous statutes regulating trespassory conduct on schoolyards that criminalized various conduct. The charge of "trespass" therefore failed to inform defendant of the particular offense with which he was charged.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the lower court's judgment, holding that defendant had failed to be informed, in the accusatory instrument, of the particular offense with which he was charged. Generically defining defendant's misdemeanor conduct as a "trespass in or upon the grounds of a public school" did not define in what particular "trespass" defendant had been engaged.

 

1598 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


San Diego County Carpenters Group Ins. v. Lorea


Civ. No. 22232.


COURT OF APPEAL OF CALIFORNIA,FOURTH APPELLATE DISTRICT, DIVISION ONE


110 Cal. App. 3d 285; 169 Cal. Rptr. 157; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2248



September 16, 1980 


NOTICE: NOT CITABLE - SUPERSEDED BY GRANT OF REHEARING


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] Rehg. granted Sept. 24, 1980 (See 112 C.A.3d 221)


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of San Diego County, No. 324889, Edward T. Butler, Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff judgment creditors challenged the decision from the Superior Court of San Diego County (California) that granted a dwelling-house exemption under Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 690.31 from a writ of execution. Plaintiffs contended that their abstract of judgment was recorded against defendant judgment debtor before the exemption statute was enacted.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff judgment creditors appealed a superior court order granting a dwelling-house exemption from a writ of execution that was based upon a recorded abstract of judgment against defendant judgment debtor. Plaintiffs contended the dwelling-house exemption provided by Cal. Civ. Proc. Code § 690.31 could not apply to defendant's home which was not exempt at the time the abstract of judgment was recorded. The exemption of § 690.31 could not be applied retroactively where the abstract of judgment was filed before the effective date of the statute. The court found that the abstract of judgment was recorded more than two years before the dwelling house exemption statute was enacted and six years before defendant recorded the declaration of homestead. Recording an abstract of judgment with the county recorder created a lien on all nonexempt property of the judgment debtor in that county. Plaintiffs' rights had vested before the statute was enacted and the court could not construe the legislative purpose to interfere with those rights. The order was reversed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that granted a dwelling-house exemption to defendant because recording an abstract of judgment with the county recorder created a lien on all nonexempt property of defendant and plaintiffs' rights had vested before the statute was enacted.

 

1599 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


People v. Gayther


Crim. No. 10507


Court of Appeal of California, Third Appellate District


110 Cal. App. 3d 79; 167 Cal. Rptr. 700; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2228



September 5, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] A petition for a rehearing was denied October 3, 1980, and appellant's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 12, 1980.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of El Dorado County, No. 33612, Charles S. Fogerty, Judge.



DISPOSITION: The judgment is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the order of the Superior Court of El Dorado County (California), which convicted defendant of rape and oral copulation. The trial court sentenced defendant to state prison for a term of three years for the rape and stayed imposition of sentence on two counts of oral copulation, the stay to become permanent upon completion of the sentence for rape.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with sexually assaulting two girls at knifepoint. The trial court convicted defendant after a jury found defendant guilty of two counts of oral copulation, and one count of rape. The trial court sentenced defendant to three years in state prison on the rape, and stayed the remaining sentences contingent upon completion of the sentence for rape. The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court. The court declined to upset the jury's finding that the victims were telling the truth, and that defendant's contention that the sex was consensual was false. No inferences needed to be drawn from the evidence, and there was nothing inherently improbable about the victims' testimony. Statutory preclusion of probation for persons convicted of rape by threat was not cruel and unusual punishment. The propensity for violence and threat to society in crimes of defendant's type were clear and were proper considerations in precluding probation. Nor was the preclusion of probation for rapists a violation of equal protection. The court held that persons convicted of different crimes were not similarly situated for equal protection purposes.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction and sentences were affirmed. There was sufficient evidence upon which a rational jury could find defendant guilty beyond a reasonable doubt, regardless of the fact that the bulk of the state's evidence came from the testimony of the victims and was contradicted by defendant. The statutory preclusion of probation for rapists constituted neither cruel and unusual punishment nor a violation of equal protection.

 

1600 of 2274 DOCUMENTS


Roth v. Dep't of Veteran Affairs


Civ. No. 57155


Court of Appeal of California, Second Appellate District, Division Five


110 Cal. App. 3d 622; 167 Cal. Rptr. 552; 1980 Cal. App. LEXIS 2372



September 4, 1980 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petitions for a rehearing were denied September 25, 1980, and respondent's petition for a hearing by the Supreme Court was denied November 12, 1980. Bird, C. J., did not participate therein.


PRIOR HISTORY: Superior Court of Los Angeles County, No. CA 000074, Harold J. Ackerman and Campbell M. Lucas, Judges.

Original Opinion Previously Reported at: 110 Cal. App. 3d 14.



DISPOSITION: Since, however, the trial court incorrectly determined that the contract in question authorized the collection of late charges, the judgment is reversed and remanded for further proceedings not inconsistent with this opinion; appellant to recover his costs on appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff, who purchased a home from defendant state department of veterans affairs, appealed the ruling of the Superior Court of Los Angeles County (California) that defendant could assess late charges pursuant to Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code § 986.9. He also argued that the late charge policy violated the Administrative Procedure Act and that the requirement that he pay the expenses of notice to other class members violated U.S. Const. amend. I.


OVERVIEW: The court reversed the trial court's finding that defendant state department of veterans affairs was authorized to assess late charges against plaintiff and other class members who purchased homes from it, finding that while Cal. Mil. & Vet. Code § 986.9 allowed for such charges, the sales contracts did not specifically provide for them, although they enumerated other charges, such as taxes and insurance, to be paid in addition to the regular monthly installment payments. Therefore, it concluded, principles of contract interpretation compelled reversal of the order. The court also rejected defendant's argument that its late charge policy did not have to be adopted in the manner provided by the Administrative Procedure Act, Cal. Gov't Code § 11370 et seq., finding that it was a rule of general application under Cal. Gov't Code § 11371(b). It rejected plaintiff's argument that requiring him to advance the expenses of notice to the class violated his right to petition under U.S. Const. amend. I, finding that plaintiff had not been denied access to the courts and could have proceeded with an individual rather than a class action if he chose not to advance the expenses.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's finding that defendant state department of veterans affairs could assess late charges against plaintiff and other buyers of defendant's homes, finding that the charges were not contractually authorized. It remanded the case, also finding that defendant's late charge policy was subject to the Administrative Procedure Act and that requiring plaintiff to advance the costs of class notice was not unconstitutional.
